The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
The Logical Syllogism is Epistemically Useless
Argues that the logical syllogism is neither necessary nor sufficient for knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's two-part argument for the uselessness of the logicians' syllogism and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
If a tool adds nothing to knowledge, it is not just useless—it is a burden.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a devastating critique of Greek logical syllogism as presented by Aristotle and his followers. He argues that the syllogism is neither necessary nor sufficient for acquiring knowledge. In this lesson, you will unpack his central claim and understand why he considers the entire enterprise of formal logic epistemically redundant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target
Ibn Taymiyya is critiquing the logical syllogism (qiyas mantiqi) as formulated by Greek logicians. Note that he quotes the Muslim theoreticians (nuzzar al-Muslimin) as his source.
2
Extract the two-part epistemic claim
He argues: (a) Any knowledge attainable through their syllogism can be attained without it; (b) Any knowledge not attainable without it cannot be attained by it. This is a double-edged critique.
3
Analyze the logical structure
This is a form of dilemma: the syllogism is either unnecessary (if the knowledge can be gotten otherwise) or insufficient (if it cannot be gotten without it, it cannot be gotten by it). The conclusion: the syllogism is 'adim al-ta’thir' (without effect).
4
Consider the implications
If true, then the entire project of formalizing demonstration in Aristotelian terms is futile for Islamic sciences. This challenges the epistemological foundation of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس في المنطق
كتاب الرد على المنطقيين
كون القياس المنطقي عديم التأثير في العلم وجوداً وعدماً
لكن الذي بينه نظار المسلمين في كلامهم على هذا المنطق اليوناني المنسوب إلى فائدة علمية أرسطو صاحب التعاليم : أن ما ذكروه من صور «القياس» ومواده، كثرة التعب العظيم، ليس فيه فائدة علمية. بل كل ما يمكن علمه بقياسهم المنطقي يمكن علمه بدون قياسهم المنطقي، وما لا يمكن علمه بدون قياسهم لا يمكن علمه بقياسهم. فلم يكن في قياسهم لا تحصيل العلم بالمجهول الذي لا يعلم بدونه، ولا حاجة به إلى ما يمكن العلم به بدونه فصار عديم التأثير في العلم وجوداً وعدماً، ولكن فيه تطويل كثير متعب . فهو مع أنه لا ينفع في العلم فيه إتعاب الأذهان، وتضييع الزمان، وكثرة الهذيان .
كونه من الأسباب المعوقة عن حصول العلم
والمطلوب من الأدلة والبراهين بيان العلم، وبيان الطرق المؤدية إلى العلم . قالوا (يعني نظار المسلمين): وهذا لا يفيد هذا المطلوب، بل قد يكون من الأسباب المعوقة حصول العلم له لما فيه من كثرة تعب الذهن كمن يريد أن يسلك الطريق ليذهب إلى مكة أو غيرها من البلاد، فإذا سلك الطريق المستقيم المعروف وصل في مدة قريبة بسعي معتدل. وإذا قيض له من يسلك به التعاسيف - والعسف في اللغة : الأخذ على غير طريق بحيث يدور به طرقاً دائرة - ويسلك به مسالك منحرفة، فإنه يتعب تعباً كثيراً حتى يصل إلى الطريق المستقيمة إن وصل وإلا فقد يصل إلى غير المطلوب، فيعتقد اعتقادات فاسدة. وقد يعجز بسبب ما يحصل له من التعب والإعياء، فلا هو نال مطلوبه، ولا هو استراح. هذا إذا بقي في الجهل البسيط . وهكذا هؤلاء.
قال أحد : ولهذا حكى من كان حاضراً عند موت إمام المنطقيين في زمانه الخونجي صاحب الموجز و كشف الأسرار وغيرهما أنه قال عند موته : أموت وما علمت شيئاً إلا علمي بأن الممكن يفتقر إلى الواجب ) ؛ ثم قال : «الافتقار وصف سلبي. أئمتهم عند موته ما علمت شيئاً»
It is not in logic. The Book of Refutation of the Logicians. That the logical syllogism is without effect in knowledge, both in existence and non-existence. But what the Muslim theoreticians have explained in their discourse on this Greek logic attributed to Aristotle, the author of the Teachings, is that what they mentioned of the forms of syllogism and its matter, with great fatigue, has no scientific benefit. Rather, everything that can be known through their logical syllogism can be known without their logical syllogism, and what cannot be known without their syllogism cannot be known through their syllogism. So their syllogism neither achieves knowledge of the unknown that cannot be known without it, nor is there need for it for what can be known without it. Thus it becomes without effect in knowledge both in existence and non-existence, but in it there is much fatiguing prolongation. So while it does not benefit knowledge, it involves fatiguing the minds, wasting time, and much raving. It is among the causes that hinder the attainment of knowledge. And what is required from proofs and demonstrations is to clarify knowledge and the ways leading to knowledge. They (meaning the Muslim theoreticians) said: This does not achieve this requirement; rather it may be among the causes that hinder the attainment of knowledge because of the great fatigue of the mind it contains, like one who wants to travel the road to go to Mecca or other cities. If he travels the straight, known road, he arrives in a short period with moderate effort. And if there is appointed for him someone who leads him through rough paths (and 'asf in language means taking without a road, so that he turns around in circular paths) and leads him through devious routes, then he becomes very fatigued until he reaches the straight road if he reaches it, otherwise he may reach something other than what is desired, and thus adopts corrupt beliefs. And he may become incapable due to the fatigue and exhaustion he experiences, so he neither attains his goal nor rests. This if he remains in simple ignorance. And such are these people. One said: For this reason, one who was present at the death of the imam of the logicians of his time, al-Khunaji, the author of al-Mujaz and Kashf al-Asrar and others, narrated that he said at his death: 'I die and I have not known anything except my knowledge that the contingent is in need of the Necessary'; then he said: 'Need is a negative attribute.' Their imams at death know nothing.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a sharp critique of the practical utility of the logicians' syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating the title: 'That the logical syllogism is without effect in knowledge, both in existence and non-existence.' He then presents the argument of Muslim theoreticians against Greek logic: the forms and matter of their syllogism involve great effort but yield no scientific benefit. The key argument is a two-part claim: (1) any knowledge attainable through their syllogism can be attained without it, and (2) any knowledge not attainable without it cannot be attained by it either. Thus the syllogism is useless for acquiring unknown knowledge and unnecessary for what is already knowable. He then compares it to a traveler: the straight path (sound reasoning) leads directly to knowledge, while the logicians' syllogism is like a guide who leads the traveler through rough, devious routes, causing fatigue, confusion, and possibly failure to reach the goal or the adoption of false beliefs. This illustrates that the logicians' method is a hindrance, not a help. Finally, he recounts the deathbed statement of al-Khunaji, a leading logician, who admitted ignorance except for the knowledge that the contingent needs the Necessary—an admission that the logicians' imams die knowing nearly nothing, underscoring the futility of their system.

Key Terms
قياس منطقي
عديم التأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, why is the logical syllogism 'without effect'?
A) Because it is too complex for anyone to learn.
B) Because it is made up of incorrect premises.
C) Because anything known through it can be known without it, and what cannot be known without it cannot be known by it.
D) Because it relies on Greek assumptions that contradict Islam.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' syllogism is epistemically redundant: it adds no new knowledge and is unnecessary for what is already knowable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's two-part argument in your own words. Then, test it against a known theological argument: 'The world is changeable; every changeable thing has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Can this knowledge be obtained without the formal syllogism? How?
?
2. Write a brief response to Ibn Taymiyya from the perspective of a logician defending the utility of syllogistic form. Does your response address the 'necessity' or 'sufficiency' point?

The Syllogism
beginner
6 min
The Logicians' Syllogism as a Hindrance: The Traveler Analogy and al-Khunaji's Confession
Uses an analogy and an anecdote to show the hindrance caused by logicians' syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Ibn Taymiyya uses the traveler analogy and al-Khunaji’s deathbed statement to argue that the logicians' syllogism hinders rather than helps the acquisition of knowledge.

Core Idea
When a method leads you in circles, it is not a path to knowledge—it is an obstacle.

Ibn Taymiyya reinforces his previous argument with a vivid analogy: the logicians' syllogism is like a deceptive guide who leads a traveler on tortuous, circuitous routes instead of the straight road. He concludes that such methods not only fail to deliver knowledge but actively obstruct it. He then presents the striking deathbed confession of a leading logician, al-Khunaji, who admits knowing almost nothing. This lesson explores these illustrations and their rhetorical power.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analogy
The straight road represents sound reasoning that leads directly to knowledge. The logicians' syllogism is like a guide who takes the traveler through rough, winding paths.
2
Identify the consequences of the bad guide
The traveler either reaches the road after much fatigue, or ends up lost with false beliefs, or gives up completely. Ibn Taymiyya says this is what happens to students of formal logic.
3
Analyze the anecdote of al-Khunaji
Even the foremost logician, on his deathbed, admits he has learned nothing except that 'the contingent needs the Necessary.' This is a profound admission that the vast system of logic produced no real knowledge.
4
Evaluate the rhetorical strategy
Ibn Taymiyya uses concrete, vivid examples to make his abstract critique concrete. Why is this effective?

Source Text

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Critique of Greek Syllogistic Form
Author critiques Greek syllogistic form using prophetic hadith as counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the two-premise requirement in Greek syllogisms and identify his alternative based on prophetic and Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the Greek two-premise requirement, not all reasoning.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's *Naqd al-Mantiq* challenges the central claim of Aristotelian logic that every valid proof must consist of exactly two premises arranged in a specific form. He uses a prophetic hadith as a springboard to expose the artificiality of this rigid structure, arguing that the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the Quran itself employ proofs with one, two, or many premises as needed. The lesson helps you understand why Ibn Taymiyya saw the Greek form as unnecessary and sometimes misleading.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Recognize that the Greek syllogistic form (nazm yunāni) requires any valid reasoning to have exactly two premises: a major and a minor, leading to a conclusion. This is the standard categorical syllogism.
Ibn Taymiyya targets this rigidity.
2
Find the counterexample from hadith
Ibn Taymiyya mentions a hadith that appears in the two-premise form: 'Every intoxicant is wine, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' He questions its authenticity and argues that even if authentic, the Prophet would not speak in such a contrived manner.
The hadith is not the focus; it is a rhetorical device.
3
Analyze the Prophet's purpose
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes two scenarios: if the Prophet merely intended to state a ruling, no proof is needed; if he intended to demonstrate evidence, he would do so as God does in the Qur'an, with one or more premises.
The Prophet's eloquence supersedes Greek constraints.
4
Extract the alternative principle
Conclude that valid evidence (dalīl) can consist of one premise, two premises, or more, depending on the audience and objective. The Qur'an itself uses varying numbers of premises to guide people generally.
This principle frees Islamic reasoning from artificial forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسكر خمر، وكل مسكر حرام. ليس على النظم اليوناني، وإن كان روي في الدليل النبوي بعض طرق الحديث فليس بثابت. فإن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أجل قدرا في علمه وبيانه من أن يتكلم بمثل هذيانهم. فإنه إن قصد مجرد تعريف الحكم لم يحتج مع قوله إلى دليل. وإن قصد بيان الدليل، كما بين الله في القرآن عامة المطالب الإلهية التي تقرر الإيمان بالله ورسله واليوم الآخر، فهو صلى الله عليه وسلم أعلم الخلق بالحق وأحسنهم بيانا له. فعلم أنه ليس جميع المطالب يحتاج إلى مقدمتين ولا يكفي في جميعها مقدمتان بل يذكر ما يحصل به البيان والدلالة سواء كان مقدمة، أو مقدمتين، أو أكثر. وما قصد به هدى عاما كالقرآن الذي أنزله الله بيانا للناس يذكر فيه من الأدلة ما ينتفع به الناس عامة.
Every intoxicant is wine, and every intoxicant is forbidden. It is not upon the Greek organization, and if some versions of the hadith were narrated in the prophetic evidence, they are not established. For the Prophet, peace be upon him, is too great in his knowledge and his explanation to speak like their ravings. For if he intended merely to define the ruling, he would not need, after his saying, to provide evidence. And if he intended to demonstrate the evidence, as God demonstrated in the Quran the general divine matters that establish faith in God, His messengers, and the Last Day, then he, peace be upon him, is the most knowledgeable of creation about truth and the best of them in explaining it. So it is known that not all objectives require two premises, nor are two premises sufficient for all of them, but rather he mentions what achieves the explanation and indication, whether it be one premise, two premises, or more. And what is intended for general guidance, like the Quran which God sent down as an explanation for people, mentioning in it evidences that people generally benefit from.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Greek syllogistic form, specifically the claim that all valid reasoning must have exactly two premises (like the categorical syllogism). Ibn Taymiyya begins by referencing a hadith that says 'Every intoxicant is wine, and every intoxicant is forbidden' in the Greek pattern (major and minor premise). He argues that this form is not authentically from the Prophet, and even if it were, the Prophet is too elevated to speak in such a confused manner. He then explains that the Prophet's speech either aims to define a ruling, in which case no proof is needed, or to demonstrate evidence, as God does in the Quran. In the Quran, proofs vary in number: sometimes one premise suffices, sometimes more. Thus, the Greek requirement of exactly two premises is false. He concludes that general guidance, like the Quran, uses proofs of varying lengths to benefit all people.

Key Terms
نظم يوناني
دليل
مقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Taymiyya argue about the number of premises needed for a valid proof?
A) Every proof requires exactly two premises
B) Proofs can have one, two, or more premises depending on the objective
C) Only one premise is permissible
D) Premises are irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya frees Islamic reasoning from the artificial constraint of exactly two premises, emphasizing flexibility based on audience and objective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a Quranic verse that uses more than two premises to argue for a theological point (e.g., the resurrection in Surah Yasin: 36:78-79). List the premises and explain how they form a proof.
?
Write a short paragraph critiquing the statement 'All valid reasoning must have exactly two premises' using Ibn Taymiyya's arguments about prophetic and Quranic evidence.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
Syllogism Only Yields Universal Knowledge
Author argues that the Greek syllogism only produces universal knowledge, not particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological objection to the Greek syllogism and evaluate its implications for the necessity of syllogism in acquiring knowledge.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya: syllogism gives only universals, which are often known more directly without it.

This passage is a direct critique of the foundational role of the syllogism in Aristotelian logic. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the syllogism only produces universal knowledge, not knowledge of particular existents. Moreover, even these universal propositions can be known without the syllogism, often more easily. This challenges the claim that syllogistic reasoning is indispensable for knowledge acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
What do logicians assert about the syllogism? They claim it yields necessary knowledge and is a primary tool for certainty.
The logicians view syllogism as essential for deriving unknown from known.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He argues that syllogism only gives knowledge of universals, not of any specific thing (جزئي). Also, these universals can be known without syllogism.
The phrase 'لا يفيد إلا العلم بأمور كلية' is key.
3
Analyze the epistemological claim about particulars
He asserts that knowledge of particular things (المعينات) may be more evident (أبين) than knowledge of universals.
This inverts the priority of universal over particular.
4
Evaluate the consequence for syllogistic necessity
If universals are knowable without syllogism, and particulars are even clearer, then the syllogism is not necessary for knowledge.
Why does this matter for Islamic theology and law?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا فما يذكرونه من «القياس لا يفيد إلا العلم بأمور كلية، لا يفيد العلم بشيء معين من الموجودات، ثم تلك الأمور الكلية يمكن العلم بكل واحد منها بما أيسر من قياسهم، فلا تعلم كلية بقياسهم إلا والعلم بجزئياتها ممكن بدون قياسهم الشمولي، وربما كان أيسر فإن العلم بالمعينات قد يكون أبين من العلم بالكليات. وهذا مبسوط في موضعه.
And also what they mention that 'the syllogism does not yield knowledge except of universal matters; it does not yield knowledge of any specific thing among existents.' Then those universal matters can be known each one by what is easier than their syllogism. So you do not know a universal by their syllogism except that knowledge of its particulars is possible without their comprehensive syllogism, and perhaps it is easier. For knowledge of specific things may be more evident than knowledge of universals. This is elaborated in its place.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage critiques the claim that syllogism (qiyas) is necessary for knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the Greek syllogism only gives universal knowledge (e.g., all humans are mortal), not knowledge of particular existents (e.g., Zayd). Moreover, these universals themselves can often be known more easily without a syllogism. For example, one knows 'all humans are mortal' through observation of particulars, and the syllogism adds nothing. In fact, knowledge of particular things (like a specific man) may be clearer than the universal. Thus, the syllogism is not indispensable for knowledge; it is often a roundabout method.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): Greek syllogism; here critiqued as producing only universal knowledge [Note: In standard logic, qiyas is the primary tool; Ibn Taymiyya limits its epistemic value.]
كلي (kulli): Universal; a general proposition covering all individuals [Note: No significant shift - standard term.]
جزئي (juz'i): Particular; specific individual or instance [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is impossible
B) It is only possible through syllogism
C) It may be more evident than knowledge of universals
D) It is identical to universal knowledge

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique undermines the necessity of the Greek syllogism for knowledge, asserting that universals and particulars are knowable by simpler means.

Then evaluate whether a syllogism like 'Nabidh is an intoxicant; all intoxicants are forbidden; therefore nabidh is forbidden' adds new knowledge or merely articulates what was already known from the universal premise.

The Syllogism's Form Does Not Guarantee Truth
Author argues that Greek syllogism only provides form, not truth of premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the formal structure of a syllogism and the material truth of its premises, and explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the claim that syllogistic form validates proof.

Core Idea
Syllogistic form only organises premises; it does not make them true.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at a core claim of the logicians: that their syllogistic method can certify correct reasoning. He argues that what they call 'syllogism' only provides the outer shape of an argument, not the truth of its foundations. Understanding this distinction is crucial before you accept any logical proof as sound, especially in Islamic theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states the claim that syllogism distinguishes correct evidence from corrupt. He calls this a claim of the ignorant.
The claim is in the third sentence: 'their syllogism distinguishes correct evidence from corrupt.'
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's central objection
Notice his assertion: the syllogism contains only the form and figure (sura) of the evidence, not its truth. The implication (luzum) between premises and conclusion is not addressed by the syllogistic form.
3
Analyse the grounds of the objection
He separates form (sura) from material (madda). The syllogism does not prove any premise true or false; that requires independent verification. When logicians do discuss material truth, they make many errors.
This is an epistemological critique: form alone cannot guarantee knowledge.
4
Connect to the larger argument
Recognize that Ibn Taymiyya is redefining the criterion for a sound proof: not formal validity but the actual implication (luzum) based on true premises. This sets up his alternative framework.
The next lesson expands on luzum as the real criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود هنا أن المطلوب هو العلم والطريق إليه هو الدليل. فمن عرف دليل مطلوبه عرف مطلوبه، سواء نظمه بقياسهم أم لا. ومن لم يعرف دليله لم ينفعه قياسهم. ولا يقال: إن قياسهم يعرف صحيح الأدلة من فاسدها فإن هذا إنما يقوله جاهل لا يعرف حقيقة قياسهم. فإن قياسهم ليس فيه إلا شكل الدليل وصورته. وأما كون الدليل المعين مستلزما لمدلوله فهذا ليس في قياسهم ما يتعرض له بنفي ولا إثبات وإنما هذا علمه بالمقدمات التي اشتمل عليها الدليل، وليس في قياسهم بيان صحة شيء من المقدمات ولا فسادها وإنما يتكلمون في هذا إذا تكلموا في مواد القياس، وهو من الكلام في المقدمات من جهة ما يصدق بها وكلامهم في هذا فيه خطأ كثير، كما نبه عليه في موضع آخر.
And the intended point here is that the objective is knowledge and the path to it is the evidence. So whoever knows the evidence of his objective knows his objective, whether he organizes it by their syllogism or not. And whoever does not know his evidence, their syllogism does not benefit him. And it is not said: Their syllogism distinguishes correct evidence from corrupt, for this is only said by an ignorant person who does not know the reality of their syllogism. For their syllogism contains only the form and figure of the evidence. As for whether a particular evidence implies its conclusion, this is not something their syllogism addresses by negation or affirmation; rather this is known by the premises that the evidence contains. And their syllogism does not demonstrate the correctness or corruption of any premise; they only speak about this when they speak about the materials of the syllogism, which is speech about premises in terms of what makes them true, and their speech in this contains much error, as noted elsewhere.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between the form and the material truth of a syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya states that the Greek syllogism only provides the formal structure (shape and figure), not the actual truth of the premises or the implication. Knowing whether an implication holds depends on the content of the premises, not the syllogistic form. The logicians claim that their syllogism can distinguish valid from invalid proofs, but Ibn Taymiyya calls this ignorance: the syllogism itself does not evaluate the truth of premises. When they do discuss material truth (in their study of 'matters' or 'material logic'), they make many errors. Therefore, the syllogism is epistemologically hollow without independent verification of premises.

Key Terms
صورة الدليل
مادة القياس
لزوم

B) Only the form of the evidence, not its truth
C) Both form and material truth
D) A way to discover new knowledge

Key Takeaway
A syllogism's formal validity does not guarantee the truth of its conclusion; the material truth of premises is a separate and more fundamental requirement.

The Three Types of Circularity (الدور)
Author enumerates and defines the three types of circularity in logical and legal contexts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between prior circularity (impossible) and simultaneous circularity (possible) as defined by Ibn Taymiyya, and identify legal circularity in fiqh reasoning.

Core Idea
Not all circularity is fallacious; only prior temporally-ranked circularity is impossible.

Ibn Taymiyya intervenes in a key logical debate: is circular reasoning always invalid? He shows that the answer depends on the kind of mutual dependence. In this passage, he clarifies that only 'prior circularity' (dawr qabli), where each of two things is temporally prior to the other, is impossible. 'Simultaneous circularity' (dawr ma'i), where two things coexist without temporal priority, is not only possible but common in theology and correlatives. He also introduces a third type, legal circularity (al-dawr al-hukmi al-fiqhi), specific to jurisprudential reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of circularity
Locate in the Arabic the three types: cosmic circularity (which splits into two subtypes) and legal circularity. Note that the first type is further divided into prior and simultaneous.
Ibn Taymiyya says 'al-dawr thalathat anwa'' and then mentions the first type (al-dawr al-kawni) before subdividing.
2
Distinguish prior vs simultaneous circularity
Prior circularity involves temporal priority: A is before B and B is before A. This is deemed impossible. Simultaneous circularity does not involve such priority; A and B exist together.
The example: God's attributes and essence — each necessitates the other without temporal precedence.
3
Examine the examples for simultaneous circularity
Ibn Taymiyya gives two examples: (1) the attributes of the Lord and His essence, (2) fatherhood and sonship. Notice that these are correlatives that logically imply each other.
In each case, the terms are mutually dependent but not temporally prior.
4
Understand legal circularity
The third type is specific to fiqh reasoning. It involves a circularity in legal rulings, such as a condition that relies on the conditioned. The passage only introduces it without examples here.
This type is not standard in Aristotelian logic; Ibn Taymiyya adds it from Islamic jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما ذكرنا فيه أن لفظ «الدور» يقال على ثلاثة أنواع . «الدور» ثلاثة أنواع الدور الكوني» الذي يُذكر في الأدلة العقلية أنه لا يكون هذا حتى يكون هذا، الأول: ولا يكون هذا حتى يكون هذا. وطائفة من النظار كالرازي يقولون هو ممتنع . والصواب أنه نوعان كما يقوله الآمدي وغيره دور قَبْلي، ودور مَعِي. فـ «الدور القبلي ممتنع ، و الدور المعي» ممكن . فـ «الدور القبلي الذي يُذكر في العلل، وفي الفاعل والمؤثر، ونحو ذلك . مثل الدور القبلي أن يقال : «لا يجوز أن يكون كل من الشيئين فاعلاً للآخر، لأنه يفضي إلى الدور». وهو أن يكون هذا قبل ذاك، وذاك قبل هذا وذاك فاعل لهذا، وهذا فاعل لذاك . فيكون الشيء فاعلاً لفاعله، ويكون قبل قبله. وقد أورد الرازي وغيره عليه إشكالات ذكرناها وبينا وجه حلها في طرق إثبات الصانع . وأما الدور المَعِيّ فهو كدور الشرط مع المشروط، وأحد المتضائفين مع الآخر. إذا قيل : صفات الرب لا تكون إلا مع ذاته، ولا تكون ذاته إلا صفاته»، فهذا صحيح. وكذلك إذا قيل : «لا تكون الأبوة إلا مع البنوّة، ولا تكون البنوة إلا مع الأبوة». والنوع الثاني : مما يسمى دوراً الدور الحُكْمي الفقهي» المذكور في المسئلة
We have already explained in the book composed on circularity that the term 'circularity' is applied to three types. Circularity has three types: the cosmic circularity which is mentioned in rational proofs that this does not exist until that exists, first: that this does not exist until that exists. And a group of theologians such as al-Razi say it is impossible. The correct view is that it is two types as al-Amidi and others say: prior circularity and simultaneous circularity. So prior circularity is impossible, and simultaneous circularity is possible. As for prior circularity, it is mentioned in causes, and in the agent and effect, and the like. For example, prior circularity: it is said: 'It is not permissible for each of two things to be the agent of the other, because it leads to circularity.' That is, this is before that, and that is before this, and that is agent of this, and this is agent of that. So the thing becomes the agent of its own agent, and it is before what is before it. Al-Razi and others have raised objections to it, which we have mentioned and shown the solution in the ways of proving the Maker. As for simultaneous circularity, it is like the circularity of condition with the conditioned, and one of the correlatives with the other. If it is said: 'The attributes of the Lord do not exist except with His essence, and His essence does not exist except with His attributes,' this is correct. Likewise if it is said: 'Fatherhood does not exist except with sonship, and sonship does not exist except with fatherhood.' And the second type: what is called circularity is the legal-fiqh circularity mentioned in the issue
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of circularity (dawr) and classifies its types. Ibn Taymiyya states that the term 'dawr' applies to three types, then immediately focuses on the first type, 'cosmic circularity' (al-dawr al-kawni) used in rational proofs. He notes that some theologians like al-Razi consider all cosmic circularity impossible, but Ibn Taymiyya corrects this by dividing it into two subtypes: prior circularity (dawr qabli) and simultaneous circularity (dawr ma'i). Prior circularity, where each of two things is the cause or agent of the other in a temporal sequence, is impossible because it leads to a thing being the agent of its own agent, which is contradictory. Simultaneous circularity, where two things are mutually dependent without temporal priority (e.g., conditions and conditioned, correlatives like fatherhood and sonship), is possible and exemplified by the coexistence of God's essence and attributes. Ibn Taymiyya then introduces a second type, 'legal-fiqh circularity' (al-dawr al-hukmi al-fiqhi), used in jurisprudential reasoning. The passage thus clarifies that circularity is not universally invalid; its impossibility depends on the kind of dependence involved.

Key Terms
دور
دور قبلي
دور معي
دور حكمي فقهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Simultaneous circularity (dawr ma'i)
C) Both prior and simultaneous
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Prior circularity (dawr qabli) is impossible because it entails a thing being prior to its own prior, while simultaneous circularity (dawr ma'i) is possible and common in theology and correlatives.

Earthly Cause of Dawn and Twilight Variation: Ibn Taymiyya's Critique
Ibn Taymiyya explains the variation in dawn and twilight lengths due to atmospheric vapors, not celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's earthly cause for dawn and twilight variation and identify the logical fallacy in the astronomers' celestial explanation.

Core Idea
Dawn and twilight depend on vapors, not celestial motion.

Ibn Taymiyya rejects the astronomers' claim that the variation in dawn and twilight lengths is due to the motion of the celestial sphere. Instead, he provides an empirical explanation: the reflection of sunlight on atmospheric vapors. This lesson dissects his argument and identifies the logical error in the astronomers' reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing explanations
Read the passage and locate what Ibn Taymiyya attributes the variation to (vapors) versus what the astronomers attribute it to (celestial motion).
The passage explicitly contrasts 'due to vapor' (بسبب البخار) with 'not due to celestial' (لا بسبب فلكي).
2
Trace the empirical reasoning
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's seasonal account: In winter, vapors are abundant, so dawn appears earlier and twilight lasts shorter. In summer, vapors are scarce, so dawn appears later and twilight lasts longer. Note how he links observation to cause.
He uses the properties of vapors (reflection, dissolution by sun) to explain the differences.
3
Locate the astronomers' fallacy
Ibn Taymiyya says the astronomers made an error by assuming that because dawn is part of night and twilight part of day, their lengths follow the lengths of night and day. He points out they failed to distinguish between the sun itself and its rays. Identify this as a false analogy (qiyas fāsid).
The astronomers' reasoning: 'dawn portion is part of night, so it should be shorter when night is shorter' – but this ignores the role of vapors.
4
Assess the epistemological point
Why does Ibn Taymiyya insist on an earthly cause? Because prayer times (fajr, ‘isha) are determined by actual observation of dawn and twilight. If the cause were purely celestial, one could calculate them universally; but earthly conditions vary, so empirical verification is necessary.
This links the logical critique to an important legal principle: rulings based on sensible phenomena require direct observation, not speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففي الشتاء تكون الأبخرة في الليل كثيرة لكثرة ما يتصعد من الأرض بسبب رطوبتها، ولا يُحلَّل البخار فيها، فينعكس الشعاع عليه، فيظهر الفجر حينئذ قبل ما يظهر لو لم يكن بخار. وأما الصيف، فإن الشمس بالنهار تُحلّل البخار، فإذا غربت الشمس لم يكن للشعاع التابع لها بخارٌ يردُّه فتطول في الصيف حصة العشاء بهذا السبب، وتطول في الشتاء حصةُ الفجر بهذا السبب. وفي الصيف تقصر حصة الفجر لتأخر ظهور الشعاع إذ لا بخار يردُّه، لأن الرطوبات في الصيف قليلة؛ وتقصر حصة العشاء في الشتاء لكثرة الأبخرة في الشتاء. فحاصله أن كلا من الحصتين تتبع ما قبلها في الطول والقصر بسبب البخار، لا بسبب فلكي. والذين ظنوا أن ذلك يكون عن حركة الفلك قدروه بذلك فغلطوا في تقديرهم، سببه أرضي لا فلكي وصاروا يقولون: حصّةُ الفجر في الشتاء أقصر منها في الصيف، وحصة العشاء في الصيف أقصر منها في الشتاء، فإن هذه جزء من الليل وهذه جزء من النهار، فتتبعه في قدره. ولم يعرفوا الفرق بين طلوع الشمس وغروبها وبين طلوع شُعاعها، فإن الشمس تتحرّك في الفلك، فحركتها تابعة للفلك، والشعاع هو بحسب ما يحمله وينعكس عليه من الهواء والأبخرة، وهذا أمر له سبب أرضي ليس مثل حركة الفلك.
In winter, vapors are abundant at night due to much moisture rising from the earth, and the vapor is not dissolved, so the rays reflect upon it, and dawn appears earlier than it would if there were no vapor. As for summer, the sun dissolves vapor during the day, so when the sun sets, the following rays have no vapor to reflect them, so the twilight portion lengthens in summer for this reason, and the dawn portion lengthens in winter for this reason. In summer, the dawn portion shortens due to the delay of the appearance of the rays since there is no vapor to reflect them, because moisture is scarce in summer; and the twilight portion shortens in winter due to the abundance of vapors in winter. The summary is that each of the two portions follows its preceding [portion] in length and shortness due to vapor, not due to celestial [motion]. Those who thought that this is due to the motion of the celestial sphere estimated it accordingly and erred in their estimation; its cause is earthly, not celestial. They began saying: 'The dawn portion in winter is shorter than in summer, and the twilight portion in summer is shorter than in winter' because this is part of night and that is part of day, so it follows it in duration. They did not distinguish between the rising and setting of the sun and the rising of its rays. The sun moves in the celestial sphere, its motion following the sphere, but the rays depend on what carries them and what they reflect upon from air and vapors, and this is a matter with an earthly cause, unlike the motion of the celestial sphere.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage refutes the astronomers' claim that the lengths of dawn (fajr) and twilight (‘isha’) are determined by celestial motion. Ibn Taymiyya provides an empirical, earthly explanation: the presence or absence of atmospheric vapors affects the reflection of sunlight. In winter, more vapors cause dawn to appear earlier and twilight to appear shorter; in summer, fewer vapors cause dawn to appear later and twilight longer. He emphasizes that the sun's motion is celestial, but the rays' behavior depends on terrestrial conditions. The astronomers' error stems from not distinguishing between the sun itself and its rays, and from applying a faulty analogy (qiyas fāsid) that contradicts sensory experience. The commentary should note that this is a critique of natural philosophy's overreach into religiously relevant phenomena. Islamic examples: the determination of prayer times (fajr and ‘isha) relies on actual observation, not calculation.

Key Terms
البخار
حصة الفجر/العشاء
حركة الفلك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the sun rises faster
B) Because vapors are scarce, delaying the appearance of reflected light
C) Because the celestial sphere moves faster
D) Because the nights are shorter

Key Takeaway
Dawn and twilight times are not solely celestial; they depend on atmospheric vapors, so prayer times must be based on actual observation, not pure calculation.

Use Ibn Taymiyya's distinction between the sun and its rays to explain how the actual 'illah (vapor reflection) is different.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Syllogism's Form vs. Its Epistemic Value
Ibn Taymiyya states his thesis and concedes formal validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Ibn Taymiyya's acceptance of syllogistic validity from his rejection of its claim to produce new knowledge of existents.

Core Idea
Syllogism is valid in form but sterile for discovering new existents.

Ibn Taymiyya opens by stating his central thesis: the syllogism, though formally valid, does not provide knowledge of existent things. He is careful to concede the formal validity of categorical and conditional syllogisms, but argues that the certainty they produce is not specific to the syllogistic form—it could have been obtained otherwise. This sets the stage for his broader critique of the epistemological pretensions of Aristotelian logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the thesis
Find the sentence that states the main claim: 'القياس مع صحته لا يستفاد به علم بالموجودات'. Notice the structure: he concedes validity (مع صحته) but denies epistemic utility.
2
Identify the concession
In the next lines, he explicitly says the form is not disputed and that if premises are known, the syllogism yields certainty. Why does he concede this?
3
Understand the key distinction
He argues that what is obtained through syllogism may be obtained without it, and vice versa. This shows that syllogism is not the exclusive source of knowledge about existents.
4
Examine the examples
He lists categorical (iqtirani) and conditional (istithna'i) syllogisms, showing he accepts their internal logic. Note that he calls categorical 'قياس التداخل' (overlap) and conditional 'التلازم' (entailment) and 'التقسيم' (division).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع ـ «القياس» لا يستفاد به علم بالموجودات
فصل
«القياس» مع صحته لا يستفاد به علم بالموجودات
فصورة القياس لا تدفع صحتها . لكن يبيّن أنه لا يستفاد به علم بالموجودات، كما أن اشتراطهم للمقدمتين دون الزيادة والنقص شرط باطل فهو وإن حصل به يقين فلا يستفاد بخصوصه يقين مطلوب بشيء من الموجودات بل ما يحصل به قد يحصل بدونه، وقد يحصل بدونه ما لا يحصل به .
فنقول : إن صورة «القياس» إذا كانت مواده معلومة فلا ريب أنه يفيد اليقين . وإذا قيل : كل أ ب، وكل ب ج ، وكانت المقدمتان معلومتين فلا ريب أن هذا التأليف يفيد العلم بأن كل أ ج . وهذا لا نزاع فيه .
فصورة «القياس» لا يتكلّم على صحتها. فإن ذلك ظاهر، سواء في ذلك الاقتراني المؤلف من الحمليات الذي هو قياس التداخل، و «الاستثنائي» المؤلف من الشرطيات المتصلة والمنفصلة الذي هو التلازم و «التقسيم».
وكذلك ما ذكروه من أن الشكل الأول من الاقتراني يفيد المطالب الأربعة - النتائج الأربعة الموجبة والسالبة والجزئية والكلية وأن «الثاني» يفيد السالبة الكلية والجزئية ؛ وأن «الثالث» يفيد الجزئية - سالبة كانت أو موجبة .
وفي «التلازم» استثناء عين المقدَّم ينتج عين التالي واستثناء نقيض التالي ينتج نقيض المقدم . وهو قول نظار المسلمين : وجود الملزوم يقتضي وجود اللازم، وانتفاء اللازم يقتضي انتفاء الملزوم. بل هذا مع اختصاره فإنه يشمل جميع أنواع الأدلة، سواء سمّيت براهين أو «أقيسة» أو غير ذلك. فإن كل ما يستدل به على غيره فإنه مستلزم له؛ فيلزم من تحقق الملزوم، الذي هو الدليل، تحقق اللازم، الذي هو المطلوب، المدلول عليه ؛ ويلزم من انتفاء اللازم الذي هو المدلول عليه، انتفاء الملزوم، الذي هو الدليل .
The fourth station: 'Qiyas' does not provide knowledge of existents. Chapter: 'Qiyas' with its validity does not provide knowledge of existents. The form of qiyas is not disputed regarding its validity. But it is made clear that it does not provide knowledge of existents, just as their condition of two premises without increase or decrease is a false condition. For even if certainty is obtained through it, it does not specifically provide the desired certainty about any existent thing. Rather, what is obtained through it may be obtained without it, and what is obtained without it may not be obtained through it. So we say: The form of 'qiyas' when its materials are known, there is no doubt that it yields certainty. And if it is said: Every A is B, and every B is C, and the two premises are known, then there is no doubt that this composition yields knowledge that every A is C. This is not disputed. So the form of 'qiyas' is not discussed regarding its validity. For that is obvious, whether in the categorical syllogism composed of assertoric propositions, which is the 'qiyas of overlap', and the 'conditional syllogism' composed of conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals, which is 'entailment' and 'division'. And likewise what they mentioned that the first figure of the categorical syllogism yields the four demands – the four results: affirmative, negative, particular, and universal – and that the second yields the universal and particular negative, and that the third yields the particular, whether negative or affirmative. And in 'entalment', affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, and denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent. And this is the statement of the Muslim theologians: Existence of the necessitor necessitates existence of the necessary, and non-existence of the necessary necessitates non-existence of the necessitor. Rather, this, despite its brevity, encompasses all types of proofs, whether they are called 'demonstrations' or 'qiyas' or otherwise. For everything used as evidence for something else entails it; so from the realization of the necessitor, which is the evidence, follows the realization of the necessary, which is the sought conclusion; and from the non-existence of the necessary, which is the conclusion, follows the non-existence of the necessitor, which is the evidence.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' claim that syllogism (qiyas) provides new knowledge of existent things. He opens by stating that while the form of the syllogism is valid (its validity is not disputed), it does not provide knowledge of existents. He clarifies that even if certainty is attained via syllogism, it does not specifically yield the desired certainty about any particular existent; whatever is gained via syllogism could have been obtained without it, and sometimes that which is obtained without syllogism cannot be obtained through it. Then he concedes that if the premises are known, the syllogistic form (e.g., all A is B, all B is C, therefore all A is C) undoubtedly yields certainty—this is not contested. However, the point is that the syllogism does not produce new knowledge of external reality; it merely rearranges what is already known. He lists the three figures of the categorical syllogism and their conclusion types, and the conditional syllogism (istithna'i) composed of conjunctive or disjunctive conditionals, which he calls 'talazum' (entailment) and 'taqsim' (division). For the conditional, he notes that affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, and denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent. He then identifies this with the principle of Muslim theologians: existence of the malzum (necessitor/evidence) necessitates existence of the lazim (necessary/conclusion), and non-existence of the lazim necessitates non-existence of the malzum. He concludes that this principle, though concise, covers all types of proofs—whether called burhan (demonstration) or qiyas—because any proof entails its conclusion. Thus, the passage is a critique: the syllogism's formal validity is accepted, but its epistemic value in producing new knowledge of existents is denied.

الملزوم
اللازم

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, what is the relationship between syllogistic form and knowledge of existents?
A) The syllogistic form is invalid and cannot yield any knowledge.
B) The syllogistic form is valid but does not provide new knowledge of existents.
C) The syllogistic form provides certain knowledge of all existents.
D) The syllogistic form is only valid for conditional arguments.

Key Takeaway
Formal validity does not guarantee that a syllogism produces new knowledge of the external world; the premises already contain the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a common syllogism from Islamic jurisprudence (e.g., 'Every permitted transaction is free from usury; this transaction is free from usury; therefore it is permitted.') and explain, in Ibn Taymiyya's terms, why this syllogism does not provide new knowledge about the permissibility of the transaction – the conclusion was already contained in the premises.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Entailment (Talazum) as the Universal Proof Structure
Ibn Taymiyya subsumes syllogisms under the universal principle of entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the principle of talazum (entailment) in Ibn Taymiyya's argument and explain how it subsumes all types of proof.

Core Idea
All proofs, whether called syllogism or demonstration, are reducible to the entailment principle.

Ibn Taymiyya, after conceding the formal validity of categorical and conditional syllogisms, reinterprets them under a single, more fundamental principle: talazum (entailment). He shows that the conditional syllogism's affirming antecedent (modus ponens) and denying consequent (modus tollens) are identical to the theologians' maxim: existence of the necessitor implies existence of the necessary, and absence of the necessary implies absence of the necessitor. This principle, he argues, covers all proofs, stripping away the logicians' elaborate classifications.

Guided Path
1
Examine the figures of categorical syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya lists the three figures and their conclusions. Note that he accepts these technical details but does not elevate them.
2
Focus on the conditional syllogism (istithna'i)
He breaks it into affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent. These are the two valid forms.
3
Connect to the Muslim theologians' principle
He equates these forms to the statement: وجود الملزوم يقتضي وجود اللازم وانتفاء اللازم يقتضي انتفاء الملزوم. This is the core of all entailment.
4
See the universal claim
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that this principle encompasses all proofs, whether called برهان or قياس. This is his reductionist move.

The Syllogism
advanced
5 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Syllogistic Knowledge: Form vs. Real Existents
Ibn Taymiyya argues that syllogisms do not produce knowledge of existing things despite formal validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's central argument that even a valid syllogism does not produce knowledge of external existents, and distinguish his epistemological critique from a rejection of formal validity.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism yields certainty, but not certainty about existing things.

Ibn Taymiyya begins this section with a bold claim: even if a syllogism is formally valid, it does not give us knowledge of the external world. This does not mean he rejects logic entirely—he acknowledges its formal soundness. Rather, he redirects attention to what syllogisms actually achieve: formal entailment, not discovery of new realities. This lesson unpacks his nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's thesis
Locate the sentence where he states that a valid syllogism does not yield knowledge of existing things. This is the opening of the passage: 'القياس مع صحته لا يستفاد به علم بالموجودات'.
Thesis is clear and explicit.
2
Understand his concession
He admits that if premises are known, the composition yields certainty. Find where he says: 'فصورة القياس إذا كانت مواده معلومة فلا ريب أنه يفيد اليقين'.
He does not deny logical validity.
3
Analyze the entailment principle (talazum)
Ibn Taymiyya elevates the conditional relationship (malzum-lazim) as the universal logical core. Trace how he presents it: 'وجود الملزوم يقتضي وجود اللازم، وانتفاء اللازم يقتضي انتفاء الملزوم'.
This principle encompasses all proofs.
4
Distinguish between formal knowledge and knowledge of existents
Note his claim: what is obtained by syllogism can be obtained without it, and sometimes what is obtained without it cannot be obtained by it. This distinguishes formal logical consequence from knowledge of actual existents.
The syllogism adds nothing new about the external world.

المقام الرابع - «القياس» لا يستفاد به علم بالموجودات

فصل

«القياس» مع صحته لا يستفاد به علم بالموجودات

فصورة القياس لا تدفع صحتها. لكن يبيّن أنه لا يستفاد به علم بالموجودات، كما أن اشتراطهم للمقدمتين دون الزيادة والنقص شرط باطل فهو وإن حصل به يقين فلا يستفاد بخصوصه يقين مطلوب بشيء من الموجودات بل ما يحصل به قد يحصل بدونه، وقد يحصل بدونه ما لا يحصل به.

فنقول : إن صورة «القياس» إذا كانت مواده معلومة فلا ريب أنه يفيد اليقين. وإذا قيل : كل ا ب، وكل ب ج ، وكانت المقدمتان معلومتين فلا ريب أن هذا التأليف يفيد العلم بأن كل ا ج . وهذا لا نزاع فيه.

فصورة «القياس» لا يتكلّم على صحتها. فإن ذلك ظاهر، سواء في ذلك الاقتراني المؤلف من الحمليات الذي هو قياس التداخل»، و «الاستثنائي» المؤلف من الشرطيات المتصلة والمنفصلة الذي هو التلازم» و «التقسيم».

وكذلك ما ذكروه من أن الشكل الأول من الاقتراني» يفيد المطالب الأربعة - النتائج الأربع الموجبة والسالبة والجزئية والكلية وأن «الثاني» يفيد السالبة الكلية والجزئية ؛ وأن «الثالث» يفيد الجزئية - سالبة كانت أو موجبة.

وفي «التلازم» استثناء عين المقدم ينتج عين التالي واستثناء نقيض التالي ينتج نقيض المقدم. وهو قول نظار المسلمين : وجود الملزوم يقتضي وجود اللازم، وانتفاء اللازم يقتضي انتفاء الملزوم». بل هذا مع اختصاره فإنه يشمل جميع أنواع الأدلة، سواء سمّيت براهين» أو «أقيسة» أو غير ذلك. فإن كل ما يستدل به على غيره فإنه مستلزم له؛ فيلزم من تحقق الملزوم، الذي هو الدليل، تحقق اللازم، الذي هو المطلوب، المدلولُ عليه ؛ ويلزم من انتفاء اللازم الذي هو المدلول عليه، انتفاء الملزوم، الذي هو الدليل.

ولهذا كان من عرف أن المدعى باطل علم أنه لا يقوم عليه دليل صحيح، فإنه يمتنع أن يقوم على الباطل دليل صحيح ومن عرف أن الدليل صحيح علم أن لازمه الذي هو المطلوب حق، فإنه يجب إذا كان الدليل حقاً أن يكون المطلوب المدلول عليه حقاً، وإن تنوعت صور الأدلة ومقدماتها وترتيبها.

ولهذا كان الدليل الذي يصوّر بصورة القياس الاقتراني» يصور أيضاً بصورة «الاستثنائي»، ويصور بصور أخرى غير ما ذكروه من الألفاظ وترتيبها. والمقصود هنا الكلام على ما ذكروه كما ذكرنا الكلام على الشرطي المتصل.
The Fourth Station: The syllogism does not produce knowledge of existing things. Section: The syllogism, even if valid, does not produce knowledge of existing things. The form of the syllogism is not contested in its validity. But it is shown that no knowledge of existing things is obtained through it, just as their condition of having exactly two premises (neither more nor less) is a false condition. For even if certainty results from it, no particular certainty of any desired thing among existents is obtained through it; rather what is obtained by it can be obtained without it, and what is obtained without it cannot be obtained by it. We say: The form of the syllogism, if its materials are known, there is no doubt it yields certainty. If it is said: Every A is B, and every B is C, and the two premises are known, there is no doubt that this composition yields the knowledge that every A is C. There is no dispute about this. So the form of the syllogism is not discussed regarding its validity, for that is obvious, whether it is the conjunctive composed of categoricals (which is the syllogism of inclusion), or the exceptional composed of conditionals and disjunctives (which is entailment and division). Likewise, what they mentioned: that the first figure of the conjunctive yields the four demands—the four results: affirmative, negative, particular, universal; that the second yields the universal and particular negative; that the third yields the particular, whether negative or affirmative. And in entailment, the exception of the exact antecedent produces the exact consequent, and the exception of the contradictory of the consequent produces the contradictory of the antecedent. This is the statement of Muslim theologians: The existence of the necessary condition entails the existence of the necessary consequence, and the non-existence of the necessary consequence entails the non-existence of the necessary condition. Indeed, this, despite its brevity, encompasses all types of proofs, whether called demonstrations or syllogisms or otherwise. For every thing used to infer another entails it; from the realization of the necessary condition (which is the proof) follows the realization of the necessary consequence (which is the sought-after, the inferred). And from the non-existence of the necessary consequence (which is the inferred) follows the non-existence of the necessary condition (which is the proof). For this reason, whoever knows that the claim is false knows that no sound proof can support it, for it is impossible for a sound proof to support falsity. And whoever knows that the proof is sound knows that its necessary consequence—which is the sought—is true, for if the proof is true, the inferred sought must be true, even if the forms of proofs, their premises, and their arrangement vary. For this reason, a proof that is formulated in the form of a conjunctive syllogism can also be formulated in the form of an exceptional syllogism, and can be formulated in other forms besides theirs, in wording and arrangement. Our purpose here is to discuss what they mentioned, just as we discussed the conditional connective.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is a critical introduction to Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the logical syllogism (qiyas) does not provide knowledge of existing things (al-mawjudat). He begins by stating the thesis: even if a syllogism is formally valid, it does not yield knowledge of external realities. He acknowledges that the form itself is not problematic—if the premises are known, the conclusion follows necessarily. He gives the standard example: 'Every A is B, every B is C, therefore every A is C,' and admits there is no dispute about this. However, his objection is that such knowledge is not particular knowledge of existents; what is obtained by syllogism can be obtained without it, and sometimes what is obtained without it cannot be obtained by it. He then reviews the types of syllogisms: conjunctive (iqtirani) composed of categoricals, and exceptional (istithna'i) composed of conditionals and disjuncts, which he calls 'talazum' (entailment) and 'taqsim' (division). He also mentions the three figures of the conjunctive and their conclusions. He then discusses 'talazum' in detail: it is the principle that the existence of the necessary condition (malzum) entails the existence of the necessary consequence (lazim), and the non-existence of the latter entails the non-existence of the former. This principle, he says, is universal and encompasses all proofs, whether called burhan or qiyas. From this he draws an epistemological consequence: one who knows a claim is false knows no sound proof can support it, and one who knows a proof is sound knows its consequent (the conclusion) is true. He concludes by noting that any proof can be reformulated in multiple syllogistic forms, and his aim is to discuss their account.

Key Terms
القياس
الملزوم
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the relationship between a valid syllogism and knowledge of existing things?

The Six Forms of Demonstration in Ilm al-Mantiq
Logicians' taxonomy of demonstration into six forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six syllogistic forms that logicians claim constitute demonstration and explain Ibn Taymiyya's objection that this taxonomy is arbitrary.

Core Idea
Logicians limit demonstration to six artificial forms; Ibn Taymiyya argues all reasoning reduces to simple implication.

In this passage from the *Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin*, Ibn Taymiyya presents the logicians' own classification of demonstrative arguments (burhan) into six specific forms. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for grasping his critique: that the classification is unnatural and that true demonstration is not so restricted. We will walk through the logicians' system and then apply Ibn Taymiyya's critical method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main branches of demonstration
The logicians divide demonstration (burhan) into conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i). Conjunctive syllogisms have two categorical premises; exceptive syllogisms involve a conditional premise and an exception (affirming or negating a part).
Keep in mind that Ibn Taymiyya considers this division artificial.
2
Examine the four figures of conjunctive syllogisms
The logicians say the conjunctive has four figures based on the position of the middle term (hadd al-awsat). Note the conditions for each figure: first figure (middle predicate in minor, subject in major) produces all conclusions; second figure (middle subject in both) produces negatives; third figure (middle predicate in both) produces affirmatives; fourth figure (middle predicate in major, subject in minor) is the reverse of the first.
The fourth figure is often omitted as unnatural (ba'id 'an al-tab').
3
Survey the two types of exceptive syllogisms
Exceptive syllogisms are either conditional connective (shartiyya muttasila, if-then) or conditional disjunctive (shartiyya munfasila, either-or). Together they give two more forms, totaling six.
The exceptive is not further divided into figures.
4
Recognize the implicit critique
Ibn Taymiyya's presentation shows the logicians' taxonomy is based on arbitrary criteria (position of the middle term) and omits the fourth figure. He will argue that all valid reasoning reduces to simple implication (luzum), not these formal molds.
This critique is foundational to his rejection of Greek logical formalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهم يقولون : «البرهان» ينحصر في الاقتراني» و «الاستثنائي). وإن «الاقتراني» ينحصر في أربعة أشكال و الاستثنائي» ينحصر في «الشرطي المتصل» و «الشرطي المنفصل. فيعود إلى ستة أشكال وجمهورهم لا يذكرون الشكل الرابع من الاقترانيات» البُعْده عن الطَّبع. وحَصَروها في أربعة بناء على حصرهم الدليل في مقدمتين تشتركان في حد أوسط . ثم الأوسط إن كان محمولاً في الصغرى، موضوعاً في الكبرى، فهو الشكل الأول المنتج للمطالب الأربعة وإن كان موضوعاً فيهما فهو الثاني المنتج للسَّلبيات . وإن كان محمولاً فيهما فهو «الثالث» المنتج للإيجابيات . وإن كان محمولاً في الكبرى (۱) ، موضوعاً في الصغرى، عکس «الأول»، فهو «الرابع»،
For they say: 'Demonstration' is limited to the 'conjunctive' and the 'exceptive'. And the 'conjunctive' is limited to four figures, and the 'exceptive' is limited to the 'conditional connective' and the 'conditional disjunctive'. So it reduces to six forms. And most of them do not mention the fourth figure of the conjunctives due to its unnaturalness. And they limited them to four based on their limitation of the proof to two premises that share a middle term. Then, if the middle is a predicate in the minor premise and a subject in the major, it is the first figure, productive of all four conclusions; if it is subject in both, it is the second, productive of negative [conclusions]; if it is predicate in both, it is the third, productive of affirmative [conclusions]; and if it is predicate in the major and subject in the minor, opposite of the first, it is the fourth.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage presents the logicians' taxonomy of demonstration (burhan) and then critiques it implicitly by showing its arbitrary limitations. According to the logicians, all demonstrative arguments are either conjunctive (iqtirani) syllogisms, which consist of two categorical premises with a middle term, or exceptive (istithna'i) syllogisms, which involve a conditional premise. The conjunctive has four figures determined by the position of the middle term: first figure (middle is predicate in minor, subject in major) produces all four conclusion types (affirmative/negative, universal/particular); second figure (middle subject in both) yields negatives; third figure (middle predicate in both) yields affirmatives; and fourth figure (middle predicate in major, subject in minor) is the reverse of the first. The exceptive is of two types: conditional connective (mutasil, if-then) and conditional disjunctive (munfasil, either-or). Thus there are six forms total. The author notes that most logicians omit the fourth figure due to its unnaturalness. The passage sets up Ibn Taymiyya's broader critique: these forms are artificial; all arguments reduce to the simple notion of implication (luzum).

Key Terms
البرهان
الاقتراني
الاستثنائي
الشكل

D) Fourth figure

Key Takeaway
The logicians' six-fold taxonomy of demonstration is a self-imposed limitation that Ibn Taymiyya challenges as unnatural; true reasoning rests on implication, not rigid forms.

Every act of wrongdoing is forbidden by Allah. Therefore, every act of disobedience is forbidden by Allah.' (Answer: Third figure, middle term 'act of wrongdoing' is predicate in both premises.)

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Six-Form Limitation
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' limitation of arguments to six forms and asserts proof is mere implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's three-pronged critique (formal, conceptual, and practical) of the logicians' claim that all demonstrative arguments are limited to six forms.

Core Idea
The logicians' six forms are neither exhaustive nor necessary; true proof is simply implication.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at the standard Aristotelian classification of arguments into six forms: four categorical figures plus two conditionals. He argues that this limitation is arbitrary and fails to capture the true nature of evidence. By dissecting his critique, you will learn to recognize when a formal system oversimplifies reality and how to refute such claims using both logical and practical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' six forms
Find where Ibn Taymiyya lists the six forms: four categorical figures, connected conditional, and disjunctive conditional. Note the alternative five-form version that omits the fourth figure.
The fourth figure is omitted by most because it is 'far from nature'.
2
Analyze the formal criticism
Ibn Taymiyya argues that evidence can be a single premise or more than two, and can take countless forms. Does the logicians' system allow for this? Find the sentence where he says 'evidence may be a single premise'.
3
Examine the conceptual criticism
He asks whether we consider the form or the meaning. If meaning, then all evidence reduces to implication (luzum). Trace his argument that even disjunctive conditionals ultimately depend on implication.
He shows that 'either A or B' implies that if not A then B, etc.
4
Assess the practical criticism
Ibn Taymiyya claims their method lengthens the path and increases errors. Find the part where he says those who adopt their method are more prone to mistakes.
He says no true scholar adheres to their conventions.

المقام الرابع ـ «التقسيم» وأقسامه
«محدث»، فقيل : لا بد في الوجود من واجب» أو «ممكن»، أو «قديم» أو «محدث»، فهذان لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان .
وكذلك إذا قيل : الوجود إما قائم بنفسه كـ «الجسم»، وإما قائم بغيره كـ «العَرَض»، فهذان لا يرتفعان وقد يجتمعان . فكل ما لا يخلو الوجود عنها مع إمكان اجتماعهما، أو لا يخلو بعضُ الأنواع عنهما مع إمكان اجتماعهما، فهو من هذا الباب . كما إذا قيل : الوارث بطريق الأصالة إما أن يرث بفرض وإما أن يرث بتعصيب، وقد يجتمع في الشخص الواحد أن يكون ذا فرض وَعَصَبة، كالزوج إذا كان ابن عم، أو معتقاً. فالشرطي المتصل هو من «التلازم»، والمنفصل هو في الثاني كما في الضدين والنقيضين. وهذه الصورة - صورة التقسيم الذي هو الشرطي المنفصل - هي أيضاً تعود إلى اللزوم، فإنه يلزم من وجود أحد الضدين عدمُ الآخر، وهذه مانعة الجمع. والخلو ويلزم من عدم أحدهما وجود الآخر، وهذه مانعة الخلو ويلزم من وجود أحدهما عدم الآخر، ومن عدمه وجوده. وهذه مانعة الجمع والخلق .
فهذه الأشكال وإن تكثرت فجميعها تعود إلى أن الدليل يستلزم المدلول. ويمكن تصوير ذلك بصور متعددة مما ذكروه ومما لم يذكروه .
فهم يقولون : «البرهان» ينحصر في الاقتراني» و «الاستثنائي). وإن «الاقتراني» ينحصر في أربعة أشكال و الاستثنائي» ينحصر في «الشرطي المتصل» و «الشرطي المنفصل. فيعود إلى ستة أشكال وجمهورهم لا يذكرون الشكل الرابع من الاقترانيات» البُعْده عن الطَّبع. وحَصَروها في أربعة بناء على حصرهم الدليل في مقدمتين تشتركان في حد أوسط . ثم الأوسط إن كان محمولاً في الصغرى، موضوعاً في الكبرى، فهو الشكل الأول المنتج للمطالب الأربعة وإن كان موضوعاً فيهما فهو الثاني المنتج للسَّلبيات . وإن كان محمولاً فيهما فهو «الثالث» المنتج للإيجابيات . وإن كان محمولاً في الكبرى، موضوعاً في الصغرى، عکس «الأول»، فهو «الرابع»، أبعدها عن الطبع. وأكثرهم لا يذكرونه، فيجعلون الأشكال خمسة : هذه الثلاثة مع وهو «الاستثنائي - الشرطي المتصل، والشرطي المنفصل .
فهذه الأشكال إذا قُدّر إفادتها فهي صور من صور الأدلة، لا ينحصر تصوير الأدلة في هذه الأشكال، كما لا ينحصر تصوير الدليل في مقدمين . بل هذا الحصر خطأ في النفي والإثبات. فقد يكون الدليل مقدمةً، وقد يكون مقدمات. وهذه الأشكال يكثرُ تصوير الدليل على أشكال أُخَر غير هذه، فلا تنحصر في خمسة أو ستة. وقد يؤتى بالدليل بدون هذه الأشكال جميعها، وبدون المقدمتين إذا كان مقدمة واحدة .
وإذا قالوا : إن جميع ما يُذكر من الأدلة يرجع إلى هذه الأشكال، قيل لهم : بل الأدلة ترجع إلى أن الدليل مستلزم للمدلول . وحينئذ فإما أن يكون الاعتبار بما ذكروه من صور الأشكال ولفظها، أو بما ذكروه من المعنى. فإن كانت العبرة بالصورة لم يكن تخصيص صورة الدليل بخمسة أو ستة صواباً، كما لم يكن تخصيصه بمقدمتين صواباً، إذ كان يمكن تصويره بصور كثيرة متنوعة ليس فيها لفظ شرط، لا متصل ولا منفصل، ولا هو على صورة القياس الحَمْلى كما ذكروه. وإن كانت العبرة بالمعنى كان ذلك أدل على فساد ما ذكروه. فإن المعنى هو أن يكون ما يُستدلّ به مستلزماً لما يستدل به عليه سواء كان مقدمة أو مقدمتان أو أكثر، وسواء كان على الشكل والترتيب الذي ذكروه أو غيره .
وليس فيه إلا والصواب في هذا الباب أن يقال : ما ذكروه إذا كان صواباً فإنه تطويل للطريق، وتبعيد للمطلوب، وعكس للمقصود فإنهم زعموا أنهم جعلوه آلة قانونية تمنع الذهن أن يَزِلَّ في فكره. وما ذكروه إذا كَلَّفوا الناظر المستدلَّ أن يلزمه في تصوراته وتصديقاته كان أقرب إلى زلله في فكره وضلاله عن مطلوبه، كما هو الواقع. فلا تجد أحداً التزم وَضْعَ هؤلاء واصطلاحهم إلا كان أكثر خطأ وأقل صواباً ممن لم يلتزم وضعهم وسلك إلى المطلوب بفطرة الله التي فَطَر عباده عليها. ولهذا لا يوجد أحد من حقق علماً من العلوم كان ملتزماً لوضعهم.
ولهذا يقال : كثرة هذه الأشكال وشروط نتاجها تطويل قليل الفائدة كثير التعب .
The fourth station — 'Division' and its types. 'Originated', so it was said: There must be in existence either 'Necessary' or 'Possible', or 'Eternal' or 'Originated'; these two neither combine nor separate. Similarly, if it is said: Existence is either self-subsistent like 'the body', or subsistent in another like 'the accident'; these two do not separate but may combine. So everything from which existence is not free while their combination is possible, or some types are not free from them while their combination is possible, is of this category. As when it is said: The inheritor by original right either inherits by a fixed share or by residual claim, and it may combine in one person to have both a fixed share and residual claim, like the husband if he is a paternal cousin or a manumitter. The connected conditional is from 'implication', and the disjunctive is in the second [type] as in opposites and contradictions. This form—the form of division which is the disjunctive conditional—also returns to implication, for from the existence of one opposite follows the non-existence of the other, and this is [the type] preventing combination. And [from] absence [follows] the existence of the other, and this is [the type] preventing emptiness. And from the existence of one follows the non-existence of the other, and from its non-existence follows the existence of the other. And this is [the type] preventing combination and emptiness. So these forms, however numerous, all return to the fact that the evidence implies the conclusion. And it is possible to depict that in multiple forms, both those they mentioned and those they did not. They say: 'Demonstration is limited to the categorical and the conditional. The categorical is limited to four figures, and the conditional is limited to the connected conditional and the disjunctive conditional. Thus it returns to six forms. And the majority of them do not mention the fourth figure of categoricals due to its distance from nature. They limited it to four based on their limitation of evidence to two premises that share a middle term. Then if the middle is a predicate in the minor premise and a subject in the major, it is the first figure, productive of all four [syllogistic] demands. If it is a subject in both, it is the second, productive of negatives. If it is a predicate in both, it is the third, productive of affirmatives. If it is a predicate in the major and a subject in the minor, opposite of the first, it is the fourth, furthest from nature. Most of them do not mention it, so they make the forms five: these three plus the conditional—the connected conditional and the disjunctive conditional. So these forms, if their utility is assumed, are forms of evidence; the depiction of evidence is not limited to these forms, just as the depiction of evidence is not limited to two premises. Rather, this limitation is an error in negation and affirmation. For evidence may be a single premise, and it may be multiple premises. And these forms—the depiction of evidence often takes other forms besides these, so it is not limited to five or six. And sometimes evidence is brought without all these forms, and without the two premises, if it is a single premise. And if they say: All that is mentioned of evidence returns to these forms, it is said to them: Rather evidence returns to the fact that the evidence implies the conclusion. Then either consideration is given to what they mentioned of the forms and their wording, or to what they mentioned of the meaning. If the consideration is of the form, then specifying the form of evidence to five or six is not correct, just as specifying it to two premises is not correct, since it could be depicted in many diverse forms that do not contain the word 'if', neither connected nor disjunctive, nor are in the form of categorical syllogism as they mentioned. If the consideration is of the meaning, then that is more indicative of the corruption of what they mentioned. For the meaning is that what is used as evidence must imply what is evidenced, whether it is one premise, two premises, or more, and whether it is in the form and order they mentioned or other. There is no... and the correct in this matter is to say: What they mentioned, if it is correct, then it is lengthening the path, distancing [from] the desired, and opposite of the goal, for they claimed that they made it a legal instrument that prevents the mind from slipping in its thought. And what they mentioned, if they burden the investigator and prover to adhere to it in his concepts and assents, it is closer to his slipping in thought and deviation from his desired, as is the reality. So you do not find anyone who adhered to the position and convention of these [people] except that he was more erroneous and less correct than one who did not adhere to their position and followed the desired by the innate disposition of God with which He created His servants. Hence, no one who has mastered any of the sciences is one who adhered to their position. Therefore it is said: The abundance of these forms and the conditions of their productivity is a lengthy [process] of little benefit, much fatigue.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is Ibn Taymiyya's critical exposition and refutation of the logicians' claim that all demonstrative arguments are limited to six forms. He begins by explaining the classification of disjunctive conditional syllogisms as understood by the logicians: some disjunctives prevent both combination and separation (like opposites/contradictions), some prevent combination only, some prevent separation only. He notes that all these forms ultimately depend on the relation of implication (luzum) between evidence and conclusion. Then he reports the logicians' claim: demonstration (burhan) is confined to categorical (iqtirani) and conditional (istithna'i) types; categorical has four figures, conditional has two (connected and disjunctive), making six forms. Most omit the fourth figure due to its unnaturalness, so they count five. Ibn Taymiyya immediately attacks this limitation as false both in negation and affirmation. He asserts that evidence can be a single premise, or more than two, and can take forms other than those six. If the logicians respond that all evidence reduces to these forms, he counters by asking whether they consider the form (laft) or the meaning (ma'na). If form, then evidence can be expressed in countless other shapes without the typical syllogistic structure. If meaning, then the core is simply that the evidence necessitates the conclusion, regardless of number of premises or arrangement. This shows the logicians' system is either incomplete or redundant. Finally, he launches a sharp practical critique: their method lengthens the path, burdens the mind, and ironically makes error more likely. Those who adopt it become more prone to mistakes than those who follow natural human reasoning (fitra). He concludes that no true scholar of any science adheres to their conventions, and the web of forms and conditions is tedious and unhelpful. Throughout, Ibn Taymiyya uses examples from theological and legal discourse, such as the division of existence into necessary/contingent or substance/accident, and inheritance shares.

Key Terms
برهان
الشرطي المتصل
الشرطي المنفصل
الدليل
اللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, what is the fundamental nature of all proof (dalil)?
A) It must be a categorical syllogism in one of four figures.
B) It must be a conditional syllogism, either connected or disjunctive.
C) It reduces to implication (luzum) between evidence and conclusion.
D) It must consist of exactly two premises.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that arguments are limited to six forms by showing that true proof is simply the relation of implication between evidence and conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using Ibn Taymiyya's own method, critique the claim that all valid legal reasoning (ijtihad) must follow a specific structure of premises. Provide an example from fiqh where a single indicator (qarina) suffices as evidence.

Identify a passage from a kalam text (e.g., al-Sanusiyya) and test whether the arguments given could be reduced to implication rather than fitting the six forms Ibn Taymiyya criticizes.

intermediate
