The Concept of Luzum as the Core of All Proof
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' limitation of arguments to six forms and asserts proof is mere implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's concept of luzum (implication) and apply it to recognize valid evidence in Islamic theological and legal arguments, independent of the logicians' formal structures.

Core Idea
Implication (luzum) is the single unifying principle behind all valid proof.

Ibn Taymiyya repeatedly stresses that all forms of evidence, including categorical and conditional syllogisms, ultimately depend on the relation of implication. In this lesson, we will unpack what luzum means and how it functions as the foundation of proof according to Ibn Taymiyya. You will learn to shift your focus from memorizing syllogistic figures to recognizing the core logical relation that makes any argument work.

Guided Path
1
Define luzum in this context
Find where Ibn Taymiyya says 'all these forms return to the fact that the evidence implies the conclusion'. Luzum means that from the truth of the evidence, the truth of the conclusion necessarily follows.
It is the relation of necessary consequence.
2
See how disjunctives reduce to luzum
He explains that a disjunctive conditional like 'either A or B' entails that if not A then B, and if A then not B. This is an implication. Trace his examples: opposites prevent combination, etc.
Even the logicians' own conditionals depend on luzum.
3
Understand the flexibility luzum allows
Ibn Taymiyya argues that evidence can be a single premise, many premises, or any form, as long as the implication holds. Contrast this with the rigid six-form system.
A single statement can imply a conclusion; no need for two premises.
4
Apply luzum to recognize valid proof
Consider a real example: in fiqh, the statement 'This is khamr' immediately implies it is forbidden, based on the prophetic hadith. That is a single-premise implication.
The evidence is the classification; the conclusion is the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع ـ «التقسيم» وأقسامه
«محدث»، فقيل : لا بد في الوجود من واجب» أو «ممكن»، أو «قديم» أو «محدث»، فهذان لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان .
وكذلك إذا قيل : الوجود إما قائم بنفسه كـ «الجسم»، وإما قائم بغيره كـ «العَرَض»، فهذان لا يرتفعان وقد يجتمعان . فكل ما لا يخلو الوجود عنها مع إمكان اجتماعهما، أو لا يخلو بعضُ الأنواع عنهما مع إمكان اجتماعهما، فهو من هذا الباب . كما إذا قيل : الوارث بطريق الأصالة إما أن يرث بفرض وإما أن يرث بتعصيب، وقد يجتمع في الشخص الواحد أن يكون ذا فرض وَعَصَبة، كالزوج إذا كان ابن عم، أو معتقاً. فالشرطي المتصل هو من «التلازم»، والمنفصل هو في الثاني كما في الضدين والنقيضين. وهذه الصورة - صورة التقسيم الذي هو الشرطي المنفصل - هي أيضاً تعود إلى اللزوم، فإنه يلزم من وجود أحد الضدين عدمُ الآخر، وهذه مانعة الجمع. والخلو ويلزم من عدم أحدهما وجود الآخر، وهذه مانعة الخلو ويلزم من وجود أحدهما عدم الآخر، ومن عدمه وجوده. وهذه مانعة الجمع والخلق .
فهذه الأشكال وإن تكثرت فجميعها تعود إلى أن الدليل يستلزم المدلول. ويمكن تصوير ذلك بصور متعددة مما ذكروه ومما لم يذكروه .
فهم يقولون : «البرهان» ينحصر في الاقتراني» و «الاستثنائي). وإن «الاقتراني» ينحصر في أربعة أشكال و الاستثنائي» ينحصر في «الشرطي المتصل» و «الشرطي المنفصل. فيعود إلى ستة أشكال وجمهورهم لا يذكرون الشكل الرابع من الاقترانيات» البُعْده عن الطَّبع. وحَصَروها في أربعة بناء على حصرهم الدليل في مقدمتين تشتركان في حد أوسط . ثم الأوسط إن كان محمولاً في الصغرى، موضوعاً في الكبرى، فهو الشكل الأول المنتج للمطالب الأربعة وإن كان موضوعاً فيهما فهو الثاني المنتج للسَّلبيات . وإن كان محمولاً فيهما فهو «الثالث» المنتج للإيجابيات . وإن كان محمولاً في الكبرى، موضوعاً في الصغرى، عکس «الأول»، فهو «الرابع»، أبعدها عن الطبع. وأكثرهم لا يذكرونه، فيجعلون الأشكال خمسة : هذه الثلاثة مع وهو «الاستثنائي - الشرطي المتصل، والشرطي المنفصل .
فهذه الأشكال إذا قُدّر إفادتها فهي صور من صور الأدلة، لا ينحصر تصوير الأدلة في هذه الأشكال، كما لا ينحصر تصوير الدليل في مقدمين . بل هذا الحصر خطأ في النفي والإثبات. فقد يكون الدليل مقدمةً، وقد يكون مقدمات. وهذه الأشكال يكثرُ تصوير الدليل على أشكال أُخَر غير هذه، فلا تنحصر في خمسة أو ستة. وقد يؤتى بالدليل بدون هذه الأشكال جميعها، وبدون المقدمتين إذا كان مقدمة واحدة .
وإذا قالوا : إن جميع ما يُذكر من الأدلة يرجع إلى هذه الأشكال، قيل لهم : بل الأدلة ترجع إلى أن الدليل مستلزم للمدلول . وحينئذ فإما أن يكون الاعتبار بما ذكروه من صور الأشكال ولفظها، أو بما ذكروه من المعنى. فإن كانت العبرة بالصورة لم يكن تخصيص صورة الدليل بخمسة أو ستة صواباً، كما لم يكن تخصيصه بمقدمتين صواباً، إذ كان يمكن تصويره بصور كثيرة متنوعة ليس فيها لفظ شرط، لا متصل ولا منفصل، ولا هو على صورة القياس الحَمْلى كما ذكروه. وإن كانت العبرة بالمعنى كان ذلك أدل على فساد ما ذكروه. فإن المعنى هو أن يكون ما يُستدلّ به مستلزماً لما يستدل به عليه سواء كان مقدمة أو مقدمتان أو أكثر، وسواء كان على الشكل والترتيب الذي ذكروه أو غيره .
وليس فيه إلا والصواب في هذا الباب أن يقال : ما ذكروه إذا كان صواباً فإنه تطويل للطريق، وتبعيد للمطلوب، وعكس للمقصود فإنهم زعموا أنهم جعلوه آلة قانونية تمنع الذهن أن يَزِلَّ في فكره. وما ذكروه إذا كَلَّفوا الناظر المستدلَّ أن يلزمه في تصوراته وتصديقاته كان أقرب إلى زلله في فكره وضلاله عن مطلوبه، كما هو الواقع. فلا تجد أحداً التزم وَضْعَ هؤلاء واصطلاحهم إلا كان أكثر خطأ وأقل صواباً ممن لم يلتزم وضعهم وسلك إلى المطلوب بفطرة الله التي فَطَر عباده عليها. ولهذا لا يوجد أحد من حقق علماً من العلوم كان ملتزماً لوضعهم.
ولهذا يقال : كثرة هذه الأشكال وشروط نتاجها تطويل قليل الفائدة كثير التعب .
The fourth station — 'Division' and its types. 'Originated', so it was said: There must be in existence either 'Necessary' or 'Possible', or 'Eternal' or 'Originated'; these two neither combine nor separate. Similarly, if it is said: Existence is either self-subsistent like 'the body', or subsistent in another like 'the accident'; these two do not separate but may combine. So everything from which existence is not free while their combination is possible, or some types are not free from them while their combination is possible, is of this category. As when it is said: The inheritor by original right either inherits by a fixed share or by residual claim, and it may combine in one person to have both a fixed share and residual claim, like the husband if he is a paternal cousin or a manumitter. The connected conditional is from 'implication', and the disjunctive is in the second [type] as in opposites and contradictions. This form—the form of division which is the disjunctive conditional—also returns to implication, for from the existence of one opposite follows the non-existence of the other, and this is [the type] preventing combination. And [from] absence [follows] the existence of the other, and this is [the type] preventing emptiness. And from the existence of one follows the non-existence of the other, and from its non-existence follows the existence of the other. And this is [the type] preventing combination and emptiness. So these forms, however numerous, all return to the fact that the evidence implies the conclusion. And it is possible to depict that in multiple forms, both those they mentioned and those they did not. They say: 'Demonstration is limited to the categorical and the conditional. The categorical is limited to four figures, and the conditional is limited to the connected conditional and the disjunctive conditional. Thus it returns to six forms. And the majority of them do not mention the fourth figure of categoricals due to its distance from nature. They limited it to four based on their limitation of evidence to two premises that share a middle term. Then if the middle is a predicate in the minor premise and a subject in the major, it is the first figure, productive of all four [syllogistic] demands. If it is a subject in both, it is the second, productive of negatives. If it is a predicate in both, it is the third, productive of affirmatives. If it is a predicate in the major and a subject in the minor, opposite of the first, it is the fourth, furthest from nature. Most of them do not mention it, so they make the forms five: these three plus the conditional—the connected conditional and the disjunctive conditional. So these forms, if their utility is assumed, are forms of evidence; the depiction of evidence is not limited to these forms, just as the depiction of evidence is not limited to two premises. Rather, this limitation is an error in negation and affirmation. For evidence may be a single premise, and it may be multiple premises. And these forms—the depiction of evidence often takes other forms besides these, so it is not limited to five or six. And sometimes evidence is brought without all these forms, and without the two premises, if it is a single premise. And if they say: All that is mentioned of evidence returns to these forms, it is said to them: Rather evidence returns to the fact that the evidence implies the conclusion. Then either consideration is given to what they mentioned of the forms and their wording, or to what they mentioned of the meaning. If the consideration is of the form, then specifying the form of evidence to five or six is not correct, just as specifying it to two premises is not correct, since it could be depicted in many diverse forms that do not contain the word 'if', neither connected nor disjunctive, nor are in the form of categorical syllogism as they mentioned. If the consideration is of the meaning, then that is more indicative of the corruption of what they mentioned. For the meaning is that what is used as evidence must imply what is evidenced, whether it is one premise, two premises, or more, and whether it is in the form and order they mentioned or other. There is no... and the correct in this matter is to say: What they mentioned, if it is correct, then it is lengthening the path, distancing [from] the desired, and opposite of the goal, for they claimed that they made it a legal instrument that prevents the mind from slipping in its thought. And what they mentioned, if they burden the investigator and prover to adhere to it in his concepts and assents, it is closer to his slipping in thought and deviation from his desired, as is the reality. So you do not find anyone who adhered to the position and convention of these [people] except that he was more erroneous and less correct than one who did not adhere to their position and followed the desired by the innate disposition of God with which He created His servants. Hence, no one who has mastered any of the sciences is one who adhered to their position. Therefore it is said: The abundance of these forms and the conditions of their productivity is a lengthy [process] of little benefit, much fatigue.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is Ibn Taymiyya's critical exposition and refutation of the logicians' claim that all demonstrative arguments are limited to six forms. He begins by explaining the classification of disjunctive conditional syllogisms as understood by the logicians: some disjunctives prevent both combination and separation (like opposites/contradictions), some prevent combination only, some prevent separation only. He notes that all these forms ultimately depend on the relation of implication (luzum) between evidence and conclusion. Then he reports the logicians' claim: demonstration (burhan) is confined to categorical (iqtirani) and conditional (istithna'i) types; categorical has four figures, conditional has two (connected and disjunctive), making six forms. Most omit the fourth figure due to its unnaturalness, so they count five. Ibn Taymiyya immediately attacks this limitation as false both in negation and affirmation. He asserts that evidence can be a single premise, or more than two, and can take forms other than those six. If the logicians respond that all evidence reduces to these forms, he counters by asking whether they consider the form (laft) or the meaning (ma'na). If form, then evidence can be expressed in countless other shapes without the typical syllogistic structure. If meaning, then the core is simply that the evidence necessitates the conclusion, regardless of number of premises or arrangement. This shows the logicians' system is either incomplete or redundant. Finally, he launches a sharp practical critique: their method lengthens the path, burdens the mind, and ironically makes error more likely. Those who adopt it become more prone to mistakes than those who follow natural human reasoning (fitra). He concludes that no true scholar of any science adheres to their conventions, and the web of forms and conditions is tedious and unhelpful. Throughout, Ibn Taymiyya uses examples from theological and legal discourse, such as the division of existence into necessary/contingent or substance/accident, and inheritance shares.

Key Terms

الدليل

Key Takeaway
Luzum (implication) is the common thread running through all valid arguments, making the logicians' rigid six-form classification unnecessary and misleading.

The Non-Restriction of Argument Forms
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' limitation of arguments to fixed forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that logical form is not essential to proof, and identify the role of entailment (istilzam) in his alternative.

Core Idea
Proof is entailment, not form.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that valid arguments are limited to a handful of fixed forms. He distinguishes between the form and the meaning of an argument, insisting that the essence of proof is that the evidence necessarily implies the conclusion. In this lesson, we will follow his deconstruction of the logicians' restriction and his alternative criterion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
The logicians assert that all valid arguments are limited to five or six specific forms: three figures of categorical syllogism plus conditional conjunctive and disjunctive forms. This is the claim Ibn Taymiyya refutes.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's first objection: diversity of actual proofs
He points out that proofs can consist of one premise or many, and can take forms outside the listed ones. Therefore, the list is incomplete.
He emphasizes that the limitation of proofs to two premises is also incorrect.
3
Examine his second objection: the criterion of form vs. meaning
If the criterion for a proof is its form, then any number of forms are possible. If the criterion is the meaning, then the essence is that the evidence necessitates the conclusion (istilzam).
This is a dilemma: either way, the logicians' restriction fails.
4
Reconstruct his alternative principle
The proof necessitates the proven, regardless of the number of premises or their arrangement. This is the true definition of proof.
Ibn Taymiyya shifts the focus from formalism to epistemic entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تنحصر الأشكال في سنة. الدليل مستلزم للمدلول. أبعدها عن الطبع. وأكثرهم لا يذكرونه، فيجعلون الأشكال خمسة : هذه الثلاثة مع وهو الاستثنائي الشرطي المتصل والشرطي المنفصل. فهذه الأشكال إذا قدر إفادتها فهي صور من صور الأدلة، لا ينحصر تصوير الأدلة في هذه الأشكال، كما لا ينحصر تصوير الدليل في مقدمين. بل هذا الحصر خطأ في النفي والإثبات. فقد يكون الدليل مقدمةً، وقد يكون مقدمات. وهذه الأشكال يكثر تصوير الدليل على أشكال أخر غير هذه، فلا تنحصر في خمسة أو ستة. وقد يؤتى بالدليل بدون هذه الأشكال جميعها، وبدون المقدمتين إذا كان مقدمة واحدة. وإذا قالوا : إن جميع ما يذكر من الأدلة يرجع إلى هذه الأشكال، قيل لهم : بل الأدلة ترجع إلى أن الدليل مستلزم للمدلول. وحينئذ فإما أن يكون الاعتبار بما ذكروه من صور الأشكال ولفظها، أو بما ذكروه من المعنى. فإن كانت العبرة بالصورة لم يكن تخصيص صورة الدليل بخمسة أو ستة صواباً، كما لم يكن تخصيصه بمقدمتين صواباً، إذ كان يمكن تصويره بصور كثيرة متنوعة ليس فيها لفظ شرط، لا متصل ولا منفصل، ولا هو على صورة القياس الحملى كما ذكروه. وإن كانت العبرة بالمعنى كان ذلك أدل على فساد ما ذكروه. فإن المعنى هو أن يكون ما يستدل به مستلزماً لما يستدل به عليه سواء كان مقدمة أو مقدمتان أو أكثر، وسواء كان على الشكل والترتيب الذي ذكروه أو غيره.
The forms are not limited to six. The proof necessitates the proven. It is furthest from nature. Most of them do not mention it, so they make the forms five: these three along with the conditional conjunctive and the conditional disjunctive (istithna'i muttasil and munfasil). So these forms, if they are granted to be useful, are forms of proofs, but the depiction of proofs is not limited to these forms, just as the depiction of proof is not limited to two premises. Rather this restriction is an error in negation and affirmation. For a proof may be one premise, and may be [several] premises. And these forms often depict proof in other forms besides these, so they are not limited to five or six. And a proof may be brought without all these forms, and without the two premises if it is a single premise. And if they say: 'All proofs that are mentioned reduce to these forms,' it is said to them: 'Rather proofs reduce to the fact that the proof necessitates the proven. Then either consideration is given to what they mentioned of the forms and their wording, or to what they mentioned of the meaning. If the criterion is the form, then specifying the form of proof to five or six is not correct, just as specifying it to two premises is not correct, since it can be depicted in many diverse forms that have no conditional wording, neither conjunctive nor disjunctive, nor is it in the form of categorical syllogism as they mentioned. And if the criterion is the meaning, then that is more indicative of the corruption of what they mentioned. For the meaning is that what is used as evidence necessitates what it indicates, whether it is one premise, two premises, or more, and whether it is in the form and order they mentioned or otherwise.'
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' claim that valid arguments are limited to five or six specific forms (the three figures of categorical syllogism plus conditional conjunctive and disjunctive forms). He begins by asserting that such a restriction is baseless. He notes that many logicians omit one form, leaving five, but even then the diversity of actual proofs exceeds these categories. The core of his refutation is the distinction between form and meaning. If form is the criterion, then any number of forms are possible that do not fit their mold. If meaning is the criterion, then the essence of proof is entailment (istilzam) — that the evidence necessarily implies the conclusion — regardless of the number of premises or their arrangement. Hence, the logicians' limitation is both arbitrary and erroneous.

Key Terms
الشكل
مستلزم
القياس الحملي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the core of any proof?
A) Having exactly two premises
B) Following one of the five forms
C) The premise entailing the conclusion
D) Using conditional statements

Key Takeaway
For Ibn Taymiyya, the validity of an argument depends solely on whether the evidence entails the conclusion, not on its conformity to predetermined forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Logicians' System: Needless Elongation and Error
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' method as counterproductive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's key objections to the formal syllogistic system and explain why he claims it increases mistakes.

Core Idea
The logicians' artificial system hinders rather than aids correct reasoning.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a sharp critique of the entire Aristotelian logical tradition as practiced by his contemporaries. He argues that their method, even when logically sound, is a needless elongation that actually leads to more errors rather than preventing them. This lesson unpacks his core objection: that the system is a camel's flesh on a rough mountain—burdensome and fruitless.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Read the opening lines: 'What they mentioned, if it is correct, is lengthening of the path...' Ibn Taymiyya is attacking the logicians' claim that their method prevents mental slips.
The phrase 'legal instrument' (آلة قانونية) refers to Aristotle's Organon.
2
Extract the empirical claim
He asserts that people who adhere to this system are actually more mistaken than those who rely on fitra. Find the sentence that states this empirical observation.
3
Analyze the example of the camel
The proverb 'like the flesh of a camel on the top of a rough mountain' illustrates the uselessness of the system despite its apparent substance.
This is a rhetorical device emphasizing benefit without accessibility.
4
Summarize the overall objection
Synthesize: Ibn Taymiyya claims the logicians' system is lengthy, distracts from the goal, and contradicts human nature (fitra).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس فيه إلا والصواب في هذا الباب أن يقال : ما ذكروه إذا كان صواباً فإنه تطويل للطريق، تطويل وتبعيد للمطلوب، وعكس للمقصود فإنهم زعموا أنهم جعلوه آلة قانونية تمنع الذهن أن يزل في فكره. وما ذكروه إذا كلفوا الناظر المستدل أن يلزمه في تصوراته وتصديقاته كان أقرب إلى زلله في فكره وضلاله عن مطلوبه، كما هو الواقع. فلا تجد أحداً التزم وضع هؤلاء واصطلاحهم إلا كان أكثر خطأ وأقل صواباً ممن لم يلتزم وضعهم وسلك إلى المطلوب بفطرة الله التي فطر عباده عليها. ولهذا لا يوجد أحد من محققي العلوم كان ملتزماً لوضعهم. ولهذا يقال : كثرة هذه الأشكال وشروط نتاجها تطويل قليل الفائدة كثير التعب. مثل لحم جمل على رأس جبل وعر. المقام الرابع - كون كثرة أشكال الأدلة تطويلاً قليل الفائدة. وقد قال: متى كانت المادة صحيحة أمكن تصويرها بالشكل الأول الفطري. فبقية الأشكال لا يحتاج إليها، وإنما هي تفيد بالرد إلى الشكل الأول، إما بإبطال النقيض الذي يتضمنه قياس الخلف، وإما بالعكس المستوي أو عكس النقيض. فإن ثبوت أحد المتناقضين يستلزم نفي الآخر إذا روعي التناقض من كل وجه. فهم يستدلون بصحة القضية على بطلان نقيضها وعلى ثبوت عكسها المستوي وعكس نقيضها بل تصور الذهن بصورة الدليل يشبه حساب الإنسان لما معه من الرقيق والعقار. والفطرة تصور القياس الصحيح من غير تعليم. والناس بفطرهم يتكلمون بالأنواع القياس الثلاثة : التداخل والتلازم والتقسيم كما يتكلمون بالحساب ونحوه. والمنطقيون قد يسلمون ذلك. ويقولون : إن المقدمتين لا بد منهما، لا أكثر ولا أقل. ويقولون أكثر الناس قياس الضمير يحذفون إحدى المقدمتين لظهورها اختصاراً، ويسمون هذا قياس الضمير. وإن احتاج المطلوب إلى مقدمات جعلوا القياس معنى أقيسة متعددة بناءً على قولهم إنه لا بد في القياس من مقدمتين، ويسمونه القياس المركب، ويقسمونه إلى موصول وهو ما لا يذكر فيه إلا نتيجة واحدة وإلى مفصول وهو ما يذكر فيه عقب كل مقدمتين نتيجة. فالأول كقولك هذا نبيذ، وكل نبيذ مسكر وكل مسكر خمر، وكل خمر حرام، فهذا حرام. والثاني كقولك: هذا نبيذ وكل نبيذ مسكر، فهذا مسكر؛ ثم تقول: وكل مسكر خمر وكل خمر حرام، فكل مسكر حرام؛ ثم تقول: هذا مسكر وكل مسكر حرام، فهذا حرام. وهذا كله مما غلطوا فيه. والصواب الذي عليه جماهير النظار من المسلمين وغيرهم أن الدليل قد يتوقف على مقدمة تارة، وقد يتوقف على مقدمتين تارة، وعلى ثلاث تارة، وعلى أكثر من تلك. فما كان من المقدمات معلوماً لم يحتج أن يستدل عليه، وإنما يستدل على المجهول. والمطلوب المجهول يعلم بدليله، ودليله (۱) فكل : في الأصل «مثل».
There is nothing in it except, and the correct opinion in this chapter is to say: What they mentioned, if it is correct, is lengthening of the path, lengthening and distancing from the sought, and contrary to the objective. For they claimed that they made it a legal instrument that prevents the mind from slipping in its thought. But what they mentioned, if they burden the observer-inferrer to adhere to it in his conceptualizations and assents, is closer to his slipping in his thought and his misguidance from his sought, as is the reality. So you do not find anyone who committed to the position and terminology of these people except that he was more mistaken and less correct than one who did not commit to their position and followed the sought by the natural disposition (fitra) of Allah with which He created His servants. Thus, none of the verifiers of the sciences were committed to their position. Hence it is said: The abundance of these forms and their conditions of productivity is a lengthening with little benefit and much weariness. Like the flesh of a camel on the top of a rough mountain. The Fourth Station - That the abundance of forms of proofs is a lengthening with little benefit. And it has been said: Whenever the matter is correct, it is possible to depict it in the first natural figure. Therefore the remaining forms are not needed; they only benefit by reduction to the first figure, either by nullifying the contradiction involved in the proof by contradiction, or by simple conversion or conversion by contraposition. For the establishment of one of the contradictories necessitates the negation of the other if the contradiction is observed from all aspects. So they infer from the truth of a proposition the falsity of its contradictory and the truth of its simple converse and its contrapositive. Rather, the mind's conception of the form of proof is like a person's calculation of his slaves and properties. And the natural disposition conceives correct syllogism without instruction. And people by their nature speak with the three types of syllogism: overlapping (tadakhul), entailment (talazum), and division (taqsim) just as they speak with arithmetic and the like. And the logicians sometimes concede that. They say: The two premises are necessary, no more and no less. And they say: Most people [use] syllogism implicit (qiyas al-damir), deleting one premise for its obviousness for brevity, and they call this 'implicit syllogism'. And if the sought requires premises, they make the syllogism a meaning of multiple syllogisms based on their statement that 'a syllogism requires two premises', and they call it 'compound syllogism' (qiyas murakkab), and divide it into 'connected' (mawsul) which is that in which only one conclusion is mentioned, and 'separated' (mafsul) which is that in which after every two premises a result is mentioned. The first is like your saying: 'This is nabidh, and every nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicating is wine, and every wine is forbidden, so this is forbidden.' The second is like your saying: 'This is nabidh and every nabidh is intoxicating, so this is intoxicating'; then you say: 'Every intoxicating is wine and every wine is forbidden, so every intoxicating is forbidden'; then you say: 'This is intoxicating and every intoxicating is forbidden, so this is forbidden.' And all of this is a mistake. The correct view, held by the majority of investigators among the Muslims and others, is that a proof may depend on one premise sometimes, and on two premises sometimes, and on three sometimes, and on more than those. For what is known of premises does not need to be proven, but only the unknown is proven. And the unknown sought is known by its proof, and its proof is what (1) 'kull' (every) in the original is 'mithl' (like).
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of Ibn Taymiyya's critique, now focusing on the practical harm of adhering to the logicians' system. He argues that their method, even if logically correct, is a needless elongation and often leads to more errors rather than preventing them. He contrasts this with the natural human capacity for reasoning (fitra), which is sufficient for correct inference. He cites the example that the logicians themselves admit that all valid arguments reduce to the first figure, making the other figures redundant. He then discusses the logicians' concepts of 'implicit syllogism' (qiyas al-damir) and 'compound syllogism' (qiyas murakkab), illustrating with the example of nabidh (date wine) to show how they force arguments into two-premise forms, even when more premises are present. Ibn Taymiyya rejects this restriction, asserting that a proof can have one, two, three, or more premises, depending on what needs to be proven. The marginal note at the end indicates a textual variant.

Key Terms
قياس الضمير
القياس المركب
التداخل
التلازم
التقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what happens to someone who commits to the logicians' system?
A) They become more accurate in reasoning
B) They become more mistaken and less correct
C) They become better at mathematics
D) They attain certainty in all matters

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's core objection is that the logicians' formal system is a needless elongation that contradicts human fitra and leads to more errors, not less.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read Ibn Taymiyya's empirical claim: 'You do not find anyone who committed to the position of these people except that he was more mistaken.' Find a historical example of a scholar who adhered to formal logic and assess Ibn Taymiyya's claim from your knowledge.
?
Consider a typical qiyas in fiqh, e.g., 'Nabidh is forbidden because it is intoxicating like wine.' Explain how Ibn Taymiyya would critique the logician's requirement to break it into a compound syllogism.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Natural Reasoning: Fitra and the Three Types of Syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya describes natural reasoning types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the three natural types of syllogism (tadakhul, talazum, taqsim) and provide an Islamic example of each.

Core Idea
People naturally reason correctly without formal training in logic.

Ibn Taymiyya argues that correct reasoning is innate (fitra) and does not require the logicians' artificial system. He identifies three natural types of syllogism that people use spontaneously: overlapping (tadakhul), entailment (talazum), and division (taqsim). This lesson explores these types and their relation to everyday inference in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Find the statement about fitra
Locate the sentence: 'And the natural disposition conceives correct syllogism without instruction.' This is the key claim.
Fitra is the God-given natural capacity for truth.
2
Identify the three named types
The passage lists: al-tadakhul (overlapping), al-talazum (entailment), and al-taqsim (division). Note the Arabic terms.
These are not standard Aristotelian categories.
3
Define each type in your own words
Tadakhul likely refers to reasoning where predicates overlap (e.g., 'All A is B and all B is C, so all A is C'). Talazum is implication (if one thing entails another). Taqsim is disjunctive elimination (either A or B, not A, therefore B).
Ibn Taymiyya does not define them here; we infer from context.
4
Provide an Islamic example for each
For tadakhul: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, and every nabidh is an intoxicant, so nabidh is forbidden.' For talazum: 'If the world is created, it has a Creator.' For taqsim: 'Either the action is obligatory or permissible; it is not obligatory, so it is permissible.'
Ensure examples are from Islamic sources.
5
Contrast with logicians' formal examples
Ibn Taymiyya says people speak with these types 'just as they speak with arithmetic and the like'—implying naturalness, unlike the logicians' artificial compound syllogisms.

The Two-Premise Restriction and Compound Syllogism: Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the two-premise rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the logicians' claim that a syllogism must have exactly two premises, using the nabidh example.

Core Idea
A proof may have one, two, or many premises; forcing it into two is artificial.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks a central tenet of Aristotelian logic: that every syllogism consists of exactly two premises. He shows how the logicians themselves are forced to invent compound syllogisms (qiyas murakkab) to handle arguments with more premises. Using the example of nabidh (date wine), he illustrates the awkwardness and argues that the correct view is flexibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' rule being critiqued
The passage states: 'They say: The two premises are necessary, no more and no less.' This is the target.
2
Examine the logicians' solution for multiple premises
They invent compound syllogism (qiyas murakkab) to chain multiple two-premise syllogisms. Note the two types: mawsul and mafsul.
Mawsul (connected) mentions only the final conclusion; mafsul (separated) mentions intermediate conclusions.
3
Analyze the nabidh example
The example: 'This is nabidh, every nabidh is intoxicating, every intoxicating is forbidden, so this is forbidden.' This is a chain of three premises. The logicians would break it into multiple syllogisms. Ibn Taymiyya calls this a mistake.
The example is from fiqh: nabidh is a date-based drink.
4
State Ibn Taymiyya's alternative ruling
He says: 'a proof may depend on one premise sometimes, and on two premises sometimes, and on three sometimes, and on more.'
This is the correct view according to the majority of investigators.
5
Reflect on the fitra connection
The natural mind would accept the three-premise argument as a single valid inference. The logicians' restriction forces an unnatural division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس فيه إلا والصواب في هذا الباب أن يقال : ما ذكروه إذا كان صواباً فإنه تطويل للطريق، تطويل وتبعيد للمطلوب، وعكس للمقصود فإنهم زعموا أنهم جعلوه آلة قانونية تمنع الذهن أن يزل في فكره. وما ذكروه إذا كلفوا الناظر المستدل أن يلزمه في تصوراته وتصديقاته كان أقرب إلى زلله في فكره وضلاله عن مطلوبه، كما هو الواقع. فلا تجد أحداً التزم وضع هؤلاء واصطلاحهم إلا كان أكثر خطأ وأقل صواباً ممن لم يلتزم وضعهم وسلك إلى المطلوب بفطرة الله التي فطر عباده عليها. ولهذا لا يوجد أحد من محققي العلوم كان ملتزماً لوضعهم. ولهذا يقال : كثرة هذه الأشكال وشروط نتاجها تطويل قليل الفائدة كثير التعب. مثل لحم جمل على رأس جبل وعر. المقام الرابع - كون كثرة أشكال الأدلة تطويلاً قليل الفائدة. وقد قال: متى كانت المادة صحيحة أمكن تصويرها بالشكل الأول الفطري. فبقية الأشكال لا يحتاج إليها، وإنما هي تفيد بالرد إلى الشكل الأول، إما بإبطال النقيض الذي يتضمنه قياس الخلف، وإما بالعكس المستوي أو عكس النقيض. فإن ثبوت أحد المتناقضين يستلزم نفي الآخر إذا روعي التناقض من كل وجه. فهم يستدلون بصحة القضية على بطلان نقيضها وعلى ثبوت عكسها المستوي وعكس نقيضها بل تصور الذهن بصورة الدليل يشبه حساب الإنسان لما معه من الرقيق والعقار. والفطرة تصور القياس الصحيح من غير تعليم. والناس بفطرهم يتكلمون بالأنواع القياس الثلاثة : التداخل والتلازم والتقسيم كما يتكلمون بالحساب ونحوه. والمنطقيون قد يسلمون ذلك. ويقولون : إن المقدمتين لا بد منهما، لا أكثر ولا أقل. ويقولون أكثر الناس قياس الضمير يحذفون إحدى المقدمتين لظهورها اختصاراً، ويسمون هذا قياس الضمير. وإن احتاج المطلوب إلى مقدمات جعلوا القياس معنى أقيسة متعددة بناءً على قولهم إنه لا بد في القياس من مقدمتين، ويسمونه القياس المركب، ويقسمونه إلى موصول وهو ما لا يذكر فيه إلا نتيجة واحدة وإلى مفصول وهو ما يذكر فيه عقب كل مقدمتين نتيجة. فالأول كقولك هذا نبيذ، وكل نبيذ مسكر وكل مسكر خمر، وكل خمر حرام، فهذا حرام. والثاني كقولك: هذا نبيذ وكل نبيذ مسكر، فهذا مسكر؛ ثم تقول: وكل مسكر خمر وكل خمر حرام، فكل مسكر حرام؛ ثم تقول: هذا مسكر وكل مسكر حرام، فهذا حرام. وهذا كله مما غلطوا فيه. والصواب الذي عليه جماهير النظار من المسلمين وغيرهم أن الدليل قد يتوقف على مقدمة تارة، وقد يتوقف على مقدمتين تارة، وعلى ثلاث تارة، وعلى أكثر من تلك. فما كان من المقدمات معلوماً لم يحتج أن يستدل عليه، وإنما يستدل على المجهول. والمطلوب المجهول يعلم بدليله، ودليله (۱) فكل : في الأصل «مثل».
There is nothing in it except, and the correct opinion in this chapter is to say: What they mentioned, if it is correct, is lengthening of the path, lengthening and distancing from the sought, and contrary to the objective. For they claimed that they made it a legal instrument that prevents the mind from slipping in its thought. But what they mentioned, if they burden the observer-inferrer to adhere to it in his conceptualizations and assents, is closer to his slipping in his thought and his misguidance from his sought, as is the reality. So you do not find anyone who committed to the position and terminology of these people except that he was more mistaken and less correct than one who did not commit to their position and followed the sought by the natural disposition (fitra) of Allah with which He created His servants. Thus, none of the verifiers of the sciences were committed to their position. Hence it is said: The abundance of these forms and their conditions of productivity is a lengthening with little benefit and much weariness. Like the flesh of a camel on the top of a rough mountain. The Fourth Station - That the abundance of forms of proofs is a lengthening with little benefit. And it has been said: Whenever the matter is correct, it is possible to depict it in the first natural figure. Therefore the remaining forms are not needed; they only benefit by reduction to the first figure, either by nullifying the contradiction involved in the proof by contradiction, or by simple conversion or conversion by contraposition. For the establishment of one of the contradictories necessitates the negation of the other if the contradiction is observed from all aspects. So they infer from the truth of a proposition the falsity of its contradictory and the truth of its simple converse and its contrapositive. Rather, the mind's conception of the form of proof is like a person's calculation of his slaves and properties. And the natural disposition conceives correct syllogism without instruction. And people by their nature speak with the three types of syllogism: overlapping (tadakhul), entailment (talazum), and division (taqsim) just as they speak with arithmetic and the like. And the logicians sometimes concede that. They say: The two premises are necessary, no more and no less. And they say: Most people [use] syllogism implicit (qiyas al-damir), deleting one premise for its obviousness for brevity, and they call this 'implicit syllogism'. And if the sought requires premises, they make the syllogism a meaning of multiple syllogisms based on their statement that 'a syllogism requires two premises', and they call it 'compound syllogism' (qiyas murakkab), and divide it into 'connected' (mawsul) which is that in which only one conclusion is mentioned, and 'separated' (mafsul) which is that in which after every two premises a result is mentioned. The first is like your saying: 'This is nabidh, and every nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicating is wine, and every wine is forbidden, so this is forbidden.' The second is like your saying: 'This is nabidh and every nabidh is intoxicating, so this is intoxicating'; then you say: 'Every intoxicating is wine and every wine is forbidden, so every intoxicating is forbidden'; then you say: 'This is intoxicating and every intoxicating is forbidden, so this is forbidden.' And all of this is a mistake. The correct view, held by the majority of investigators among the Muslims and others, is that a proof may depend on one premise sometimes, and on two premises sometimes, and on three sometimes, and on more than those. For what is known of premises does not need to be proven, but only the unknown is proven. And the unknown sought is known by its proof, and its proof is what (1) 'kull' (every) in the original is 'mithl' (like).
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of Ibn Taymiyya's critique, now focusing on the practical harm of adhering to the logicians' system. He argues that their method, even if logically correct, is a needless elongation and often leads to more errors rather than preventing them. He contrasts this with the natural human capacity for reasoning (fitra), which is sufficient for correct inference. He cites the example that the logicians themselves admit that all valid arguments reduce to the first figure, making the other figures redundant. He then discusses the logicians' concepts of 'implicit syllogism' (qiyas al-damir) and 'compound syllogism' (qiyas murakkab), illustrating with the example of nabidh (date wine) to show how they force arguments into two-premise forms, even when more premises are present. Ibn Taymiyya rejects this restriction, asserting that a proof can have one, two, three, or more premises, depending on what needs to be proven. The marginal note at the end indicates a textual variant.

الموصول
المفصول
قياس الضمير

The Syllogism

The Redundancy of Multiple Syllogistic Forms
Ibn Taymiyya argues that multiple syllogistic forms are unnecessary and reducible to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the proliferation of syllogistic forms and explain why he considers only the first figure necessary.

Core Idea
Only the first figure is natural; all other forms are mere reductions to it.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logician tradition for multiplying syllogistic forms. He argues that if the premises are correct, any valid argument can be expressed in the natural first figure. The other figures are merely reductions via contradiction, conversion, or contrapositive. In this lesson, we analyze his critique and its implications for logical methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
What exactly is Ibn Taymiyya criticizing? He is criticizing the multiplication of syllogistic forms beyond the first figure, which he considers unnecessary.
He uses the metaphor of a thin camel on a rough mountain to illustrate useless complexity.
2
Understand the metaphor
Why does he compare the other forms to a thin camel on a rough mountain? Because they are of little benefit and difficult to use, unlike the first figure which is natural and straightforward.
The metaphor emphasizes effort without reward.
3
List the three reduction methods
What three methods does he mention for reducing other figures to the first figure? (1) Nullifying the contradictory (khalf), (2) simple conversion (aks mustawi), (3) contrapositive (aks al-naqid).
These are immediate inferences or indirect proofs.
4
Appreciate the logical basis
How does affirming one contradictory entail negation of the other? Ibn Taymiyya explains that if two propositions are contradictories (one true, one false), affirming one necessarily negates the other when the contradiction is perfect.
This underpins the reduction via khalf.
5
Evaluate the critique
Is Ibn Taymiyya denying the validity of other forms? No, he acknowledges they can be reduced to the first figure, but he argues they are redundant and add no new logical power.
His critique is about economy and naturalness, not correctness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - كون كثرة أشكال الأدلة تطويلاً قليل الفائدة
متی
فهو لحم جَمَل غَتْ على رأس جبل وَعِر ، لا سهل فيُرتَقَى ولا سمين فينتقل . فإنه كانت المادة صحيحة أمكن تصويرها بالشكل الأول الفِطري. فبقية الأشكال لا يحتاج إليها، وإنما هي تفيد بالرد إلى الشكل الأول، إما بإبطال النقيض الذي يتضمنه قياس الخلف»، وإما بالعكس المستوي أو عكس النقيض . فإن ثبوت أحد المتناقضين يستلزم نفي الآخر إذا رُوعي التناقض من كل وجه . فهم يستدلون بصحة القضية على بطلان نقيضها وعلى ثبوت عكسها المستوي وعكس نقيضها
The fourth topic: That the multiplicity of the forms of proofs is lengthy with little benefit. … It is like the flesh of a thin camel on top of a rough mountain, neither easy to climb nor fat to carry. For if the matter is correct, it can be depicted in the natural first figure. So the rest of the figures are not needed; they only benefit by reduction to the first figure, either by nullifying the contradictory included in the syllogism per absurdum, or by simple conversion, or by contrapositive. For the confirmation of one of two contradictories entails the negation of the other when the contradiction is observed from every aspect. Thus they infer from the truth of a proposition the falsehood of its contradictory, and the truth of its simple converse and its contrapositive.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' multiplication of syllogistic forms. Ibn Taymiyya opens with the metaphor of a thin camel on a rough mountain to illustrate the uselessness of excessive forms. He argues that if the matter (premises) is sound, it can be expressed directly in the first figure, which is natural. All other figures are unnecessary and only serve to reduce to the first figure through three methods: (1) reduction by contradiction (khalf), where one proves a proposition by showing its negation leads to absurdity; (2) simple conversion (aks mustawi), switching subject and predicate; (3) contrapositive (aks al-naqid), replacing a proposition with its contrapositive. He then explains the logical basis: if two propositions are true contradictories (one true, one false), affirming one necessarily negates the other. Similarly, from a true proposition, one can infer the falsehood of its contradictory, the truth of its simple converse, and the truth of its contrapositive. The commentator should note that Ibn Taymiyya is not denying the validity of these reductions but criticizing the proliferation of forms as redundant. The first figure alone is sufficient; the other figures are merely tools to bring arguments back to it.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول
قياس الخلف
العكس المستوي
عكس النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Natural Reasoning and the Three Types of Qiyas
Ibn Taymiyya on natural reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three natural types of qiyas (tadakhul, talazum, taqsim) and explain how fitra grasps correct reasoning without formal instruction.

Core Idea
Fitra spontaneously uses three types of qiyas: overlap, implication, and division.

Ibn Taymiyya argues that human fitra naturally conceives correct reasoning without needing formal logic. People by their nature employ three types of syllogism: tadakhul (categorical overlap), talazum (conditional implication), and taqsim (disjunctive division). This lesson explores this natural logic and contrasts it with the logicians' artificial constraints.

Guided Path
1
Understand fitra's role in reasoning
Read the first sentence: 'The natural disposition (fitra) and the fitra conceive correct reasoning without instruction.' Note that fitra is sufficient for sound inference.
2
Identify the three natural qiyas types
Find the phrase 'three types of qiyas: tadakhul, talazum, and taqsim.' Tadakhul corresponds to categorical syllogism (overlap of terms), talazum to conditional syllogism (implication), and taqsim to disjunctive syllogism (division of options).
These are not the only types; they are the natural ones.
3
Compare to arithmetic
Note the analogy: 'just as they speak using arithmetic and the like.' This implies logical reasoning is as innate as basic math.
4
Contrast with logicians' two-premise rule
Ibn Taymiyya then mentions that logicians concede this natural reasoning but still insist on exactly two premises, leading to artificial constructs like enthymeme and compound syllogism.
This sets up the critique in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصور الفطرة والفطرة تصوّر القياس الصحيح من غير تعليم . والناس بفطرهم يتكلمون بالأنواع القياس الثلاثة : التداخل والتلازم، والتقسيم كما يتكلمون بالحساب ونحوه . والمنطقيون قد يسلمون ذلك . ويقولون : إن المقدمتين لا بد منهما، لا أكثر ولا أقل ويقولون أكثر الناس قياس الضمير يحذفون إحدى المقدمتين لظهورها اختصاراً، ويسمون هذا «قياس الضمير». وإن احتاج المطلوب إلى مقدمات جعلوا القياس معنى أقيسة متعددة بناءً على قولهم إنه لا بد في القياس من مقدمتين»، ويسمونه «القياس المركب»، ويقسمونه إلى «موصول»، وهو ما لا يذكر فيه إلا نتيجة واحدة وإلى مفصول »، وهو ما يذكر فيه عقب كل مقدمتين نتيجة . فالأول كقولك هذا نبيذ، وكل نبيذ مسكر وكل مسكر خمر، وكل خمر حرام، فهذا حرام والثاني كقولك: هذا نبيذ وكل نبيذ مسكر، فهذا مسكر ؛ ثم تقول : وكل مسكر ،خمر وكل خمر ،حرام، فكل مسكر حرام ؛ ثم تقول : هذا مسكر وكل مسكر حرام، فهذا حرام.
The natural disposition (fitra) and the fitra conceive correct reasoning without instruction. People by their fitras speak using the three types of qiyas: overlapping (tadakhul), implication (talazum), and division (taqsim), just as they speak using arithmetic and the like. The logicians may concede that. They say: the two premises are necessary, no more and no less. And they say: most people have an enthymeme (qiyas al-damir) — they delete one of the two premises due to its obviousness for brevity, and they call this 'enthymeme'. And if the sought conclusion requires premises, they make the qiyas a meaning of multiple syllogisms based on their statement that a qiyas must have two premises, and they call it 'compound qiyas', and they divide it into 'connected' (mawsul) — where only one conclusion is mentioned — and 'disconnected' (mafsul) — where after each pair of premises a conclusion is mentioned. The first is like your saying: 'This is nabidh, and every nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicating thing is khamr, and every khamr is forbidden, therefore this is forbidden.' The second is like your saying: 'This is nabidh, and every nabidh is intoxicating, therefore this is intoxicating; then you say: And every intoxicating thing is khamr, and every khamr is forbidden, therefore every intoxicating thing is forbidden; then you say: This is intoxicating, and every intoxicating thing is forbidden, therefore this is forbidden.'
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' insistence on exactly two premises and their invention of compound syllogisms. Ibn Taymiyya begins by affirming that human fitra (natural disposition) grasps correct reasoning without formal instruction. People naturally use three types of qiyas: tadakhul (overlap, like categorical syllogism), talazum (implication, like conditional syllogism), and taqsim (division, like disjunctive syllogism). Logicians themselves acknowledge this natural reasoning. They then impose a strict two-premise rule and call incomplete arguments 'enthymeme' (qiyas al-damir). When more than two premises are needed, they artificially construct 'compound qiyas' (al-qiyas al-murakkab), dividing it into connected (mawsul) and disconnected (mafsul) forms. Ibn Taymiyya presents the example of nabidh (date wine) to illustrate both forms. In the connected form, all premises are linked and only the final conclusion is stated. In the disconnected form, intermediate conclusions are stated after each pair of premises. The commentator should highlight that Ibn Taymiyya is critiquing this as an unnecessary and artificial multiplication of rules. The example uses an Islamic legal chain: nabidh is intoxicating, intoxicants are khamr, and khamr is haram. This example shows how natural reasoning suffices without formal divisions.

Key Terms
التداخل
التلازم
التقسيم

Key Takeaway
Human fitra naturally uses three types of syllogism without formal instruction, and logicians' artificial rules are superimposed on this innate ability.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Compound Syllogisms
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of compound syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two forms of compound syllogism (mawsul and mafsul) and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's critique that they are artificial constructs.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms are an unnecessary invention of logicians; fitra uses a single chain of reasoning.

Ibn Taymiyya presents the logicians' compound syllogism, divided into connected and disconnected forms, using the example of nabidh. He critiques this as an artificial multiplication of rules, arguing that natural reasoning would simply see one continuous inference.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logicians' two-premise rule
The logicians insist that every syllogism must have exactly two premises. When a conclusion requires more than two premises, they create a compound syllogism.
This rule is the basis for the compound syllogism.
2
Learn qiyas al-damir (enthymeme)
When one premise is obvious, people omit it for brevity. Logicians call this qiyas al-damir.
3
Examine the compound syllogism definitions
The logicians divide compound syllogism into mawsul (connected) – only final conclusion stated – and mafsul (disconnected) – intermediate conclusions stated after each pair of premises.
4
Analyze the nabidh example in both forms
In mawsul: 'This is nabidh, and every nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicating thing is khamr, and every khamr is forbidden, therefore this is forbidden.' In mafsul: three separate syllogisms with step-by-step conclusions.
Both give the same final result.
5
Grasp Ibn Taymiyya's point
Ibn Taymiyya implies that fitra would simply see one continuous reasoning chain, not three separate syllogisms. The compound structure is an unnecessary formalism.
The critique argues that natural reasoning is simpler and more intuitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصور الفطرة والفطرة تصوّر القياس الصحيح من غير تعليم . والناس بفطرهم يتكلمون بالأنواع القياس الثلاثة : التداخل والتلازم، والتقسيم كما يتكلمون بالحساب ونحوه . والمنطقيون قد يسلمون ذلك . ويقولون : إن المقدمتين لا بد منهما، لا أكثر ولا أقل ويقولون أكثر الناس قياس الضمير يحذفون إحدى المقدمتين لظهورها اختصاراً، ويسمون هذا «قياس الضمير». وإن احتاج المطلوب إلى مقدمات جعلوا القياس معنى أقيسة متعددة بناءً على قولهم إنه لا بد في القياس من مقدمتين»، ويسمونه «القياس المركب»، ويقسمونه إلى «موصول»، وهو ما لا يذكر فيه إلا نتيجة واحدة وإلى مفصول »، وهو ما يذكر فيه عقب كل مقدمتين نتيجة . فالأول كقولك هذا نبيذ، وكل نبيذ مسكر وكل مسكر خمر، وكل خمر حرام، فهذا حرام والثاني كقولك: هذا نبيذ وكل نبيذ مسكر، فهذا مسكر ؛ ثم تقول : وكل مسكر ،خمر وكل خمر ،حرام، فكل مسكر حرام ؛ ثم تقول : هذا مسكر وكل مسكر حرام، فهذا حرام.
The natural disposition (fitra) and the fitra conceive correct reasoning without instruction. People by their fitras speak using the three types of qiyas: overlapping (tadakhul), implication (talazum), and division (taqsim), just as they speak using arithmetic and the like. The logicians may concede that. They say: the two premises are necessary, no more and no less. And they say: most people have an enthymeme (qiyas al-damir) — they delete one of the two premises due to its obviousness for brevity, and they call this 'enthymeme'. And if the sought conclusion requires premises, they make the qiyas a meaning of multiple syllogisms based on their statement that a qiyas must have two premises, and they call it 'compound qiyas', and they divide it into 'connected' (mawsul) — where only one conclusion is mentioned — and 'disconnected' (mafsul) — where after each pair of premises a conclusion is mentioned. The first is like your saying: 'This is nabidh, and every nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicating thing is khamr, and every khamr is forbidden, therefore this is forbidden.' The second is like your saying: 'This is nabidh, and every nabidh is intoxicating, therefore this is intoxicating; then you say: And every intoxicating thing is khamr, and every khamr is forbidden, therefore every intoxicating thing is forbidden; then you say: This is intoxicating, and every intoxicating thing is forbidden, therefore this is forbidden.'
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' insistence on exactly two premises and their invention of compound syllogisms. Ibn Taymiyya begins by affirming that human fitra (natural disposition) grasps correct reasoning without formal instruction. People naturally use three types of qiyas: tadakhul (overlap, like categorical syllogism), talazum (implication, like conditional syllogism), and taqsim (division, like disjunctive syllogism). Logicians themselves acknowledge this natural reasoning. They then impose a strict two-premise rule and call incomplete arguments 'enthymeme' (qiyas al-damir). When more than two premises are needed, they artificially construct 'compound qiyas' (al-qiyas al-murakkab), dividing it into connected (mawsul) and disconnected (mafsul) forms. Ibn Taymiyya presents the example of nabidh (date wine) to illustrate both forms. In the connected form, all premises are linked and only the final conclusion is stated. In the disconnected form, intermediate conclusions are stated after each pair of premises. The commentator should highlight that Ibn Taymiyya is critiquing this as an unnecessary and artificial multiplication of rules. The example uses an Islamic legal chain: nabidh is intoxicating, intoxicants are khamr, and khamr is haram. This example shows how natural reasoning suffices without formal divisions.

Key Terms
قياس الضمير
القياس المركب
موصول
مفصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Simple and complex
B) Connected (mawsul) and disconnected (mafsul)
C) Inductive and deductive
D) Valid and invalid

Key Takeaway
Compound syllogisms are an artificial imposition by logicians; natural fitra reasons correctly without such formal divisions.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Fixed Premise Number
Ibn Taymiyya states the correct Islamic view on the number of premises in a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the fixed two-premise requirement and demonstrate how the number of premises varies based on the chain of malzum and lazim.

Core Idea
The number of premises in a proof is flexible, not fixed at two.

Classical logicians insisted that every syllogism must have exactly two premises. Ibn Taymiyya here corrects this error, citing the practice of Muslim and non-Muslim thinkers. He builds a simpler, more intuitive model: the number of premises depends on how many steps of entailment separate the known from the unknown. This lesson unpacks his alternative framework using the concepts of malzum (necessary condition) and lazim (necessary consequence).

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Find the exact claim Ibn Taymiyya is attacking: that a proof must have exactly two premises. The passage begins by calling this an error.
He refers to the logicians who restricted proofs to two premises.
2
Extract the correct view
State the correct view he advocates: the proof may depend on one, two, three, or more premises. The number is not fixed.
He says 'the majority of investigators from the Muslims and others' hold this view.
3
Understand the malzum-lazim framework
Grasp that a proof is based on entailment: the known entails the unknown. The malzum (necessary condition) is what we start from, and the lazim (necessary consequence) is what we aim to prove—or vice versa. If the sought conclusion is directly entailed by known knowledge, one premise suffices. If there is a chain, each missing link adds a premise.
For example, if we know that a drink is khamr and we know that khamr is haram, one premise ('this is khamr') suffices because the entailment is already known. If we only know that it is a liquid from grapes, more premises are needed.
4
Apply the framework to varying premise counts
Work through his examples: if the seeker only knows the malzum of the malzum of the conclusion, he needs two premises; if only the malzum of malzum of malzum, he needs three, and so on. This shows flexibility.
The key is that the number of premises equals the number of unknown entailment steps.
5
Assess the critique's validity
Evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's argument: does it logically refute the fixed-two-premise claim? His framework is internally consistent and based on a realistic view of how proofs work in Islamic sciences (e.g., fiqh, kalam).
The critique is valid because it provides a more general model that explains both simple and complex proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا كله مما غلطوا فيه. والصواب الذي عليه جماهير النظار من المسلمين وغيرهم أن الدليل قد يتوقف على مقدمة تارة، وقد يتوقف على مقدمتين تارة، وعلى ثلاث تارة، وعلى أكثر من تلك. فما كان من المقدمات معلوماً لم يحتج أن يستدل عليه، وإنما يستدل على المجهول. والمطلوب المجهول يعلم بدليله، ودليله ما استلزمه. وكل ملزوم فإنه يصح الاستدلال به على لازمه وحينئذ فإذا كان المطلوب ملزوم يُعلَم لزومه له أستدل عليه به، وكفى ذلك . وإن لم يكن المستدِل يَعلم إلا ملزوم ملزومه احتاج إلى مقدمتين وإن لم يعلم إلا ملزوم ملزوم ملزومه احتاج إلى ثلاث، وهلم جرا .
All of this is among the things they erred in. The correct view upon which the majority of the theoretical investigators (nuzzar) from the Muslims and others is that the proof may depend on one premise at times, may depend on two premises at times, on three at times, and on more than that. For what is known of the premises does not need to be proven; rather, one only proves the unknown. And the unknown sought conclusion is known by its proof, and its proof is what entails it. And every necessary condition (malzum) it is valid to infer from it its necessary consequence (lazim). So then if the sought conclusion is a necessary condition whose entailment to it is known, one can infer it from that, and that suffices. And if the inferrer only knows the necessary condition of the necessary condition of it, he needs two premises; and if he only knows the necessary condition of the necessary condition of the necessary condition of it, he needs three, and so on.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a correction of the logicians' error regarding the fixed number of premises. Ibn Taymiyya states the correct view held by the majority of Muslim and non-Muslim investigators: a proof may depend on one premise, two premises, three, or more. He justifies this by explaining that only unknown conclusions need proof. The proof is what entails the conclusion. If the conclusion is a malzum (that which implies something else), and its lazim (implicatum) is known, then proving the lazim suffices to prove the malzum. For example, if we want to prove that something is haram (the malzum), and we know that it is khamr (the lazim), and we know the entailment ('all khamr is haram'), one premise ('this is khamr') plus the known entailment (which is already known) suffices; actually the example shows that the number depends on how many steps of implication are unknown. If the inferrer knows only the malzum of the malzum of the conclusion, he needs two premises to connect them; if only the malzum of malzum of malzum, he needs three, and so on. Thus the number of premises is flexible, not fixed at two. Ibn Taymiyya uses the terminology of malzum (necessity) and lazim (necessary consequence) to explain the logical structure. The commentator should emphasize that this is a direct attack on the logicians' dogma of exactly two premises.

Key Terms
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, how many premises may a proof require?
A) Always exactly two
B) Always exactly three
C) One, two, three, or more depending on the logical entailment
D) Never more than two

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the fixed two-premise dogma by showing that premise count depends on the chain of entailment, making proof structure flexible and intuitively aligned with Islamic reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a chain of malzum and lazim to prove that a specific act (e.g., smoking) is haram using the framework: first, identify the immediate entailment (e.g., smoking harms the body), then the intermediate entailments if the known premise is further removed. Determine how many premises are needed given your starting knowledge.
?
Take a classical theological proof (e.g., the existence of God from the temporality of the world) and identify the number of premises used. According to Ibn Taymiyya's model, could this proof be simplified to one premise if the entailment is directly known? Explain.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Sense Perceptions as Universal Premises
Ibn Taymiyya critiques sense perceptions as sources of universal premises for demonstrations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of sense perceptions as a source of universal premises and evaluate its force against the logicians' claim.

Core Idea
Sense perceptions yield particulars, not universals; universal claims like 'fire burns' rest on habit and analogy, not certainty.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets a foundational assumption of Aristotelian logic: that every syllogism requires a universal premise, and that sense perceptions (al-hissiyāt) can provide such premises. He argues that sense experience is inherently particular and cannot yield the universality needed for demonstrative certainty. By deconstructing the common example of 'fire burns,' he exposes the reliance on habit, analogy, and incomplete induction—all of which fall short of certainty. This lesson guides you through his argument, step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
Ibn Taymiyya states that logicians list sense perceptions among the materials of certainties (اليقينيات). They believe sense perceptions can provide universal premises for demonstrative syllogisms.
The logicians' claim is that sense perceptions yield certain universal propositions.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's core objection
He argues that sense perception never perceives anything universal; it only perceives concrete particulars. Therefore, no universal premise can be derived directly from sense experience.
The core insight: 'الحس لا يدرك أمراً كليا عاماً أصلاً' (sense does not perceive anything universal and general at all).
3
Deconstruct the example of fire
The logicians give the example 'every fire burns.' Ibn Taymiyya shows that this universal claim is not based on sense alone but on habit and analogical reasoning (قياس التمثيل). He points out that fire does not burn everything (e.g., salamander, ruby, coated bodies), so the claim is false unless the recipient is susceptible.
This step exposes the empirical weakness of the universal claim.
4
Analyze the rationalist counter-argument
Some logicians argue that the soul, upon seeing particulars, receives a universal from the Active Intellect. Ibn Taymiyya responds that this is also a form of analogical reasoning; the universality is not trustworthy unless the common denominator is known, which would require prior knowledge of all particulars, making the argument circular.
This refutes the rationalist attempt to salvage universal premises from sense experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا كان لا بد في كل قياس من قضية كلية فنقول : المواد اليقينيات قد حصروها في الأصناف المعروفة عندهم . أحدها الحسّيات. ومعلوم أن الحس لا يدرك أمراً كليا عاماً أصلاً . فليس في الأول: الحسيات المجرَّدة قضية كلية عامة تصلح أن تكون مقدمةً في «البرهان اليقيني». وإذا مثلوا ذلك بـ أن النار تُحرق ونحو ذلك لم يكن لهم علم بعموم هذه القضية، وإنما التجربة والعادة التي هي من جنس قياس التمثيل لما يعلمونه من الحكم الكلي، لا فرق بينه وبين قياس الشمول» و «قياس التمثيل». وإن عُلم ذلك بواسطة اشتمال النار على قوة مُحْرِقة، فالعلم بأن كل نار لا بد فيها من هذه القوة هو أيضاً حكم كلي . وإن قيل : إن الصورة النارية لا بد أن تشتمل على هذه القوة، وأن ما لا قوة فيه ليس بنار، فهذا الكلام إذا قيل : إنه صحيح، قيل : إنه لا يفيد الجزم بأن كل ما فيه هذه القوة يحرقُ كلَّ ما لاقاه، وإن كان هو الغالب. فهذا يشترك فيه قياس التمثيل، والشمول، والعادة، والاستقراء الناقص. ومعلوم أن كل من قال «إن كل نار تَحْرِقُ كلَّ ما لاقته فقد أخطأ. فإنه لا بد من كون المحل قابلاً للإحراق، إذ قد علم أنها لا تحرق كل شيء، كما لا تحرق السَّمَندَل، والياقوت ؛ وكما لا تحرق الأجسام المطلية بأمور مصبوغة . وأما خرقُ العادة فمقام آخر . وليس في القضايا الحسية كلية لا يمكن نقضُها مع أن القضية الكلية ليست في الحسيات . وأما الحكم العقلي فيقولون إن النفس عند رؤيتها هذه المعيَّنات تستعد لأن تفيض عليها قضية كلية بالعموم. ومعلوم أن هذا من جنس قياس التمثيل»، ولا يوثق بعمومه إن لم يُعلم أن الحكم العام لازم للقدر المشترك. وهذا إذا عُلم علم في جميع المعينات، فلم يكن العلم بالمعيَّنات موقوفاً على هذا.
If it is necessary in every syllogism to have a universal premise, we say: the materials of certainties they have enumerated in the known categories among them. One of them is sense perceptions. It is known that sense does not perceive anything universal and general at all. So there is not in the first [category], the abstract sense perceptions, a universal general premise that is suitable to be a premise in the demonstrative syllogism. And if they illustrate that with the example that fire burns and so on, they have no knowledge of the universality of this proposition. Rather, experience and habit, which are of the genus of analogical reasoning, [are] what they know of the universal judgment. There is no difference between it and the syllogism of inclusion and analogical reasoning. And if that is known by means of fire containing a burning power, then the knowledge that every fire must contain this power is also a universal judgment. And if it is said that the form of fire must contain this power, and that whatever does not have this power is not fire, then this statement, if it is said to be correct, it is said that it does not give certainty that everything that has this power burns everything it meets, even if it is the predominant. So this is shared by analogical reasoning, inclusion, habit, and incomplete induction. And it is known that everyone who says 'Every fire burns everything it meets' is wrong. For the place must be susceptible to burning, since it is known that it does not burn everything, just as it does not burn salamander and ruby; and as it does not burn bodies coated with dyed things. And breaking the custom is another matter. And there is not among sense propositions a universal that cannot be refuted, while the universal proposition is not in sense perceptions. As for the rational judgment, they say that the soul, when seeing these particular things, is prepared that a universal proposition flows upon it by universality. It is known that this is of the genus of analogical reasoning, and its universality is not trusted unless it is known that the universal judgment is necessary for the common denominator. And if this is known, it is known in all particulars, so the knowledge of the particulars is not dependent on this.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a critique by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that every syllogism requires a universal premise. He focuses on the category of sense perceptions (al-hissiyat) which they list among the materials of certainties. Ibn Taymiyya argues that sense perception never grasps universals; it only perceives particulars. Therefore, no universal premise can be derived solely from sense experience. When logicians give examples like 'fire burns,' they assume a universal judgment, but that judgment is actually based on experience and habit, which are analogous to analogical reasoning (tamthil) or incomplete induction. He further deconstructs the example: the statement 'every fire burns everything it meets' is false because fire does not burn certain things like salamander or ruby, and burning depends on the recipient's susceptibility. Thus, such universal claims are not certain. He also addresses the rationalist explanation that the soul receives a universal from particulars; Ibn Taymiyya responds that this is also a form of analogical reasoning and is unreliable unless the universal is necessarily tied to a common denominator — but that would require prior knowledge of all particulars, which defeats the purpose. The passage shows that the logicians' foundation for certain knowledge is flawed, as their supposedly certain premises are not truly universal or certain.

Key Terms
حسيات
قضية كلية
قياس التمثيل
الاستقراء الناقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that sense perceptions cannot ground universal premises because they are inherently particular, and universal claims like 'fire burns' are based on habit and analogy, not certainty.

Critique of External Abstract Entities
Historical overview and refutation of abstract entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical error of reifying mental abstractions and trace how Ibn Taymiyya refutes each historical school's version of external universals.

Core Idea
Mental concepts do not float outside the mind—confusing the two leads to philosophical dead ends.

Ibn Taymiyya here takes you on a tour of how Greek philosophy went wrong. Starting from a simple observation—that our minds grasp commonalities—philosophers mistakenly projected those mental concepts into the external world. This lesson will walk you through each school's error and show why Ibn Taymiyya insists that universals exist only in the mind, not as separate entities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seed of error
Notice the origin: philosophers observed natural bodies and perceived a 'common universal aspect' (القَدْرِ المُشْتَرَكِ الكُلِّي). They then assumed that this mental abstraction has a counterpart in external reality. This is the basic logical mistake.
The error is a confusion between mental and extramental existence.
2
Trace Pythagoras and Plato's positions
Pythagoras posited abstract numbers; Plato posited universal quiddities (e.g., universal man, universal horse) as eternal, external entities. Ibn Taymiyya groups these as 'intellectual abstracts' that have no external existence.
3
Analyze Aristotle's partial refutation
Aristotle rejected Plato's separate Forms but still maintained that universals exist 'concomitant with individuals' in external reality. Ibn Taymiyya includes Aristotle among those who erred, though the error is subtler.
Aristotle's error: he distinguished between a thing and its universal quiddity but still placed the quiddity externally.
4
Consider the later elaborations
Ibn Taymiyya mentions Avicenna's distinction between a thing and its quiddity, and the theory of ten intellects and celestial souls. He dismisses the proofs as extremely corrupt, showing that later philosophers compounded the initial error.
The 'ten intellects' and 'celestial souls' are further examples of unwarranted reification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن ما يدعون ثبوته في الخارج من المجردات العقلية» لا يثبت ـ على السبر العقلي - له تحقق إلا في الذهن. وهذا كان مبدأ فلسفة هؤلاء فإنهم نظروا في الأجسام الطبيعية، فعلموا القدر المشترك الكلي، فصاروا يظنون ثبوته في الخارج. فكان أولهم فيثاغورس وشيعته أثبتوا أعداداً مجرَّدة في الخارج. ثم رَدَّ ذلك عليهم أفلاطن وشيعته، وأثبتوا ماهيات كلية مجردة، مثل: إنسان كلي و فرس كلي - أزلي أبدى خارج الذهن. وأثبتوا أيضاً زماناً مجرّداً سموه الدهر»، وأثبتوا مكاناً مجرّداً سموه «الخَلاء»، وأثبتوا مادة مجرّدة عن الصُّوَر ، وهي المادة الأولى» و «الهيولى الأولى» عندهم. فجاء أرسطو وشيعته، فردُّوا ذلك كله عليهم، ولكن أثبتوا هذه «المجردات» في الخارج مقارنةً للأعيان وفرَّقوا بين الشيء الموجود في الخارج، وبين ماهيته الكلية المقارنة لأفرادها في الخارج، كما ذكر ذلك ابن سينا وأمثاله. وغلط هؤلاء في هذا. وكذلك أثبتوا العقول العشرة»، وظنوا وجودها في الخارج وهم غالطون في ذلك، وأدلتهم عليها في غاية الفساد. وأما [٢٩٥] النفوس الفلكية فكان قدماؤهم يجعلونها أعراضاً، لكن ابن سينا وطائفة رجّحوا أنها جواهر قائمة بنفسها كنفس الإنسان. وهذا لبسطه موضع آخر.
But what they claim to exist externally of the intellectual abstracts is not established—upon intellectual investigation—it only has actualization in the mind. This was the starting point of the philosophy of these people: they looked at natural bodies and knew the common universal aspect, so they began to assume its existence externally. The first among them was Pythagoras and his followers; they affirmed abstract numbers externally. Then Plato and his followers refuted them and affirmed universal abstract quiddities, such as universal man and universal horse—eternal and perpetual outside the mind. They also affirmed abstract time, which they called 'al-dahr', and abstract place, which they called 'al-khalā'', and abstract matter devoid of forms, which is the 'prime matter' and 'prime hyle' with them. Then Aristotle and his followers came and refuted all that against them, but they affirmed these 'abstracts' externally concomitant with individuals, and they distinguished between a thing existing externally and its universal quiddity concomitant with its individuals externally, as Avicenna and his likes mentioned. These people erred in this. Likewise, they affirmed the 'ten intellects' and assumed their existence externally, and they are mistaken in that, and their proofs for them are extremely corrupt. As for the celestial souls, their ancients made them accidents, but Avicenna and a group favored that they are self-subsisting substances like the human soul. This is expanded elsewhere.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a critical historical overview by Ibn Taymiyya of the development of the concept of abstract entities (mujarradāt) in philosophy. He begins by asserting that what the philosophers claim to exist externally as intellectual abstracts has no actuality except in the mind, based on intellectual investigation (السبر العقلي). He then explains the origin of this error: philosophers observed natural bodies, perceived a common universal aspect (القدر المشترك الكلي), and wrongly assumed that this universal exists externally. Ibn Taymiyya traces the schools: Pythagoras posited abstract numbers; Plato posited universal quiddities (like universal man and horse) that are eternal and external; they also posited abstract time (al-dahr) and abstract space (al-khalā') and prime matter. Aristotle and his followers refuted Plato's ideas but still maintained that abstracts exist externally 'concomitant with individuals' (مقارنة للأعيان)—that is, as universals inherent in particular objects. Ibn Taymiyya calls this a mistake. He then mentions the theory of the ten intellects and celestial souls, criticizing the proofs as extremely corrupt (في غاية الفساد). On celestial souls, he notes that earlier philosophers considered them accidents, but Avicenna treated them as substances like the human soul, though this is not elaborated here.

ماهيات كلية مجردة
الدهر
الخلاء
الهيولى الأولى
العقول العشرة

D) Avicenna

Key Takeaway
The fundamental error of the philosophers is mistaking mental abstractions for independent external realities, a mistake that Ibn Taymiyya refutes by tracing its historical development and exposing its lack of proof.
