Why Particulars Precede Universals in Knowledge
Epistemological critique: particular instances are known before and more strongly than universal premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that knowledge of particulars is prior to knowledge of universals and evaluate its epistemological implications.

Core Idea
Knowledge of particulars is more primordial and certain than knowledge of universals.

This passage presents one of Ibn Taymiyya's most incisive critiques of Aristotelian logic. He challenges the view that universal premises are the foundation of certain knowledge by showing that particulars are known first and more directly. Understanding this argument is key to grasping his broader critique of syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Logicians assert that universal propositions are necessary for syllogism and that knowledge of particulars is derived from them. Find where Ibn Taymiyya states this claim in the text.
He refers to 'the particular intended to be known through universal propositions'.
2
Follow the regress argument
Ibn Taymiyya argues that universal propositions themselves must ultimately be known without syllogism, otherwise infinite regress. Locate this conditional reasoning in the Arabic.
Look for 'otherwise infinite regress or circularity would occur'.
3
Examine the fitra examples
He gives examples like 'this one is half of these two' vs. 'every one is half of every two'. Notice how he claims the particular knowledge is more primordial.
The examples show that specific instances are known before the universal.
4
Assess the conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that syllogism yields little benefit for sound fitra. Consider what this implies about the role of syllogism in acquiring knowledge.
He says syllogism is only useful for the mistaken or obstinate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: إن المعيّن المطلوب علمه بالقضايا الكلية يُعلم قبلها وبدونها. إذا كان لا بد في كل قياس من قضية كلية فتلك القضية الكلية لا بد أن تنتهي إلى أن تُعْلَم بغير قياس، وإلا لزم الدور والتسلسل. فإذا كان لا بد أن تكون لهم قضايا كلية معلومة بغير قياس فنقول: ليس في الموجودات ما تعلم الفطرة له قضية كلية بغير قياس إلا وعلمها العلم بالمعين بالمفردات المعيّنة من تلك القضية الكلية أقوى من علمها بتلك القضية الكلية. مثل قولنا: الواحد نصف الاثنين، و الجسم لا يكون في مكانين، و «الضدان لا يجتمعان». فإن العلم بأن هذا الواحد نصفُ هذين الاثنين، أقدمُ في الفطرة من العلم بأن كل واحد نصفُ كل اثنين. وهكذا كل ما يُفرَض من الآحاد. فيقال: المقصود بهذه القضايا الكلية إما أن يكون العلم بالموجود الخارجي، أو لا يحصل العلم بالمقدّرات الذهنية. أما الثاني ففائدته قليلة. وأما الأول فما من موجود معين إلا وحُكْمُه بعِلم تعينه أظهرُ وأقوى من العلم به عن قياس كلي يتناوله. فلا يحصل بالقياس كثير فائدة، بل يكون ذلك تطويلاً. وإنما يُستعمل القياس في مثل ذلك لأجل الغالط والمعاند فيضرب له المثل وتذكر الكلية ردّاً لغلطه أو عناده، بخلاف مَنْ كان سليم الفطرة.
The second aspect: The particular intended to be known through universal propositions is known before them and without them. If in every syllogism a universal proposition is necessary, then that universal proposition must ultimately be known without a syllogism, otherwise infinite regress or circularity would occur. Therefore, if they must have universal propositions known without syllogism, we say: There is nothing among existing things whose innate disposition (fitra) knows a universal proposition without syllogism except that its knowledge of the particular, the specific individuals of that universal proposition, is stronger than its knowledge of that universal proposition. Like our statement: 'The one is half of two', and 'A body cannot be in two places', and 'Opposites cannot coexist'. For the knowledge that this one is half of these two is more primordial in the fitra than the knowledge that every one is half of every two. And similarly for every individual that can be conceived. So it is said: The purpose of these universal propositions is either knowledge of external existence, or knowledge of mental estimations. The second has little benefit. As for the first, there is no specific existent except that its ruling by knowledge of its specificity is more apparent and stronger than knowledge of it through a universal syllogism that includes it. Thus, syllogism yields little benefit; rather, it is a prolongation. Syllogism is only used in such cases for the mistaken or the obstinate, so an example is presented and the universal is mentioned as a refutation of his error or obstinacy, contrary to one of sound innate disposition.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological critique of the claim that knowledge of particulars is derived from universal propositions via syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya argues that universal premises themselves require non-inferential justification (otherwise infinite regress). Then he claims that innate human nature (fitra) knows particulars before and more strongly than universals. He gives examples: the knowledge that 'this one is half of these two' is prior to the knowledge that 'every one is half of every two'. Similarly for 'a body cannot be in two places' and 'opposites cannot coexist'. He then distinguishes two purposes of universal propositions: either they refer to external existents or to mental concepts. For external existents, knowledge of the specific instance is always stronger and more apparent than knowledge derived via a universal syllogism. Therefore, syllogism offers little benefit and is only useful for correcting the mistaken or refuting the obstinate, not for a person of sound natural disposition. This is a direct challenge to the Aristotelian claim that syllogism is the primary route to certain knowledge.

Key Terms
معين
قضية كلية
فطرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the relationship between knowledge of a particular (e.g., 'this one is half of these two') and knowledge of the universal (e.g., 'every one is half of every two')?
A) The universal knowledge is prior and causes the particular knowledge.
B) The particular knowledge is more primordial and stronger, and the universal is known through it.
C) Both are equally primordial and independent.
D) Universal knowledge is impossible without syllogism.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's argument shows that universal propositions are not the foundation of certain knowledge; particulars are known more directly and strongly, limiting syllogism's role to dialectical correction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a universal proposition used in a qiyas (analogy) from fiqh (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'). Then find a specific instance (e.g., 'This specific nabidh is an intoxicant') and explain, following Ibn Taymiyya, which knowledge is prior and why.
?
Critically evaluate: Ibn Taymiyya says syllogism is only useful for the mistaken or obstinate. Give an example of a situation in Islamic theology where a universal syllogism might be used to refute an opponent's error.

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Limited Utility of Syllogism
Epistemological critique: syllogism benefits only the mistaken or obstinate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's claim that syllogism provides little benefit for a person of sound fitra and identify the only context where it is useful.

Core Idea
Syllogism is only useful for refuting error, not for acquiring sound knowledge.

In the same passage, Ibn Taymiyya draws a practical conclusion from his epistemological argument: syllogism is not a tool for discovery but for correction. Understanding this distinction helps clarify his overall critique of logic and its proper place in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two purposes of universals
Ibn Taymiyya says the purpose of universal propositions is either knowledge of external existents or mental estimations. Find this distinction in the Arabic text.
He calls the second 'little benefit'.
2
Analyze the case of external existents
For external things, he claims knowledge of the specific instance is always stronger than knowledge through a universal syllogism. Identify why he thinks syllogism is prolongation.
He says 'its ruling by knowledge of its specificity is more apparent and stronger'.
3
Identify the only legitimate use
He states syllogism is used 'for the mistaken or the obstinate'. Note the contrast with the person of sound fitra.
This limits syllogism to a dialectical, corrective role.

الوجه الثاني: إن المعيّن المطلوب علمه بالقضايا الكلية يُعلم قبلها وبدونها. إذا كان لا بد في كل قياس من قضية كلية فتلك القضية الكلية لا بد أن تنتهي إلى أن تُعْلَم بغير قياس، وإلا لزم الدور والتسلسل. فإذا كان لا بد أن تكون لهم قضايا كلية معلومة بغير قياس فنقول: ليس في الموجودات ما تعلم الفطرة له قضية كلية بغير قياس إلا وعلمها العلم بالمعين بالمفردات المعيّنة من تلك القضية الكلية أقوى من علمها بتلك القضية الكلية. مثل قولنا: الواحد نصف الاثنين، و الجسم لا يكون في مكانين، و «الضدان لا يجتمعان». فإن العلم بأن هذا الواحد نصفُ هذين الاثنين، أقدمُ في الفطرة من العلم بأن كل واحد نصفُ كل اثنين. وهكذا كل ما يُفرَض من الآحاد. فيقال: المقصود بهذه القضايا الكلية إما أن يكون العلم بالموجود الخارجي، أو لا يحصل العلم بالمقدّرات الذهنية. أما الثاني ففائدته قليلة. وأما الأول فما من موجود معين إلا وحُكْمُه بعِلم تعينه أظهرُ وأقوى من العلم به عن قياس كلي يتناوله. فلا يحصل بالقياس كثير فائدة، بل يكون ذلك تطويلاً. وإنما يُستعمل القياس في مثل ذلك لأجل الغالط والمعاند فيضرب له المثل وتذكر الكلية ردّاً لغلطه أو عناده، بخلاف مَنْ كان سليم الفطرة.
The second aspect: The particular intended to be known through universal propositions is known before them and without them. If in every syllogism a universal proposition is necessary, then that universal proposition must ultimately be known without a syllogism, otherwise infinite regress or circularity would occur. Therefore, if they must have universal propositions known without syllogism, we say: There is nothing among existing things whose innate disposition (fitra) knows a universal proposition without syllogism except that its knowledge of the particular, the specific individuals of that universal proposition, is stronger than its knowledge of that universal proposition. Like our statement: 'The one is half of two', and 'A body cannot be in two places', and 'Opposites cannot coexist'. For the knowledge that this one is half of these two is more primordial in the fitra than the knowledge that every one is half of every two. And similarly for every individual that can be conceived. So it is said: The purpose of these universal propositions is either knowledge of external existence, or knowledge of mental estimations. The second has little benefit. As for the first, there is no specific existent except that its ruling by knowledge of its specificity is more apparent and stronger than knowledge of it through a universal syllogism that includes it. Thus, syllogism yields little benefit; rather, it is a prolongation. Syllogism is only used in such cases for the mistaken or the obstinate, so an example is presented and the universal is mentioned as a refutation of his error or obstinacy, contrary to one of sound innate disposition.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Epistemological Priority of Particular Over Universal
Ibn Taymiyya argues that knowledge of particular instances is prior to and stronger than universal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that knowledge of particular instances is prior to and stronger than universal propositions, and evaluate its implications for syllogistic reasoning.

Core Idea
Particular knowledge is epistemologically prior to universal knowledge; syllogisms rest on universals already known by fitra.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the foundational role of universal propositions in syllogistic reasoning. He argues that the universal premises needed for syllogisms must themselves be known without syllogism, otherwise we face circularity or infinite regress. Moreover, he claims that for any such universal, our knowledge of its particular instances is actually stronger and more immediate. In this lesson, we will dissect his argument and see how it questions the utility of syllogisms for acquiring new knowledge of external existents.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's starting point
He assumes every syllogism requires a universal proposition. Then he asks how that universal proposition itself is known.
2
Locate his objection to the universal's origin
He argues that the universal must ultimately be known without a syllogism; otherwise there is circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul).
3
Extract his core claim about particular knowledge
He asserts that for every naturally known universal, knowledge of its particular instances is prior (aqdam) and stronger (aqwa) in the natural disposition (fitra).
4
Analyse his examples
He gives examples: 'one is half of two', 'a body cannot be in two places', 'opposites do not coexist'. For each, the particular instance is known first and more directly.
5
Determine the implications for syllogism's purpose
He concludes that syllogisms are only useful for refuting the obstinate or mistaken, not for obtaining genuine knowledge for the sound intellect. External existents are known directly without syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني أن يقال : إذا كان لا بد في كل قياس من قضية كلية فتلك القضية الكلية لا بد أن تنتهي إلى أن تُعْلَم بغير قياس، وإلا لزم الدور والتسلسل . فإذا كان لا بد أن تكون لهم قضايا كلية معلومة بغير قياس فنقول : ليس في الموجودات ما تعلم الفِطرةُ له قضية كلية بغير قياس إلا وعلمها العلم بالمعين بالمفردات المعيَّنة من تلك القضية الكلية أقوى من علمها بتلك القضية الكلية . مثل قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين و الجسم لا يكون في مكانين و «الضدان لا يجتمعان» . فإن العلم بأن هذا الواحد نصفُ هذين الاثنين، أقدمُ في الفِطرة من العلم بأن كل واحد نصفُ كل اثنين». وهكذا كل ما يُفرَض من الآحاد . فيقال : المقصود بهذه القضايا الكلية إما أن يكون العلم بالموجود الخارجي، أو لا يحصل العلم بالمقدّرات الذهنية. أما الثاني ففائدته قليلة وأما الأول فما من موجود معين إلا وحُكْمُه بعِلم تعينه أظهرُ وأقوى من العلم به عن قياس كلي يتناوله. فلا يحصل بالقياس كثير فائدة، بل يكون ذلك تطويلاً وإنما يُستعمل القياس في مثل ذلك لأجل الغالط والمعاند فيضرب له المثل وتذكر الكلية ردّاً لغلطه أو عناده، بخلاف مَنْ كان سليم الفطرة. وكذلك قولهم : الضدان لا يجتمعان». فأي شيئين عُلم تضادهما فإنه يعلم أنهما لا يجتمعان قبل استحضار قضية كلية بأن كل ضدين لا يجتمعان». وما من جسم معين إلا ويعلم أنه لا يكون في مكانين قبل العلم بأن كل جسم لا يكون في مكانين». وكذلك ولهم : النقيضان لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان. فإن مرادهم بذلك أن وجودَ الشيء وعدمه لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان فما من شيء معيَّن إلا ويُعلم أنه لا يكون موجوداً معدوماً، وأنه لا يخلو عن الوجود والعدم، قبل العلم بهذه القضية العامة. وأمثال ذلك كثيرة لمن تدبره . ويُعلم أن المعين المطلوب علمه بهذه القضايا الكلية الأولية يُعلم قبل أن تعلم هذه القضية الكلية، ويُعلم بدونها، ولا يحتاج العلم به إلى القضية الكلية. وإنما يعلم بالقضية الكلية ما يقدَّر في الذهن من أمثال ذلك مما لم يوجد في الخارج. وأما الموجودات الخارجية فتعلم بدون هذا القياس.
The second aspect: it is said: If every syllogism necessarily has a universal proposition, then that universal proposition must ultimately be known without a syllogism, otherwise there would be circularity or infinite regress. So if they must have universal propositions known without syllogism, we say: Among existing things, there is nothing for which the natural disposition (fitra) knows a universal proposition without syllogism, except that its knowledge of the particular from the specified individuals of that universal proposition is stronger than its knowledge of that universal proposition. For example, our saying 'one is half of two' and 'a body cannot be in two places' and 'opposites do not coexist'. Indeed, the knowledge that this one is half of these two is more primordial in the natural disposition than the knowledge that every one is half of every two. And likewise for every individual that is assumed. So it is said: The purpose of these universal propositions is either knowledge of external existence, or it does not produce knowledge of mental estimations. As for the second, its benefit is little. As for the first, there is no specific existent whose judgment, by knowledge of its particularity, is not more apparent and stronger than knowledge of it through a universal syllogism that covers it. So the syllogism does not produce much benefit; rather, that is a prolongation. The syllogism is only used in such cases for the sake of the deceiver and the obstinate, by giving an example and mentioning the universal to refute his deception or obstinacy, unlike one of sound nature. And similarly their saying 'opposites do not coexist.' For any two things whose opposition is known, it is known that they do not coexist before invoking the universal proposition that every two opposites do not coexist. And there is no specific body except that it is known that it cannot be in two places before knowing that every body cannot be in two places. And similarly their saying 'contradictories do not coexist and do not cease to exist.' For they mean by that that the existence of a thing and its non-existence do not coexist and do not rise together. So there is no specific thing except that it is known that it cannot be both existent and non-existent, and that it is not free from existence and non-existence, before knowing this universal proposition. And similar examples are many for one who contemplates. And it is known that the specific thing whose knowledge is sought by these primary universal propositions is known before knowing this universal proposition, and is known without it, and does not need the universal proposition for its knowledge. The universal proposition only gives knowledge of what is mentally estimated from such things that do not exist externally. As for external existents, they are known without this syllogism.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's second argument against the logicians' reliance on syllogism for knowledge. He begins by asserting that every syllogism requires a universal proposition, but that universal proposition itself must be known without a syllogism, otherwise we fall into circular reasoning or infinite regress. Therefore, there must be universal propositions that are known 'by nature' (fitra). Then he makes his central claim: for every such naturally known universal proposition, the knowledge of its particular instances is actually stronger and more prior in the natural disposition than the knowledge of the universal itself. He gives examples: the proposition 'one is half of two' is known intuitively, but knowing that 'this one is half of these two' is even more immediate. Similarly, 'a body cannot be in two places' and 'opposites do not coexist' are universals, but the knowledge of a specific pair of opposites or a specific body is prior. He then analyses the purpose of universal propositions: they either aim at knowledge of external realities or merely mental constructs. If the latter, their benefit is little. If the former, then for every specific external thing, its judgment is known directly and more clearly than through a syllogism. So syllogism adds no benefit; it only serves to argue against a mistaken or stubborn person by reminding them of the universal. For a person of sound intellect, the particular knowledge suffices. He reinforces this with further examples: any two things known to be opposites are known not to coexist without the universal, any specific body is known not to be in two places without the universal, and any specific thing is known not to be both existent and non-existent without the universal. He concludes that the specific thing sought is known before and without the universal proposition; the universal only covers mental abstractions that do not exist externally. External existents are known directly, without syllogism.

Key Terms
الوجه الثاني
القياس
الفطرة

A) The specific knowledge is derived from the universal by syllogism.
B) The specific knowledge is prior and stronger in the natural disposition.
C) They are equally certain.
D) The universal is known first and the specific is an application.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique exposes that universal propositions in syllogisms are not the ultimate epistemic foundation; particular knowledge by fitra is prior and stronger, limiting the syllogism's role to polemical contexts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the universal proposition 'every intoxicant is prohibited'. Identify a specific instance of an intoxicant (e.g., khamr). Explain, following Ibn Taymiyya, why your knowledge that this specific khamr is prohibited is prior and stronger than the universal rule.
?

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Categorical Syllogism's Independence
Ibn Taymiyya argues that categorical syllogism depends on analogy, undermining logicians' distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma argument against the logicians' separation of categorical and analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
Categorical syllogism collapses into analogical reasoning, undermining the logicians' claim to certainty.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a cornerstone of Aristotelian logic: the categorical syllogism. He argues that this supposedly certain form of inference is actually rooted in analogical reasoning (tamthīl). His argument presents a dilemma that forces logicians to either admit that analogy can yield certainty or lose their claim to a privileged epistemic method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians assert that categorical syllogisms (qiyās shumūl) yield certain knowledge, while analogical reasoning (qiyās tamthīl) is merely probable. Ibn Taymiyya targets this distinction.
2
Extract the dilemma
Ibn Taymiyya presents two options: either analogy is of the same genus as categorical syllogism, meaning both can be certain or probable (battering the distinction), or they are different, in which case certainty in external particulars actually depends on analogy, as the intellect perceives universals only through particular examples.
This is a classic dilemma form: if A, then P; if not A, then Q; but P and Q both lead to the same conclusion.
3
Examine the intellect's role
Ibn Taymiyya explains that the intellect perceives universals by means of analogy from particular instances. Without the particular examples, the mind cannot form universal concepts. This supports his claim that categorical syllogisms, which rely on universal premises, ultimately depend on analogy.
He emphasizes that sense knowledge is particular, while intellect abstracts universals through comparison of particulars.
4
Note the epistemic consequences
If categorical syllogisms depend on analogy, then the logicians cannot claim that categorical reasoning is inherently more certain. Instead, both types fall on a spectrum of certainty determined by the strength of the analogical inference. This erodes the foundational distinction in Aristotelian logic.

قياس الشمول مبناه على قياس التمثيل. وإذا قيل إن من الناس من يعلم بعض الأعيان الخارجية بهذا القياس فيكون مبناه على قياس التمثيل الذي ينكرون أنه يقيني فهم بين أمرين. إن اعترفوا بأن قياس التمثيل من جنس قياس الشمول ينقسم إلى يقيني وظني بطل تفريقهم. وإن ادعوا الفرق بينهما وأن قياس الشمول يكون يقينياً دون التمثيل، منعوا ذلك، وبين لهم أن اليقين لا يحصل في مثل هذه الأمور إلا أن يحصل بالتمثيل، فيكون العلم بما لم يعلم من المفردات الموجودة في الخارج قياساً على ما عُلم منها. وهذا حق لا ينازع فيه عاقل. خاصة العقل بل هذا من أخص صفات العقل التي فارق بها الحس إذا الحس لا يعلم إلا معيناً، والعقل يدركه كلياً مطلقاً، لكن بواسطة التمثيل. ثم العقل يدركها كلها مع إدراك الكليات من الجزئيات عزوب الأمثلة المعينة عنه، لكن هي في الأصل إنما صارت في ذهنه كلية عامة بعد تصوره لأمثال معينة من أفرادها وإذا بعد عهد الذهن بالمفردات المعينة فقد يغلط كثيراً بأن يجعل الحكم إما أعم وإما أخص. وهذا يعرض للناس كثيراً.
The categorical syllogism is based on analogical reasoning. And if it is said that among people there is one who knows some external particulars by this syllogism, then its basis is on analogical reasoning which they deny is certain. So they are between two matters. If they acknowledge that analogical reasoning is of the same genus as categorical syllogism, dividing into certain and probable, their distinction is invalid. And if they claim a difference between them, and that categorical syllogism is certain without analogy, they are prevented from that, and it is made clear to them that certainty is not obtained in such matters except by obtaining through analogy, so the knowledge of what is not known of particular existents externally is by way of analogy to what is known from them. And this is true, which no rational person disputes. Especially the intellect; rather this is among the most specific attributes of the intellect by which it distinguishes from sense, for sense only knows the particular, and the intellect perceives it universally absolutely, but by means of analogy. Then the intellect perceives all of them together with the perception of universals from particulars, the absence of the particular examples from it, but originally they only become general universals in his mind after he conceives of specific examples of individuals. And when the mind's acquaintance with particular individuals becomes distant, it may err greatly by making the judgment either more general or more specific. And this occurs frequently among people.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' separation of categorical syllogism (qiyas shumul) from analogical reasoning (qiyas tamsil). He claims that categorical syllogism is actually based on analogy. He then presents a dilemma: either the logicians admit that analogy is of the same kind as categorical syllogism, which would mean both can be certain or probable, thus invalidating their exclusive claim of certainty for categorical syllogism; or they insist on a distinction, but then they are refuted because certainty in matters of external particulars only comes through analogy. He asserts that this is undeniable by any rational person. He further explains that it is a specific attribute of the intellect to perceive universals, but this is achieved through analogy from particular examples. The universals in the mind originate from conceiving specific instances. When the mind loses familiarity with those particulars, it can make errors such as overgeneralization or under-specification. Thus, Ibn Taymiyya elevates analogical reasoning as the foundation of rational knowledge.

قياس التمثيل
يقيني

Ibn Taymiyya argues that categorical syllogism is ultimately based on analogical reasoning, thereby collapsing the logicians' hierarchical epistemic distinction.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Al-Nawbakhti's Objection to the Two-Premise Rule
Al-Nawbakhti argues that a single premise suffices for valid inference, challenging the Aristotelian rule that a syllogism requires two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Nawbakhti's argument that a single premise suffices for a valid inference and evaluate his critique of the logicians' requirement.

Core Idea
A single premise can prove a conclusion if the missing premise is implicit in the audience's knowledge.

Al-Nawbakhti, a Muslim theologian, challenges the foundational Aristotelian rule that every syllogism requires two explicit premises. He argues that in many cases, a single premise is sufficient because the missing premise is already known or follows directly from the relationship between the particular and the universal. This passage presents a dialectical exchange that questions the very structure of categorical syllogisms and opens the door to a more flexible understanding of logical inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians assert that a syllogism (qiyas) cannot be built from a single premise; two premises are always necessary. Al-Nawbakhti begins by stating this claim in order to refute it.
2
Extract al-Nawbakhti's example
He gives the example of proving that man is a substance by noting that man accepts contraries at different times. This single premise is offered as proof without a second premise stating that everything accepting contraries is a substance.
The example uses a known property of substance from Aristotle's Categories.
3
Understand his reasoning: particular included in universal
Al-Nawbakhti argues that the particular (man) is included in the universal (substance). Proving the property for the particular implicitly proves it for the universal, because whatever is true of the particular's property (accepting contraries) is true of the universal that includes it.
This relies on the principle that the specific entails the general when the general is defined by that specific property.
4
Consider his supplementary examples
He adds everyday analogies: seeing a trace implies an effect, and seeing a writing implies a writer, without needing to state the general rule explicitly. These show that implicit knowledge often suffices.
These examples are meant to appeal to common sense, not formal logic.
5
Examine the logicians' response and al-Nawbakhti's counter-argument
The logicians respond that two premises are necessary, but one can be omitted if the listener knows it. Al-Nawbakhti then counters that even with two explicit premises, the conclusion is already known from the minor premise alone. For instance, in 'Every man is an animal, every animal is a substance', the premise 'Every animal is a substance' already entails 'Every man is a substance' because man is a subset of animal.
This shows that the second premise does not add new logical content; it is redundant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اعترض قوم من متكلمي أهل الإسلام على أوضاع المنطق هذه. وقالوا: أما بيان غلط قول صاحب المنطق إن القياس لا يبني من مقدمة واحدة فغلط. لأن القائل إذا أراد مثلاً أن يدل على أن الإنسان جوهر فقال: استدل على نفس الشيء المطلوب من غير تقديم المقدمتين. وهو أن يقول: إن الدليل على أن الإنسان جوهر أنه يقبل المتضادات في أزمان مختلفة. وليس يحتاج إلى مقدمة ثانية، هي قول القائل: إن كل قابل للمتضادات في أزمان مختلفة فجوهر، لأن دلالته على أن كل قابل للمتضادات في أزمان مختلفة فجوهر هو نفس ما خولف فيه وأراد الدلالة عليه، لأن الخاص داخل في العام، فعلى أيهما دل استغنى عن الآخر. وقد يستدل الإنسان إذا شاهد الأثر على أن له مؤثراً، والكتابة على أن لها كاتباً، من غير أن يحتاج في استدلاله على صحة ذلك إلى المقدمتين. قالوا: لا بد من مقدمتين، فإذا ذكرت إحداهما استغنى بمعرفة المخاطب بالأخرى، فترك ذكرها، لا لأنه مستغن عنها. قلنا: لسنا نجد مقدمتين كليتين يستدل بهما على صحة نتيجة. لأن القائل إذا قال «الجوهر لكل حي» و «الحياة لكل إنسان» فتكون النتيجة أن «الجوهر لكل إنسان». فسواء في العقول قول القائل: «الجوهر لكل حي» وقوله: «لكل إنسان». ذلك أنا إذا قلنا كل إنسان حي، وكل حي جوهر كما يقولون: «كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان جوهر أو جسم فسواء في العقول علمنا بأن «كل إنسان جوهر أو جسم» وعلمنا بأن كل حيوان جوهر أو جسم فمن علم أن كل حيوان جوهر فقد علم أيضاً أن كل إنسان جوهر.
And a group of Muslim theologians objected to these logical conventions. They said: As for clarifying the error of the statement of the author of logic that a syllogism cannot be built from a single premise, it is wrong. Because if one wants to prove, for example, that man is a substance, he says: I infer the very thing sought without presenting the two premises. That is, he says: The proof that man is a substance is that he accepts contraries at different times. And he does not need a second premise, namely, 'Everything that accepts contraries at different times is a substance,' because his indication that everything that accepts contraries at different times is a substance is the very thing that was disputed and he intended to prove it, because the particular is included in the universal; whichever one he indicates, he dispenses with the other. And a person may infer, when he sees a trace, that it has an effect, and a writing, that it has a writer, without needing in his inference for its validity the two premises. They said: Two premises are necessary; if one of them is mentioned, one dispenses with the other by the knowledge of the addressee, so he omits mentioning it, not because he is independent of it. We said: We do not find two universal premises that are used to prove the validity of a conclusion. Because if one says 'Substance belongs to every living thing' and 'Life belongs to every man,' then the result is 'Substance belongs to every man.' So in the minds, the statement 'Substance belongs to every living thing' is the same as 'to every man.' That is because if we say 'Every man is living, and every living thing is substance,' as they say: 'Every man is an animal, and every animal is substance or body,' then in the minds we know that 'Every man is substance or body' and we know that 'Every animal is substance or body'; so whoever knows that every animal is substance also knows that every man is substance.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange between Muslim theologians and the logicians. The theologians argue that a syllogism can indeed be built from one premise, contrary to the logicians' claim. Their first point: to prove 'man is a substance' (jawhar), one can simply say 'man accepts opposites at different times' because the concept 'accepting opposites' is a property of substance. The missing premise 'Everything that accepts opposites is a substance' is implicitly known, as the particular is contained in the universal. They give further examples: seeing a trace implies an effect, and seeing writing implies a writer, without needing to state the general rule explicitly. The logicians respond by saying that two premises are necessary, but one can be omitted if the listener already knows it. The theologians then counter: even with two explicit premises, the conclusion is already known from the minor premise alone. For instance, in 'Every man is an animal, every animal is a substance,' the premise 'Every animal is a substance' already entails 'Every man is a substance' because man is a subset of animal. Therefore, the second premise does not add new knowledge, showing that the syllogism effectively depends on one premise.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nawbakhti's critique undermines the necessity of two explicit premises in categorical syllogisms, suggesting that one premise can suffice when the entailment is clear from the particular-universal relationship.

The Syllogism

Al-Nawbakhti's Critique: Single Premise Suffices
Al-Nawbakhti argues a single premise suffices and challenges logicians to produce two self-evident premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Nawbakhti's argument that a single premise can suffice in a syllogism and evaluate its force against the Aristotelian claim.

Core Idea
Al-Nawbakhti shows that a single premise can suffice because the specific includes the general.

Al-Nawbakhti, a prominent mutakallim, challenges the foundational Aristotelian claim that a syllogism must have two premises. Using examples like 'man accepts opposites' to prove 'man is a substance,' he argues that a single premise suffices because the specific term (man) implies the general (acceptor of opposites). This critique undercuts the necessity of two premises in demonstrative reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Find the exact statement that a syllogism requires two premises. How does al-Nawbakhti report it?
The logicians claim 'al-qiyas la yubna min muqaddima wahida'.
2
Understand al-Nawbakhti's example
How does he prove 'man is a substance' with one premise? What is the single premise?
The single premise is 'man accepts opposites at different times.' The conclusion 'man is a substance' follows because the specific (man) is included in the general (acceptor of opposites).
3
Analyze the reasoning: specific includes general
Explain why the second premise 'every acceptor of opposites is a substance' is unnecessary according to al-Nawbakhti.
The second premise is redundant because the first premise already entails it: if man accepts opposites, and man is a specific instance, then the general rule is already implied.
4
Consider the logicians' counter-reply
How do logicians respond? They say two premises are still needed but one can be known by the addressee. How does al-Nawbakhti refute this?
Al-Nawbakhti argues that in disputed matters, such known premises are not available; if they were self-evident, one would suffice.
5
Evaluate the ultimate challenge
What does al-Nawbakhti demand from logicians? What is the consequence if they cannot produce two self-evident premises?
He demands two primary self-evident premises for any disputed conclusion. Their failure shows that the claim of two necessary premises is invalid.

The fourth station - the second aspect: al-Nawbakhti's speech in refutation of logic. And this refutation of them is mentioned in many books of the people of kalam. But it happened that I saw this. The substance of their claim is built from one premise. The chapter first on al-Nawbakhti's speech. For after he mentioned Aristotle's method in logic, he said: Some of the Muslim mutakallimun objected to these logical postulates. They said: As for clarifying the error of the logician's statement that the syllogism cannot be built from one premise, it is erroneous. Because the speaker, if he intends to indicate, for example, that man is a substance, he says: I deduce the very thing sought without presenting the two premises. He says: The evidence that man is a substance is that he accepts opposites at different times. And he does not need a second premise, which is the statement that every acceptor of opposites at different times is a substance, because its indication that every acceptor of opposites at different times is a substance is the very thing that was disputed and he intended to indicate, because the specific is included in the general; so whichever he indicates, he dispenses with the other. And a person may infer, when he sees the effect, that it has an efficient cause, and writing that it has a writer, without needing two premises in his deduction of the correctness of that. They said: Regarding two premises, we say: Two premises are necessary; if one is mentioned, he dispenses with the other due to the addressee's knowledge, so he omits it, not because he is independent of it. We replied: We do not find two universal premises by which a conclusion is deduced. Because if someone says 'Substance is for every living thing' and 'Life is for every man', then the conclusion is 'Substance is for every man'. So in the intellects, the statement 'Substance is for every living thing' and his statement 'For every man' are equal. That is because if we say 'Every man is living, and every living thing is substance', as they say 'Every man is an animal and every animal is a substance or body', so in the intellects our knowledge that 'Every man is a substance or body' and our knowledge that 'Every animal is a substance or body' are equal; so whoever knows that every animal is a substance also knows that every man is a substance. And his intention is that they do not find two primary self-evident premises by which to deduce anything from the points of dispute that require demonstration; rather, one of them or both must be non-self-evident. And if it is assumed that they are both self-evident, then one suffices, as he mentioned in the example. And if one is assumed theoretical, then it needs to be clarified. And if both are theoretical, then both need to be clarified, as if there were three premises. And what needs clarification is deduced, then deduced from it. And one only deduces initially from what is evident in itself, like self-evidents. He said: And they do not find in scientific inquiries that the required conclusion depends on two premises that are evident in themselves. Rather, if that were the case, one of them would suffice. He said: And we say to them: Show us two primary premises that do not need a preceding proof, by which something disputed is deduced, and the two premises are more acceptable in the intellects than the conclusion. If you do not find that, then your claim is invalid. Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn Musa al-Nawbakhti said: And I asked more than one of their leaders to show me that, but they did not show me.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents al-Nawbakhti's critique of the Aristotelian claim that a syllogism necessarily requires two premises. The author begins by quoting al-Nawbakhti, who reports that some Muslim mutakallimun objected to the logical postulate that a syllogism cannot be built from a single premise. They argue that the logician's statement is erroneous. For example, to prove that 'man is a substance,' one can simply say 'man accepts opposites at different times' without needing the second premise 'every acceptor of opposites is a substance,' because the specific (man) is included in the general (acceptor of opposites), so indicating the specific suffices. Similarly, inferring an effect from its cause or writing from a writer does not require two premises. When logicians respond that two premises are necessary but one may be known by the addressee and thus omitted, al-Nawbakhti counters that in disputed matters, one cannot find two self-evident premises that are more acceptable than the conclusion. If both premises were self-evident, one would suffice; if one is theoretical, it needs proof, leading to an infinite regress. He then challenges logicians to produce two primary premises that need no proof and are more acceptable than the conclusion for any disputed matter, and reports that when he asked their leaders, they could not produce them. This critique effectively undermines the foundational claim that syllogisms must have two premises, showing that in practice, either one premise suffices or the premises themselves are not self-evident, thus the logical system fails to provide certain knowledge in disputed matters.

Key Terms
مقدمة (muqaddima): A premise in a syllogism; al-Nawbakhti questions whether two are always necessary. [Note: Stable term across logical texts, but here its necessity is contested.]
قياس (qiyas): Syllogism; the target of critique, specifically the claim it requires two premises. [Note: In Aristotelian logic, qiyas is strictly categorical syllogism; extension to analogy occurs later.]
بديهية (badihiyya): Self-evident premise; al-Nawbakhti argues such premises are rare for disputed matters. [Note: Stable across the tradition; used to distinguish axiomatic from theoretical knowledge.]
برهان (burhan): Demonstration; the goal of syllogistic reasoning, which al-Nawbakhti claims is unattainable with two self-evident premises. [Note: Standard term for demonstrative syllogism, stable across texts.]

According to al-Nawbakhti, why does a single premise sometimes suffice in a syllogism?
A) Because the second premise is always unknown.
B) Because the specific includes the general, so indicating the specific implies the general.
C) Because the logician intentionally omits the second premise to avoid complexity.
D) Because two premises can never be self-evident.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nawbakhti's critique shows that the necessity of two premises in a syllogism is not absolute; a single premise can suffice when the specific presupposes the general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using al-Nawbakhti's method, construct a single-premise argument to prove that 'the Quran is the word of Allah' based on its inimitability (i'jaz). Explain why the second premise is implied.
?
Imagine a logician claims you need two premises to prove that 'charity (sadaqa) is recommended.' Formulate al-Nawbakhti's response by identifying the specific-in-general relationship.

Al-Nawbakhti's Rejection of the Second and Third Figures
Al-Nawbakhti dismisses the second and third figures as reducible to the first and thus redundant.

Learning Objective
You will be able to reconstruct al-Nawbakhti's critique of the second and third syllogistic figures and explain why he considers them unnecessary.

Core Idea
Reducibility to the first figure makes the second and third figures redundant—and the first figure is itself useless.

In this passage, al-Nawbakhti continues his assault on Aristotelian logic by targeting the second and third figures of the categorical syllogism. He argues that these figures are redundant because they must be converted to the first figure to be valid. Since he has already shown the first figure to be useless for disputed matters, the derivative figures are even more worthless.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doctrine under attack
Al-Nawbakhti targets the logicians' claim that the second and third figures are necessary independent figures of the categorical syllogism. Read the passage to pinpoint the exact claim he is rejecting.
2
Follow the reduction argument
He argues that these figures are valid only by converting their premises to return to the first figure. Understand the methods of reduction: qiyas al-khalf (syllogism of contradiction) and immediate inferences (converse, contradictory, etc.).
Conversion (qalb) changes the order of premises or terms.
3
Connect to the earlier critique
Al-Nawbakhti references that the first figure itself lacks benefit (from the previous lesson). Since the second and third figures depend on the first, they are even more useless. Identify how he links this.
This is a cumulative argument: the foundation is flawed, so its branches are even worse.
4
Assess the overall thrust
The passage concludes by praising the Islamic community for discerning falsehood. Recognize that al-Nawbakhti's critique is epistemological: Greek logic is unnecessary because it does not align with the truth-seeking methods of the ummah.
This is typical of the anti-logic tradition in Islam.

والمقصود هنا أن هذه الأمة - والله الحمد - لم يَزَل فيها من يَتَفَطَّنُ لِمَا في كلام أهل الباطل من الباطل، ويردُّه . وهم لما هداهم الله به يتوافقون في قبول الحق وردّ
He said: As for what he mentioned after that of the remaining two figures, they are not used as he built them. And if they are valid by converting their premises so that they return to the first figure, then the discussion about the first figure is the discussion about them. And he mentioned another speech not in this place. And his intention is that the other figures only produce conclusions by reduction to the first figure, either by syllogism of contradiction (qiyas al-khalf) which involves proving something by negating its opposite, or by means of the judgment of the contradictory of a proposition, or its converse, or the converse of its contradictory. So the explanation of the figures and their conclusions is that there is no need for them. For the first figure can be used for all materials with difficulty and hardship, be they affirmative or negative, universal or particular. And you have known the absence of its benefit, so the absence of benefit of its branches, which do not benefit except by reduction to it, is even more appropriate and fitting. And the intention here is that this ummah - praise be to Allah - has not ceased to have among it those who discern the falsehood in the speech of the people of falsehood and refute it. And they, by Allah's guidance, agree in accepting truth and rejecting.

Commentary
In this passage, al-Nawbakhti continues his critique by addressing the second and third figures of the syllogism. He asserts that these two figures are not used as they were originally constructed. Even if they are valid, they only work by converting their premises to return to the first figure. Therefore, any discussion about these figures is actually a discussion about the first figure. He mentions that reduction occurs either through a syllogism of contradiction (qiyas al-khalf), which proves something by negating its opposite, or through operations on the contradictory, converse, or converse of the contradictory of a proposition. Consequently, explaining these figures and their conclusions shows that they are unnecessary. The first figure itself can handle all materials—affirmative, negative, universal, and particular—though with difficulty. Since the author has already shown that the first figure lacks benefit (in the previous lesson), its branches, which depend on reduction to it, are even more worthless. The passage ends by praising the Islamic community for having people who discern falsehood and refute it, and that they agree on truth by Allah's guidance.

The Syllogism

6 min
Reduction of the Second and Third Figures to the First Figure
Author argues other syllogistic figures are reducible and thus unnecessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the process of reducing second and third figure syllogisms to the first figure and evaluate the author's claim that they are unnecessary.

Core Idea
The second and third figures only produce valid conclusions by reduction to the first figure, exposing their redundancy.

In this passage, the author critiques the utility of the second and third categorical syllogistic figures. He asserts that these figures are not independent; they only yield valid conclusions when their premises are converted or transformed to fit the first figure. Understanding this reduction process is key to seeing why he deems the other figures superfluous and why the first figure alone suffices for all logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two remaining figures
Recall that the categorical syllogism has four figures based on the position of the middle term. The first figure has the middle term as subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor. The second figure has the middle term as predicate in both premises. The third figure has the middle term as subject in both. The author calls these 'the two remaining figures' after the first.
The fourth figure is often omitted or considered as a special case.
2
Understand the reduction methods
The author lists three methods: qiyas al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum), simple conversion (ʿaks mustawī), and contrapositive (ʿaks naqīḍ). For example, a second figure syllogism 'No A is B; All C is B; therefore No C is A' can be reduced to first figure by converting the major premise to 'No B is A' and using that as minor in a first figure Celarent.
Conversion swaps subject and predicate; contrapositive swaps and negates.
3
Apply reduction to an Islamic example
Consider a second figure syllogism: 'No mubah is wajib (invalid act is not obligatory); Every prayer is wajib (obligatory); therefore No prayer is mubah (not invalid)'. This is valid second figure (Camestres). To reduce to first figure: convert the major 'No mubah is wajib' to 'No wajib is mubah' (simple conversion). Then we have: 'Every prayer is wajib; No wajib is mubah; therefore No prayer is mubah'. This is first figure Celarent.
Always check that the reduction preserves validity.
4
Assess the author's claim of redundancy
Since every valid syllogism in the second and third figures can be reduced to a valid first figure syllogism, the first figure alone is sufficient for proving any conclusion. The author argues that if the first figure is already problematic (as he claims earlier), then the others, which depend on it, are even more pointless.
The author's overall critique aims to show the entire syllogistic apparatus is unnecessary.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رد الأشكال قال: وأما ما ذكره بعد ذلك من الشكلين الباقيين فهما غير مستعملين على ما إلى الشكل الأول بناهما عليه. وإذا كانا يصحان بقلب مقدماتهما حتى يعودا إلى الشكل الأول، الأول رد العلماء عليهم من غير تواطؤ بينهم فالكلام حينئذ في الشكل الأول هو الكلام فيهما. وذكر كلاماً آخر ليس هذا موضعه. ومقصوده أن سائر الأشكال إنما تنتج بالرد إلى الشكل الأول، إما بقياس الخلف الذي يتضمن إثبات الشيء بإبطال نقيضه، وإما بواسطة حكم نقيض القضية أو عكسها المستوي أو عكس نقيضها. فبيان الأشكال ونتاجها فيه أنه لا حاجة إليها. فإن الشكل الأول يمكن أن يستعمل فيه جميع المواد كلفة ومشقة مع الثبوتية والسلبية، الكلية والجزئية وقد عرفت انتفاء فائدته فانتفاء فائدة فروعه التي لا تفيد إلا بالرد إليه أولى وأحرى.
Refutation of the figures. He said: As for what he mentioned after that of the two remaining figures, they are not used as he built them on the first figure. And if they are valid by converting their premises until they return to the first figure, the first (point) is that the scholars refuted them without collusion, so the discussion then about the first figure is the discussion about them. And he mentioned another speech not in its place. And his point is that the other figures only produce (conclusions) by reduction to the first figure, either by qiyas al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum) which involves proving a thing by invalidating its opposite, or by means of the judgment of the contradictory of the proposition or its simple converse or the converse of its contradictory. So the explanation of the figures and their products (shows) that there is no need for them. For the first figure can be used for all materials, with burden and difficulty, for affirmative and negative, universal and particular, and you already know the loss of its benefit, so the loss of benefit of its branches, which only benefit by reduction to it, is more fitting and proper.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage discusses the reduction of the second and third syllogistic figures to the first figure. The author begins by stating that the two remaining figures are not used as constructed; they are only valid by converting their premises to return to the first figure. He then notes that scholars have refuted them. The point is that all other figures only produce conclusions by reduction to the first figure, either through reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf) which proves a statement by disproving its contradictory, or through operations on the premises such as conversion or obversion. Since the first figure can handle all materials (affirmative/negative, universal/particular) and its utility is already questioned, the utility of its branches (the other figures) which only work by reduction is even more questionable. This reinforces the critique that the entire syllogistic system is unnecessary.

The Syllogism

The Categorical Syllogism and the Role of Tafattun
Author explains qiyas with example of nabidh and mule, then debates whether two premises always need a third mental act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a categorical syllogism with correct minor, middle, and major terms, and explain the dispute between Ibn Sina and al-Razi regarding the necessity of tafattun (realization).

Core Idea
A syllogism's conclusion depends on the mental act of realizing that the particular falls under the universal.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya critically examines the logical claim that every theoretical conclusion requires exactly two premises. He uses the classic example of nabidh, khamr, and haram to illustrate the structure of a categorical syllogism, then introduces the example of the mule to highlight a subtle point: sometimes a person knows both premises but fails to draw the conclusion because he lacks 'tafattun' (realization) of how the particular is subsumed under the universal. This leads to a debate between Ibn Sina, who insists on tafattun as a necessary third element, and al-Razi, who disagrees. Your task is to understand both the formal structure of the syllogism and the epistemological nuance of tafattun.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism's terms
Find the three terms in the nabidh example: the minor term (nabidh), the middle term (khamr), and the major term (haram). Note that the middle term appears in both premises and connects the minor to the major.
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion, the major is the predicate, and the middle is the repeating term.
2
Understand subsumption (idraj)
Recognize that the syllogism works by subsuming a particular (nabidh) under a universal (haram) through a middle universal (khamr). This is the essence of qiyas.
Subsumption means that if A is included in B, and B in C, then A is included in C.
3
Analyze the mule example
Consider the scenario: someone knows that a specific animal is a mule and knows that mules do not carry, but he does not immediately conclude that this mule does not carry. Ibn Sina says this is because he lacks tafattun—the realization that this particular mule falls under the universal 'mule'.
Tafattun is the mental act of bringing the two pieces of knowledge together.
4
Compare the positions of Ibn Sina and al-Razi
Ibn Sina argues that tafattun is necessary for the syllogism to produce knowledge; the two premises alone are not enough. Al-Razi disagrees, holding that the premises themselves suffice. Note the author's criticism that the dispute stems from blind imitation of Aristotle.
The author (Ibn Taymiyya) uses this dispute to show the flaws in the logicians' framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه الثاني: بطلان دعواهم أن النتائج النظرية تحتاج إلى مقدمتين. وهذا الذي نبه عليه هؤلاء النظار يوافق ما نبهنا عليه. فإن «القياس» مشتمل على مقدمتين - صغرى وكبرى فالكبرى هي العامة والصغرى أخص منها. كما إذا قلت: كل نبيذ خمر وكل خمر حرام فإن النبيذ المتنازع فيه أخص من الخمر والخمر أخص من الحرام. فالأول هو الحد الأصغر، والآخر هو الحد الأكبر، والأوسط المتكرر فيهما هو الحد الأوسط. وحاصل «القياس» إدراج خاص تحت عام. ومثال البغلة ومعلوم أن من علم العام فقد علم شموله لأفراده ولكن قد يعرب عنه دخول بعض الأفراد فيه إما لعزوب علمه بالعام وإما لعزوب علمه بالخاص. كما مثله المنطقيون - ابن سينا وغيره - فيمن ظن أن هذه الدابة تحمل. فيقال له «أما تعلم أن هذه بغلة؟ فيقول: «نعم»، ويقال له: «أما تعلم أن البغلة لا تحمل؟» فحينئذ يتفطن لأنها لم تحمل. فهذا وإن ذكر بشيء كان غافلاً عنه لم يستفد بذلك علم ما لم يكن يعلمه. فإن لم يخطر بقلبه هذا لم يلزم من علمه بأنها بغلة أنها لا تحمل. لكن هذا قد لا يحتاج إلا إلى مقدمة واحدة وهي التي يذكرها. فإنه إن كان يعلم أن هذه الدابة بغلة ونسي أن البغلة لا تحمل بحمل، وهو قد جهل أو نسي أن هذه بغلة، مقدمة ثالثة عرف بهذه وحدها. وقد تنازع في هذا الموضع طائفتان - ابن سينا ومن معه والرازي ومن معه. خلافهم في كون التفطن وسبب نزاعهما الأصل الفاسد الذي أخذوه تقليداً لأرسطو. فقال ابن سينا: لا بد مع المقدمتين من التفطن لاندراج الخاص تحت العام. ومثله بهذا، فقال: قد يكون الرجل يعلم أن هذه الدابة المعينة بغلة ويعلم أن البغلة لا تحمل، لكن يذهل عن دخول هذا المعين تحت ذلك العام. فإذا تفطن لذلك حصل النتاج، وإلا فلا.
The fourth station - the second aspect: The invalidity of their claim that theoretical conclusions need two premises. And this that these theorists pointed to agrees with what we pointed to. For the syllogism (qiyas) comprises two premises - minor and major. So the major is the universal and the minor is more specific than it. As when you say: Every nabidh is khamr, and every khamr is haram. So the nabidh under dispute is more specific than khamr, and khamr is more specific than haram. So the first is the minor term, and the other is the major term, and the middle repeated in them is the middle term. And the essence of the syllogism is subsuming a particular under a universal. And the example of the mule: and it is known that whoever knows the universal knows its inclusion of its individuals, but (sometimes) the inclusion of some individuals is obscure to him, either due to his ignorance of the universal or due to his ignorance of the particular. As the logicians - Ibn Sina and others - gave an example of someone who thinks that this animal (dābba) carries (loads). It is said to him: 'Do you not know that this is a mule?' He says: 'Yes,' and it is said to him: 'Do you not know that the mule does not carry?' Then he realizes that it does not carry. Thus, even if he was reminded of something he was heedless of, he did not thereby gain knowledge of what he did not know. For if this does not occur to his mind, it does not follow from his knowing that it is a mule that it does not carry. But this may not need more than one premise, which is the one he mentions. For if he knows that this animal is a mule and forgets that the mule does not carry, or he is ignorant or forgets that this is a mule, a third premise is known by this alone. And two groups have disputed in this place - Ibn Sina and those with him, and al-Razi and those with him. Their dispute concerns the matter of tafattun (realization), and the cause of their dispute is the corrupt origin they took in imitation of Aristotle. So Ibn Sina said: Along with the two premises, tafattun (realization) is necessary for the subsumption of the particular under the universal. And he gave an example: a man may know that this specific animal is a mule and know that the mule does not carry, but he is heedless of the inclusion of this specific (mule) under that universal. So when he realizes that, the production (of the conclusion) occurs, otherwise not.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the claim that theoretical conclusions require two premises. It begins by stating the invalidity of that claim and then explains the nature of qiyas (syllogism). The qiyas consists of two premises: a major (universal) and a minor (specific). The example given is: 'Every nabidh is khamr, and every khamr is haram.' Here, nabidh is the minor term, haram is the major term, and khamr is the middle term. The essence of qiyas is subsuming a particular under a universal. Then the author gives the example of the mule: someone may know that a particular animal is a mule and that mules do not carry, but he may not realize that this mule is included under the universal 'mule' (or he may be heedless of the universal). The logicians, including Ibn Sina, use this example to show that tafattun (realization) is needed in addition to the two premises. However, the author argues that sometimes only one premise is needed, for example, if the person knows the particular but forgets the universal, or vice versa. Then the author notes the dispute between Ibn Sina and al-Razi: Ibn Sina insists on the necessity of tafattun, while al-Razi disagrees. The cause of their disagreement is their blind imitation of Aristotle. The passage concludes with Ibn Sina's position: the two premises are not enough; the mental act of realizing that the particular falls under the universal is also required for the syllogism to produce the conclusion.

الحد الأصغر
الحد الأكبر
الحد الأوسط

C) One premise only.
D) Three premises.

Key Takeaway
A categorical syllogism requires not only correct premises but also the mental act of realizing how the particular is subsumed under the universal—a point of contention between Ibn Sina and al-Razi.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Two-Premise Dogma
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the two-premise dogma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that theoretical conclusions require exactly two premises, and apply his flexible premise-count analysis to a new case from Islamic law.

Core Idea
The number of premises needed depends on the knower's state, not a rigid rule.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges a foundational dogma of Aristotelian logic: that every syllogism must have exactly two premises. Using the mule example from Ibn Sina, he shows that the required number of premises varies based on whether the knower is ignorant, knows but forgets, or lacks one piece of information. This critical refinement exposes the rigidity of the logicians' system and grounds logic in real cognitive processes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya states the claim: 'that theoretical conclusions need two premises' (أن النتائج النظرية تحتاج إلى مقدمتين). Understand that he is rejecting this as a universal rule.
2
Understand the mule example and the Ibn Sina vs. al-Razi debate
Read the example: a person sees a mule and thinks it is pregnant. Ibn Sina says two premises plus attentiveness are needed; al-Razi says only two premises suffice. Notice how both assume a fixed number, differing only on the role of attentiveness.
3
Analyse Ibn Taymiyya's scenarios
He lists three cognitive states: (a) ignorant of both the particular and universal, (b) knows universal but not particular, (c) knows both but forgets. For each, he determines the required number of premises: two, one, or one (reminder).
4
Synthesise his conclusion that premise count is flexible
Ibn Taymiyya's decisive statement: 'what is sought may need one premise, or two, or three, or four.' He rejects both the necessity and sufficiency of exactly two premises.
5
Evaluate the Quranic evidence he cites
He quotes Qaf 50:6-8 to show that divine signs serve as both insight and reminder, supporting his point that knowledge can be acquired or recovered through varying means.

Source Text

المقام الرابع - الوجه الثاني : بطلان دعواهم أن النتائج النظرية تحتاج إلى مقدمتين
وهذا الذي نبه عليه هؤلاء النظار يوافق ما نبهنا عليه . فإن «القياس» مشتمل على مقدمتين - صغرى وكبرى فالكبرى هي العامة والصغرى أخص منها. كما إذا قلت: كل نبيذ خمر و كل خمر حرام فإن النبيذ المتنازع فيه أخصُّ من الخمر والخمر أخص من الحرام. فالأول هو الحد الأصغر، والآخر الحد الأصغر، والآخر هو الحد الأكبر، والأوسط المتكرر فيهما هو الحد الأوسط. وحاصل «القياس» إدراج خاص تحت عام . مثال البغلة ومعلوم أن مَن عَلم العام فقد عَلم شموله لأفراده ولكن قد يعرب عنه دخولُ وأنها لا تحمل بعض الأفراد فيه إما لعزوب علمه بالعام وإما لعزوب علمه بالخاص. كما مثله /٣٤٠ المنطقيون - ابن سينا وغيره - فيمن ظن أن هذه الدابةَ تحمل . فيقال له «أما تعلم أن هذه بغلة؟ فيقول: «نعم»، ويقال له : «أما تعلم أن البغلة لا تحمل؟» فحينئذ يتفطن لأنها لم تحمل . فهذا وإن ذُكر بشيء كان غافلاً عنه لم يستفد بذلك علم ما لم يكن يعلمه . فإن لم يخطر بقلبه هذا لم يلزم من علمه بأنها بغلة أنها لا تحمل .
لكن هذا قد لا يحتاج إلا إلى مقدمة واحدة وهي التي يذكرها . فإنه إن كان يعلم أن هذه الدابة بغلةٌ ونسي أن البغلة لا تحمل بحمل، وهو قد جهل أو نسي أن هذه بغلة ، مقدمة ثالثة عُرف بهذه وحدها. وقد تنازع في هذا الموضع طائفتان - ابن سينا ومن معه والرازي ومن معه. خلافهم في كون التفطن وسبب نزاعهما الأصلُ الفاسد الذي أخذوه تقليداً لأرسطو . فقال ابن سينا: لا بد مع المقدمتين من التفطن لاندراج الخاص تحت العام. ومثله بهذا، فقال: قد يكون الرجل يعلم أن هذه الدابة المعيَّنة ،بغلة ويعلم أن البغلة لا تحمل، لكن يذهل عن دخول هذا [٣٢٦] المعين تحت ذلك العام . فإذا تفطّن لذلك حصل النتاج، وإلا فلا.
وقال الرازي: وهذا يقتضي أنه لا بد من ثلاث مقدمات : مقدمة بأن يعلم أن هذه بغلة، ومقدمة أن البغلة لا تحمل ومقدمة أن هذا يتناول هذا واختار أنه لا يحتاج إلا إلى مقدمتين : أن هذه ،بغلة وأن البغلة لا تحمل . وأنه إذا كان غافلاً عن هذه الكلية لم يكن عالماً بها بالفعل. فإذا صار عالماً بها بالفعل بحيث تكون حاضرة في ذهنه امتنع مع ذلك أن لا يعلم أن هذه البغلة لا تحمل . الاحتياج إلى وفصلُ الخِطاب أن المطلوب قد يحتاج إلى مقدمة وإلى ثنتين، وإلى ثلاث، مقدمة أو وإلى أربع . فأصلُ الاضطراب دعواهم أن النتائج النظرية تحتاج إلى مقدمتين، وتكفي أو أكثر فيها مقدمتان. فجعلوا لا بدَّ في كل مطلوب نظري من مقدمتين، وادعوا أنه يكفي في كل مطلوب نظري مقدمتان . وكلا الأمرين باطل .
فالشخص المعين إذا رأى دابة وظنها حاملاً إن كان ممن [لم] يعلم أنها بغلة ولم يعلم أن البغال عُقم، أو يعلم الأمرين ونسيهما ، أو نسي أحدهما وجهل الآخر، فإنه يحتاج إلى العلم بمقدمتين - وتذكُر المنسي نوع من العلم. يحتاج أن يعلم أنها بغلة، ويعلم أن البَغْلات لا يحمِلْنَ . وإن كان يعلم أن البغلة لا تحمل لكن لم يعرف أنها بغلة احتاج إلى مقدمة واحدة. فإذا قيل له هذه بغلة»، فإذا عَرَف أنها بغلة، وفي نفسه معلوم أن البغلة لا تحمل علم أن هذه المعيَّنة لا تحمل . وإن كان يعلم أن هذه ،بغلة، وقد علم قديماً أن البغلة لا تحمل لكن عَزَب هذا العلم عن ذهنه في هذا الوقت ونسيه، فهذا قد نسى عِلْمِه، والنسيان من أضداد العلم، فإذا ذكر بعلمه ذكره . فإذا ذكر أن البغلة لا تحمل حصل له مقدمة واحدة .
والعلم يحصل بالعلم بالدليل لمن لم يكن عالماً به قط، ولمن يذكره بعد النسيان إذا كان قد علمه ثم نسيه. ولهذا قال سبحانه: ﴿ أَفَلَمْ يَنظُرُوا إِلَى السَّمَاءِ فَوْقَهُمْ كَيْفَ بَنَيْنَهَا وَزَيَّتْنَا وَمَا لَهَا مِن فُرُوج وَالْأَرْضَ مَدَدْنَا وَأَلْقَيْنَا فِيهَا رَوَاسِيَ وَأَنبَتنَا فِيهَا مِن كُلِّ نَوْعٍ بَهِيجٍ إِنَّ فِي ذَٰلِكَ لَآيَاتٍ لِّكُلِّ عَبْدٍ مٌّنِيبٍ ) [سورة ق : ٦ - ٨] . فبين سبحانه أن آياته تبصرة وتذكرة.
The Fourth Station - The Second Aspect: Refutation of their claim that theoretical conclusions need two premises. And this which these scholars pointed out agrees with what we pointed out. For the syllogism includes two premises - minor and major; the major is the general and the minor is more specific than it. As when you say: Every nabidh is khamr and every khamr is forbidden; so the disputed nabidh is more specific than khamr, and khamr is more specific than the forbidden. So the first is the minor term, and the other is the minor term, and the other is the major term, and the middle repeated in them is the middle term. And the summary of the syllogism is the subsumption of a particular under a universal. The example of the mule: It is known that whoever knows the universal knows its inclusion of its individuals, but its inclusion may become obscured for him regarding some individuals, either due to the absence of his knowledge of the universal or the absence of his knowledge of the particular. As the logicians - Ibn Sina and others - exemplified in the case of one who thinks that this beast is pregnant. It is said to him: 'Do you not know that this is a mule?' He says: 'Yes.' And it is said to him: 'Do you not know that the mule does not become pregnant?' Then he realizes that she is not pregnant. So this, though reminded of something he was heedless of, does not thereby gain knowledge of what he did not know. For if this does not occur to his mind, it does not follow from his knowledge that it is a mule that it does not become pregnant. But this may need only one premise, which is what he is reminded of. For if he knows that this beast is a mule and forgot that the mule does not become pregnant, or he was ignorant of or forgot that this is a mule, a third premise is needed; he would know by this alone. And two groups disputed this point - Ibn Sina and those with him, and al-Razi and those with him. Their dispute about the 'attentiveness' (tafattun) and the cause of their disagreement is the corrupt principle which they took from Aristotle. Ibn Sina said: Along with the two premises, there must be attentiveness to the subsumption of the particular under the universal. And he exemplified it with this: A man may know that this specific beast is a mule and know that the mule does not become pregnant, but he is heedless of the subsumption of this particular under that universal. So if he becomes attentive to that, the conclusion results, otherwise not. And al-Razi said: This would necessitate three premises: a premise that he knows this is a mule, a premise that the mule does not become pregnant, and a premise that this is subsumed under that. And he chose that [only] two premises are needed: that this is a mule and that the mule does not become pregnant. And that if he is heedless of this universality, he is not actually knowing it. So when he becomes actually knowing it such that it is present in his mind, it is impossible that he does not know that this mule does not become pregnant. The decisive statement is that what is sought may need one premise, or two, or three, or four. So the root of the confusion is their claim that theoretical conclusions need two premises, and that two suffice, or more. So they made it necessary in every theoretical sought that it [needs] two premises, and claimed that two premises suffice for every theoretical sought. Both claims are false. So the specific person when he sees a beast and thinks it is pregnant: if he does not know that it is a mule and does not know that mules are barren, or he knows both and forgets them, or forgets one and is ignorant of the other, then he needs knowledge of two premises - and recollection of the forgotten is a kind of knowledge. He needs to know that it is a mule, and know that mules do not become pregnant. And if he knows that the mule does not become pregnant but does not know that it is a mule, he needs one premise. So if it is said to him 'this is a mule', and he knows that it is a mule, and in his mind it is known that the mule does not become pregnant, he knows that this specific one does not become pregnant. And if he knows that this is a mule, and he previously knew that the mule does not become pregnant but that knowledge departed from his mind at this time and he forgot it, then he has forgotten his knowledge, and forgetfulness is opposite of knowledge. So if he is reminded of his knowledge, he remembers it. Then if he remembers that the mule does not become pregnant, he obtains one premise. And knowledge is obtained by knowing the proof for one who never knew it at all, and for one who remembers it after forgetfulness if he knew it and then forgot it. And therefore Allah Most High said: 'Have they not looked at the heaven above them, how We built it and adorned it, and it has no cracks? And the earth We spread out, and placed therein firm mountains, and caused to grow therein every beautiful kind. Indeed in this are signs for every servant who turns in repentance.' So He showed that His signs are insights and reminders.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that every theoretical conclusion requires exactly two premises. Ibn Taymiyya begins by acknowledging that the standard syllogism (qiyas) consists of a minor and major premise, with the major being general and the minor more specific. He gives the example: 'Every nabidh is khamr, and every khamr is haram' — here nabidh is the minor term, khamr the middle, and haram the major. The essence of the syllogism is subsuming a particular under a universal. However, he introduces the well-known mule example used by Ibn Sina: a person knows that this animal is a mule and knows that mules do not get pregnant, but fails to apply the universal rule, so he needs a reminder (tafattun) to produce the conclusion. Ibn Taymiyya points out that the logicians themselves disagree: Ibn Sina says a third element (attentiveness) is required, making it effectively three premises, while al-Razi argues that no third premise is needed because if the universal is actually known, the conclusion follows directly. Ibn Taymiyya then declares the decisive answer: the number of premises varies depending on the knower's state. He lists scenarios: if the person lacks both pieces of knowledge, he needs two premises; if he knows the universal but not the particular, he needs only one; if he knew but forgot, reminding him gives one premise (recollection). Thus the logicians' rigid two-premise rule is false in both directions — it is not always necessary, nor is it always sufficient. The passage ends with a Quranic verse (Qaf 50:6-8) to illustrate that divine signs serve as both insight and reminder, showing that knowledge can be acquired or recovered through various means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Now imagine a person who already knows that nabidh is an intoxicant but does not know the general rule about intoxicants being haram. How many premises does he need to reach the conclusion?
