The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya on Variable Premise Requirements
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that exactly two premises are sufficient for every theoretical result.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the logicians' claim that exactly two premises suffice for every theoretical result by using the mule example and explaining the role of forgetfulness.

Core Idea
Two premises are neither necessary nor sufficient; the knower's state determines the count.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a fundamental claim of the logicians: that every theoretical result requires exactly two premises. He argues that this is false, showing through a concrete example how the number varies with the knower's prior knowledge, ignorance, and forgetfulness. This lesson unpacks his critique and its implications for deductive reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians, including al-Razi, claimed that every theoretical result (natija nazariyya) needs exactly two premises. Ibn Taymiyya says this is the root of confusion.
The claim is both a necessity and sufficiency claim: two premises are always required and always enough.
2
Extract the mule scenario
A person sees an animal and wrongly thinks it pregnant. Depending on what the person knows or forgets, different numbers of premises are needed. This is the key counterexample.
3
Analyze the knowledge states
If the person knows neither that it is a mule nor that mules are sterile, two premises are needed. If he knows the general rule but not the particular, one premise suffices. If he forgot the general rule, reminding him is one premise.
Remembering is considered a kind of knowledge, so a reminder counts as a premise.
4
Refute the logicians' claim
The logicians' claim fails because sometimes one premise is enough (when one premise is already known), and sometimes more than two are needed (if the knower is ignorant in multiple ways). Thus the fixed number is invalid.
5
Conclude with the contextual principle
The number of premises is not fixed but depends on the individual's epistemic state. This is not a flaw in logic but a correction of an overgeneralization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الرازي: وهذا يقتضي أنه لا بد من ثلاث مقدمات : مقدمة بأن يعلم أن هذه بغلة، ومقدمة أن البغلة لا تحمل ومقدمة أن هذا يتناول هذا واختار أنه لا يحتاج إلا إلى مقدمتين : أن هذه بغلة وأن البغلة لا تحمل . وأنه إذا كان غافلاً عن هذه الكلية لم يكن عالماً بها بالفعل. فإذا صار عالماً بها بالفعل بحيث تكون حاضرة في ذهنه امتنع مع ذلك أن لا يعلم أن هذه البغلة لا تحمل . وفصلُ الخِطاب أن المطلوب قد يحتاج إلى مقدمة وإلى ثنتين، وإلى ثلاث، وإلى أربع . فأصلُ الاضطراب دعواهم أن النتائج النظرية تحتاج إلى مقدمتين، وتكفي فيها مقدمتان. فجعلوا لا بدَّ في كل مطلوب نظري من مقدمتين، وادعوا أنه يكفي في كل مطلوب نظري مقدمتان . وكلا الأمرين باطل . فالشخص المعين إذا رأى دابة وظنها حاملاً إن كان ممن لم يعلم أنها بغلة ولم يعلم أن البغال عُقم، أو يعلم الأمرين ونسيهما ، أو نسي أحدهما وجهل الآخر، فإنه يحتاج إلى العلم بمقدمتين - وتذكُر المنسي نوع من العلم. يحتاج أن يعلم أنها بغلة، ويعلم أن البَغْلات لا يحمِلْنَ . وإن كان يعلم أن البغلة لا تحمل لكن لم يعرف أنها بغلة احتاج إلى مقدمة واحدة. فإذا قيل له هذه بغلة»، فإذا عَرَف أنها بغلة، وفي نفسه معلوم أن البغلة لا تحمل علم أن هذه المعيَّنة لا تحمل . وإن كان يعلم أن هذه بغلة، وقد علم قديماً أن البغلة لا تحمل لكن عَزَب هذا العلم عن ذهنه في هذا الوقت ونسيه، فهذا قد نسى عِلْمِه، والنسيان من أضداد العلم، فإذا ذكر بعلمه ذكره . فإذا ذكر أن البغلة لا تحمل حصل له مقدمة واحدة .
And al-Razi said: This requires that there must be three premises: a premise that one knows that this is a mule, a premise that the mule does not become pregnant, and a premise that this (particular) falls under that (general). And he chose that it needs only two premises: that this is a mule and that the mule does not become pregnant. And that if he is heedless of this universal, he is not actually knowing it. And when he becomes actually knowing it such that it is present in his mind, it is impossible that he not know that this mule does not become pregnant. The decisive statement is that the required (result) may need one premise, or two, or three, or four. The root of the confusion is their claim that theoretical results need two premises, and two premises are sufficient. So they made it necessary that every theoretical result requires two premises, and claimed that two premises suffice for every theoretical result. Both claims are false. So a particular person, if he sees an animal and thinks it is pregnant, if he is one who does not know that it is a mule and does not know that mules are sterile, or knows both but forgets them, or forgets one and is ignorant of the other, then he needs knowledge of two premises—and remembering what is forgotten is a kind of knowledge. He needs to know that it is a mule and to know that mules do not become pregnant. But if he knows that the mule does not become pregnant but does not know that it is a mule, he needs one premise. So if it is said to him, 'This is a mule,' then if he knows that it is a mule, and in his mind it is known that the mule does not become pregnant, he knows that this particular does not become pregnant. And if he knows that this is a mule, and had known previously that the mule does not become pregnant but that knowledge vanished from his mind at that time and he forgot it, then he has forgotten his knowledge, and forgetfulness is the opposite of knowledge. So if he remembers, he remembers by his knowledge. So if he remembers that the mule does not become pregnant, he obtains one premise.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the logicians' claim that every theoretical result (natija nazariyya) necessarily requires exactly two premises. Ibn Taymiyya begins by citing al-Razi's position that three premises are needed, but al-Razi chose two. The author then gives the decisive answer: the number of premises varies. The logicians' claim that two premises always suffice and are necessary is false. He demonstrates this with a concrete example: a person seeing an animal and mistakenly thinking it is pregnant. Depending on the person's knowledge state—ignorance, forgetfulness, or partial knowledge—the number of required premises changes. If the person knows nothing, two premises are needed (this is a mule; mules are not pregnant). If he knows the general rule but not the particular, one premise suffices (this is a mule). If he knows the particular but forgot the general rule, reminding him of the rule is one premise. Thus, the claim of a fixed number is refuted.

Key Terms
مقدمة (muqaddima): A premise: a statement from which a conclusion is derived. Ibn Taymiyya shows the number needed depends on the knower's state. [Note: No significant shift; stable across logical works.]
نتيجة نظرية (natīja naẓariyya): A theoretical result or conclusion that is not immediately known but requires reasoning. [Note: Standard term in logic; no shift.]
نسيان (nisyān): Forgetfulness, described as the opposite of knowledge ('ilm). Remembering forgotten knowledge is considered a type of knowledge in the context of premises. [Note: In this context, it is treated as a psychological state that affects the need for premises.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, if a person knows that mules are not pregnant but does not know that a particular animal is a mule, how many premises does he need to conclude that this animal is not pregnant?
A) Zero
B) One
C) Two
D) Three

Key Takeaway
The number of premises required for a theoretical conclusion depends on the knower's prior knowledge and memory, not on a fixed logical form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a student who knows that every intoxicant is prohibited (hukm) but does not know whether a particular beverage (nabidh) is intoxicating. How many premises does he need to conclude that nabidh is prohibited? Explain using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning. Then, describe a situation where the same student forgets the universal rule and needs a reminder; how many premises now?

Noticing as a Single Premise: Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Two-Premise Requirement
Author argues that noticing is a single premise, not two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that remembering a forgotten premise is a single premise and explain how it challenges the classical syllogistic requirement.

Core Idea
Noticing is not an extra premise; it is the same premise remembered.

Ibn Taymiyya takes on a key assumption of the logicians: that a syllogism must have exactly two premises. Here, he examines a case where someone knows a premise but forgets it. The logicians argue that a third premise of 'noticing' is required. Ibn Taymiyya counters that this noticing is nothing but the recall of the forgotten premise, making it a single premise. This challenges the very foundation of the two-premise doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians claim that two premises are necessary for a syllogism, and that when a premise is forgotten, an additional act of noticing is required.
2
Analyze the mule example
Consider the case of someone who knows that mules do not carry offspring but forgets this knowledge. The logicians say noticing is needed.
3
Track Ibn Taymiyya's reframing
Ibn Taymiyya argues that this noticing is simply the remembering of the forgotten premise, not a separate premise.
4
Count the premises
According to Ibn Taymiyya, the total premises are just one: the recalled major premise. This undermines the necessity of two premises.
The key is that the act of noticing does not add new content; it retrieves existing knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه الثاني : كون تذكر المنسي مقدمة مستقلة واحدة فالتبصرة بعد العَمَى، وهو الجهل ؛ والتذكرة بعد النسيان، وهو ضد العلم. التفطن مقدمة وهؤلاء لما تقلدوا قول من يقول : إنه لا بد من مقدمتين وبهما يحصل النتاج»، فرأى بعضهم أن المقدمتين معلومتان لهذا الظانّ أن هذه البغلة حامل مع عدم العلم .. قال: فلا بد من التفطن. فيقال له: ما ذكرته من التفطن هو الذي يحتاج إليه هذا، لا. يحتاج إلى شيء من المقدمات غير هذه. وهذا التفطن هو تذكر ما كان نسيه، وهو مقدمة واحدة .
The fourth station - the second aspect: that remembering the forgotten is a single independent premise. For insight after blindness, which is ignorance; and reminding after forgetfulness, which is the opposite of knowledge. The act of noticing is a premise. And these people, when they accepted the statement of those who say: 'It is necessary to have two premises and through them the conclusion is produced,' so some of them saw that the two premises are known to this person who thinks that this mule is pregnant without knowledge... He said: 'So noticing is necessary.' So it is said to him: 'What you mentioned of noticing is what this one needs; he does not need any of the premises other than this.' And this noticing is remembering what he had forgotten, and it is a single premise.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' insistence on the necessity of two premises. Ibn Taymiyya uses the example of someone who knows a major premise (that mules do not carry offspring) but forgets it. The logician would say that in addition to the major and minor premises, a third premise of 'noticing' (التفطن) is needed. Ibn Taymiyya counters that this noticing is itself a single premise — it is simply the remembering of the forgotten major premise. Thus, the total number of premises is one, not two or three. This undercuts the claim that a syllogism must have exactly two premises. The argument proceeds by first defining terms: insight after ignorance, reminding after forgetfulness. Then it presents the logicians' claim that two premises are necessary. Ibn Taymiyya then shows that the required noticing is nothing but the recollection of the forgotten premise, making it a single premise.

Key Terms
التفطن
التذكرة
النسيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in the passage, what is the act of noticing (التفطن) in the mule example?
A) A third premise needed besides the major and minor.
B) Part of the minor premise.
C) A single premise consisting of the recalled major premise.
D) Unnecessary for knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya challenges the necessity of two premises by showing that noticing a forgotten premise is just its recall, making it a single premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a scenario: A student of fiqh knows the hadith 'The prayer of one who does not recite al-Fatiha is invalid' but forgets it when asked about the ruling for a specific prayer. The Nature of Implication: Customary vs. Intellectual Necessity
Author discusses the necessary connection between evidence and its signified, and the debate on its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between customary necessity ('adi) and intellectual necessity (tadammun) as discussed by Ibn Taymiyya, and explain their implications for how evidence yields knowledge in Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
Not all implications are intellectually necessary; many are customary, dependent on God's sunnah.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya tackles a foundational question in Islamic epistemology: what kind of necessity connects evidence to what it signifies? Is it a logical, inseparable bond, or a habitual pattern established by God? He presents the views of the Ash'aris and Mu'tazilites and clarifies his own position. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating how proofs work in kalam and usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
Ibn Taymiyya states that knowledge requires conceiving both the evidence and the necessity of its implication. Without conceiving the necessity, mere evidence yields no knowledge.
2
Distinguish two types of implications
He differentiates between clear implications (bedihiyyat) that are known directly upon understanding the evidence, and hidden implications that require an intermediate proof (a second evidence).
This shows that the necessity of implication is not always self-evident.
3
Contrast the views of Ash'aris and Mu'tazilites
The Ash'aris hold that necessity is customary ('adi), like satiety after eating – it occurs by God's habit and could be otherwise. The Mu'tazilites argue for intellectual necessity (tadammun), where the signified is contained in the evidence, inseparable.
The Mu'tazilites also used the concept of tawallud (generation) to explain how evidence causes knowledge.
4
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's position
He sides with the Ash'aris, emphasizing that the connection is by God's sunnah, not logical necessity. He contrasts this with the relationship between life and knowledge, where life is a condition, not a cause.
This highlights that knowledge through evidence is contingent on God's decree, not inherent necessity.
5
Apply the distinction
Consider how this distinction affects the certainty of proofs in kalam. For example, the proof from temporality (huduth) relies on customary necessity that the world requires a creator, which is not intellectually necessary but reliable by God's custom.
This prevents over-rationalizing theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمدلول لازم للدليل . فمتى تصور الإنسان الدليل ولزوم المدلول له كون المدلول لازمًا للدليل تَصَوَّر المدلول . فإذا تصور أنها بغلة وتصور لازم ذلك، وهو نفي الحمل عن البغلة، تصور قطعاً نفيه عن هذه. فأما مجرد الدليل بدون تصور لزوم المدلول له فلا يحصل به العلم. واللوازم البين لزومها للدليل تُعلم بمجرد العلم به وبلزومها له ؛ واللوازم الخفية التي يفتقر العلم بلزومها إلى وسط وهو دليل ثانٍ على اللزوم، يقف على ذلك. والأذهان في هذا متفاوتة. فقد يحتاج هذا الذهن في معرفة اللزوم إلى وسط الدليل، والآخر لا يحتاج إليه . وقد تنازع النظار في العلم الحاصل بالدليل: هل هو لزومه عن الدليل لزوماً عادياً كما يقولونه في الشَّبَع مع الأكل، أو لزوماً عقلياً يسمى «التضمن» بحيث لا يمكن الانفكاك عنه كما يمتنع وجود العلم والإرادة بدون الحياة. والأول قول قدماء النظار كالأشعري وغيره. ولهذا جعله المعتزلة من باب العلم بالدليل سبب للعلم التولد»، وهذا كالرؤية مع التحديق وكالسمع مع الإصغاء. وإلا فحصول العلم بالدليل دون المدلول عليه ليس ممتنعاً لذاته، بل الأول سبب للثاني، ومقتض له، وموجب له، بحكم سُنَّة الله تعالى في عباده بخلاف الحياة مع العلم، فإن الأول شرط للثاني. ولهذا كان العلم يوجد مع الحياة، ليست الحياة متقدمة عليه كما يتقدم العلم بالمدلول عليه بالدليل على «العلم بالمدلول عليه .
The signified is necessary for the indicator. So whenever a person conceives the indicator and the necessity of the signified to it — that the signified is necessary for the indicator — he conceives the signified. So if he conceives that it is a mule and conceives the necessary concomitant of that, which is the negation of pregnancy for the mule, he necessarily conceives its negation for this particular. As for mere evidence without conceiving the necessity of the signified to it, no knowledge is obtained. And the necessary concomitants whose necessity to the evidence is clear are known merely by knowing it and their necessity to it; but the hidden necessary concomitants, whose necessity requires a middle term — that is, a second evidence on the necessity — that depends. And minds differ in this. So one mind may need a middle evidence to know the necessity, while another does not need it. And the theorists have disagreed about the knowledge obtained through evidence: is it its necessity from the evidence by customary necessity, as they say in satiety with eating, or by intellectual necessity called 'implication' (tadammun) such that it cannot be separated, just as the existence of knowledge and will without life is impossible. The first is the view of the ancient theorists like al-Ash'ari and others. For this reason, the Mu'tazilites made it a matter of knowledge through evidence as a cause for generated knowledge, like vision with focusing and hearing with listening. Otherwise, the existence of knowledge through evidence without what is signified is not impossible in itself; rather, the first is a cause for the second, a requirement for it, and a necessitator for it, by the decree of Allah's sunnah in His servants, unlike life with knowledge, for the first is a condition for the second. For this reason, knowledge exists with life; life does not precede it as knowledge of the signified precedes through evidence over 'knowledge of the signified.'
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the nature of the necessary connection (luzum) between evidence (daliil) and its signified (madlool). Ibn Taymiyya states that for knowledge to be obtained, one must conceive both the evidence and its necessary implication. He distinguishes between clear implications, which are known directly, and hidden ones that require a middle term. He then presents a central debate among theorists: whether the necessity is customary ('adi), like eating leading to satiety by habit, or intellectual ('aqli), like life being necessary for knowledge. The Ash'arites held the customary view, while the Mu'tazilites saw the evidence as a cause that generates knowledge (tawallud), akin to vision causing sight. Ibn Taymiyya sides with the Ash'arites, emphasizing that the connection is not logically necessary but by God's custom (sunnah). He contrasts this with life and knowledge, where life is a condition, not a cause.

Key Terms
اللزوم
التضمن
التولد

A) Customary necessity can be separated by God's will; intellectual cannot.
B) Both are the same.
C) Intellectual necessity is based on habit.
D) Customary necessity is necessary by itself.

Key Takeaway
The connection between evidence and conclusion is often customary, not intellectually necessary, and operates by God's sunnah.

Independence of Particular and Universal Knowledge
Author generalizes the mule example, arguing particular and universal knowledge can be independent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that knowledge of the particular does not depend on knowledge of the universal, and identify a real case from Islamic jurisprudence where this principle applies.

Core Idea
You can know that this is a mule without knowing that all mules are sterile.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya extends his critique of syllogistic logic by showing that particular and universal knowledge are often independent. He uses the example of knowing a specific mule without knowing the general rule about mules, and vice versa. This challenges the Aristotelian claim that all knowledge ultimately depends on universal premises. Let's unpack his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of knowledge
The author distinguishes between al-khass (particular, a specific instance) and al-'amm (universal, a general rule). Look for these terms in the Arabic text.
e.g., 'this mule' vs. 'every mule does not carry offspring'
2
Observe the independence claim
Find the phrase 'قد يعلم الخاص ولا يعلم العام' (may know the particular without knowing the universal). Note that the independence works both ways: one may also know the universal without knowing the particular.
This directly contradicts the Aristotelian view that universal knowledge is necessary for particular knowledge.
3
Examine the temporal order
The author says it is not necessary that knowledge of the particular precedes the universal or vice versa; it varies among people ('بل قد يتفق في بعض الناس...'). This refutes any fixed hierarchy.
4
Apply to the mule example
Consider the specific case: a person knows that this animal is a mule (particular) but does not know that mules are sterile (universal). Conversely, someone may know the universal rule but mistake a mule for a horse.
The example shows that syllogistic deduction is not always required to know a particular fact.
5
Generalize to all theoretical matters
The author claims this pattern holds across all sciences ('سائر النظريات'). This undermines the claim that syllogism is the only reliable method for knowledge.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to argue that experience and direct observation can suffice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال البغلة سائر النظريات ونظار المسلمين مع تنازعهم في هذا متفقون على أن الدليل قد يكون ينطبق على مقدمة واحدة كما ذكرناه وما ذكروه من البغلة موجود في سائر النظريات . فإن الإنسان قد يعلم الخاص ولا يعلم العام، وقد يعلم العام ولا يعلم الخاص، كما قد يعلم أن هذه بغلة ولا يعلم أن البغلة لا تحمل، وقد يعلم أن البغلة لا تحمل ولا يعلم أن هذه بغلة . ولا يجب أن يكون علمه بالخاص مقدماً على العام ولا متأخراً، بل قد يتفق في بعض الناس علمه بالخاص قبل العام، وفي بعض الناس يعلم العام قبل الخاص . وكذلك المعيّنات. وذلك أن علمه بأن هذه البغلة لا تحمل كعلمه بأن هذه لا تحمل، وبأن هذه لا تحمل . فلا يجب أن يعلم أن هذا المعين مثل هذا المعين، بل قد بالمعينات قبل العلم بالقضية يعلم أحد المعيَّنين ولا يعلم الآخر . ولا يجب أن يكون علمه بالمعينات قبل علمه بالقضية العامة، ولا أن يكون بالمعينات أعلم . ولا يجب أن يكون بالقضية العامة الكلية التي يستفيد بها العلم بحكم القضايا المعينة أعلم منه بوصف القضايا المعينة. أي لا يجب أن يكون علمه بأن كل بغلة لا تحمل أقوى، ولا يجب أن يكون العلم العام الذي يفيده علم المعينات في نفسه أسبقَ مِنْ عِلْمٍ معيَّن عَلِم به أنها بغلة. أي، العلم بأن البغلة لا تحمل لا يجب أن يكون أقوى ولا أسبق في الذهن من العلم بأن هذه الدابة بغلة، بل قد يجهل أنها بغلة، كما قد يجهل أن البغلة لا تحمل . فإذا قدر أنه يعلم أن البغلة لا تحمل، ومستنده في ذلك ما اشتهر من خَبَر الناس، فذلك يتناول المعيَّن كتناوله لما هو أعم منه مثل تناوله للبغلات الحمراء، والسوداء، ولما تكون أمه أتاناً. فلو خطر له أن ما تكون أمه أتاناً. وأبوه حصاناً يحملُ قيل له أما تعلم أن هذه البغلة، وأن البغلة لا تحمل؟». وإن كان مستنده في أن البغلة لا تحمل هو تجربته فالتجربة لا تكون عامة، وإنما جرب ذلك في بغلات معينة، فما به يعلم مساواة سائر البغلات لها يعلم مساواة هذه البغلة لها . واعتبر هذا بنظائره يتبيَّن لك أنه يمكن الاستغناء عن القياس المنطقي، بل يكون
The example of the mule applies to all theoretical matters. And the Muslim theorists, despite their disagreement on this, agree that evidence may apply to a single premise as we mentioned, and what they mentioned about the mule exists in all theoretical matters. For a person may know the particular and not know the universal, and may know the universal and not know the particular, just as he may know that this is a mule and not know that the mule does not carry, and may know that the mule does not carry and not know that this is a mule. And it is not necessary that his knowledge of the particular precedes the universal or follows it; rather, it may happen in some people that his knowledge of the particular is before the universal, and in some people he knows the universal before the particular. And likewise with the particulars. That is because his knowledge that this mule does not carry is like his knowledge that this does not carry, and that this does not carry. So it is not necessary that he know that this particular is like that particular; rather, he may know one particular and not know the other. And it is not necessary that his knowledge of particulars precedes his knowledge of the universal proposition, nor that he is more knowledgeable about the particulars. And it is not necessary that he is more knowledgeable about the universal general proposition by which he gains knowledge of the particular propositions' rulings than his description of the particular propositions. That is, it is not necessary that his knowledge that every mule does not carry is stronger, nor is it necessary that the universal knowledge that informs him of the knowledge of particulars is itself prior to the knowledge of a particular by which he knows that it is a mule. That is, the knowledge that the mule does not carry is not necessarily stronger nor prior in the mind than the knowledge that this animal is a mule; rather, he may be ignorant that it is a mule, just as he may be ignorant that the mule does not carry. So if it is supposed that he knows that the mule does not carry, and his basis for that is what is famous from people's reports, then that includes the particular just as it includes what is more general than it, such as its inclusion of red mules, black mules, and those whose mother is a donkey. If it occurs to him that what has a donkey mother and a horse father may carry, it is said to him: 'Do you not know that this is a mule, and that the mule does not carry?' And if his basis for the mule not carrying is his experience, then experience is not general; rather, he only experienced that in particular mules. So what he uses to know the equivalence of other mules to those, he uses to know the equivalence of this mule to them. And consider this by its parallels, it becomes clear to you that it is possible to dispense with logical syllogism, rather it is...
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage extends the mule example from the previous lesson to all theoretical matters. Ibn Taymiyya argues that knowledge of the particular (khass) and the universal ('amm) can be independent: one may know that this is a mule without knowing that all mules do not carry offspring, and vice versa. He emphasizes that the order of knowledge (particular before universal or vice versa) is not fixed; it varies among people. Similarly, knowledge of one particular does not require knowledge of another. This undermines the necessity of syllogistic reasoning, because one can know a particular conclusion directly without a syllogism. The example of experience shows that one can know by analogy that another mule is similar without a formal syllogism. The passage concludes that logical syllogism can be dispensed with, as consideration of parallels shows.

Key Terms
الخاص (al-khass): Particular; a specific instance, e.g., 'this mule'.
العام (al-'amm): Universal; a general rule, e.g., 'every mule does not carry offspring'.
المعين (al-mu'ayyan): A specific individual or particular entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge of the universal always precedes knowledge of the particular.
B) Knowledge of the particular always precedes knowledge of the universal.
C) The order of knowledge between particular and universal can vary.
D) One cannot know a particular without first knowing the universal.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of particulars and universals can be independent; one does not require the other, and the order of acquisition varies, rendering syllogistic reasoning non-essential.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Independence of Particular and Universal Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the necessity of logical syllogism via the mule example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Ibn Taymiyya's mule example demonstrates that knowledge of a particular instance does not require knowledge of a universal rule, and vice versa.

Core Idea
Knowing that this is a mule does not require knowing that all mules are barren.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents a powerful challenge to the logicians' claim that all knowledge of particulars must be derived from universal premises through syllogism. Using the simple example of a mule, he shows that you can know a specific mule without knowing that all mules are barren, and you can know the universal rule without recognizing the specific instance. This insight has far-reaching implications for how we think about evidence and proof in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between 'particular knowledge' (al-‘ilm al-khass) and 'universal knowledge' (al-‘ilm al-‘amm). The particular is knowledge of a specific individual (e.g., 'this mule'), while the universal is a general proposition (e.g., 'all mules are barren').
Notice the Arabic terms: الخاص for particular, العام for universal.
2
Analyze the four scenarios
Ibn Taymiyya lists four possibilities: know particular without universal, know universal without particular, know both, or know neither. The mule example illustrates each: you may know this is a mule but not that mules are barren, or you may know the general rule but not recognize this animal as a mule.
This undermines the logicians' assumption that universal knowledge always precedes particular.
3
Examine the independence claim
The key claim is that there is no necessary priority or strength between the two types of knowledge. One can be prior or stronger in the mind regardless of the other. This is a direct refutation of the Aristotelian view that demonstration must proceed from universals to particulars.
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that the mind does not require a universal premise to know a particular.
4
Apply the critique to the necessity of syllogism
If knowledge can arise independently, then the full syllogism with two premises is often unnecessary. In many cases, a single premise is sufficient. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the logical syllogism is frequently a wasteful elongation of thought.
This is a 'critical refinement' of the standard logical doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونظار المسلمين مع تنازعهم في هذا متفقون على أن الدليل قد يكون ينطبق على مقدمة واحدة كما ذكرناه وما ذكروه من البغلة موجود في سائر النظريات . فإن الإنسان قد يعلم الخاص ولا يعلم العام، وقد يعلم العام ولا يعلم الخاص، كما قد يعلم أن هذه بغلة ولا يعلم أن البغلة لا تحمل، وقد يعلم أن البغلة لا تحمل ولا يعلم أن هذه بغلة . ولا يجب أن يكون علمه بالخاص مقدماً على العام ولا متأخراً، بل قد يتفق في بعض الناس علمه بالخاص قبل العام، وفي بعض الناس يعلم العام قبل الخاص . وكذلك المعيّنات. وذلك أن علمه بأن هذه البغلة لا تحمل كعلمه بأن هذه لا يجوز العلم تحمل، وبأن هذه لا تحمل . فلا يجب أن يعلم أن هذا المعين مثل هذا المعين، بل قد يعلم أحد المعيَّنين ولا يعلم الآخر . ولا يجب أن يكون علمه بالمعينات قبل علمه بالقضية العامة، ولا أن يكون بالمعينات أعلم . ولا يجب أن يكون بالقضية العامة الكلية التي يستفيد بها العلم بحكم القضايا المعينة أعلم منه بوصف القضايا المعينة. أي لا يجب أن يكون علمه بأن كل بغلة لا تحمل أقوى، ولا يجب أن يكون العلم العام الذي يفيده علم المعينات في نفسه أسبقَ مِنْ عِلْمٍ معيَّن عَلِم به أنها بغلة. أي، العلم بأن البغلة لا تحمل لا يجب أن يكون أقوى ولا أسبق في الذهن من العلم بأن هذه الدابة بغلة، بل قد يجهل أنها بغلة، كما قد يجهل أن البغلة لا تحمل . فإذا قدر أنه يعلم أن البغلة لا تحمل، ومستنده في ذلك ما اشتهر من خَبَر الناس، فذلك يتناول المعيَّن كتناوله لما هو أعم منه مثل تناوله للبغلات الحمراء، والسوداء، ولما تكون أمه أتاناً. فلو خطر له أن ما تكون أمه أتاناً وأبوه حصاناً يحملُ قيل له أما تعلم أن هذه البغلة، وأن البغلة لا تحمل؟». وإن كان مستنده في أن البغلة لا تحمل هو تجربته فالتجربة لا تكون عامة، وإنما جرب ذلك في بغلات معينة، فما به يعلم مساواة سائر البغلات لها يعلم مساواة هذه البغلة لها . واعتبر هذا بنظائره يتبيَّن لك أنه يمكن الاستغناء عن القياس المنطقي، بل يكون المقام الرابع - الوجه الثاني : مثال البغلة ينطبق على سائر النظريات المقدمات بحسب حال استعماله تطويلاً وتكثيراً للفكر والنظر والكلام بلا فائدة وأن الحاجة إلى المقدمات الحاجة إلى بحسب حال المستدلّ . فقد يحتاج تارةً إلى مقدمة وتارةً إلى ثنتين، وتارة إلى ثلاث، وتارةً إلى أكثر من ذلك . وأنه تارةً يجهل كون المعين بغلة، وإذا كان كذلك فمتى علم أن المستدل هذه بغلة علم أنها لا تحمل إذا كان قد حصل في نفسه علم عام يتناول جميع البغلات . وإذا علم المعين، وهو أنها بغلة ولم يعلم المطلق، لم يحتج إلا إلى علم العام، وهو أن البغلة لا تحمل . واعتبر هذا بسائر الأمور تجده كذلك
The speculative theologians among the Muslims, despite their disagreement on this, agree that the evidence may apply to a single premise as we mentioned, and what they mentioned about the mule exists in all theoretical matters. For a person may know the particular without knowing the universal, and may know the universal without knowing the particular, just as he may know that this is a mule and not know that the mule does not bear offspring, and may know that the mule does not bear offspring and not know that this is a mule. And it is not necessary that his knowledge of the particular be prior to the universal nor posterior; rather, it may happen in some people that his knowledge of the particular is before the universal, and in some people he knows the universal before the particular. And likewise with determinate individuals. That is because his knowledge that this mule does not bear offspring is like his knowledge that this does not bear offspring, and that this does not bear offspring. So it is not necessary that he know that this determinate individual is like that determinate individual; rather, he may know one of the two determinate individuals and not know the other. And it is not necessary that his knowledge of the determinate individuals be before his knowledge of the universal proposition, nor that he be more knowledgeable of the determinate individuals. And it is not necessary that he be more knowledgeable of the universal general proposition by which he gains knowledge of the ruling of determinate propositions than he is of the description of the determinate propositions. That is, it is not necessary that his knowledge that every mule does not bear offspring be stronger, nor is it necessary that the universal knowledge that provides him knowledge of the determinate individuals be, in itself, prior to a determinate knowledge by which he knows that it is a mule. That is, the knowledge that the mule does not bear offspring does not have to be stronger nor prior in the mind to the knowledge that this animal is a mule; rather, he may be ignorant that it is a mule, just as he may be ignorant that the mule does not bear offspring. So if it is assumed that he knows that the mule does not bear offspring, and his basis for that is the well-known report from people, then that applies to the determinate just as it applies to what is more general than it, such as its application to red mules, black mules, and those whose mother is a donkey. So if it occurs to him that what has a donkey mother and a horse father may bear offspring, it would be said to him: 'Do you not know that this is a mule, and that the mule does not bear offspring?' And if his basis for the mule not bearing offspring is his experience, then experience is not general; rather, he experimented on determinate mules, so what he uses to know the equality of the rest of the mules to them, he uses to know the equality of this mule to them. And consider this by its analogues; it will become clear to you that it is possible to dispense with logical syllogism; rather, its use becomes a prolongation and multiplication of thought, reflection, and speech without benefit. And that the need for premises is according to the state of the person seeking proof. So sometimes he needs one premise, sometimes two, sometimes three, sometimes more than that. And that sometimes he is ignorant that the determinate is a mule, and if that is the case, then whenever he knows that the seeker of proof is this mule, he knows that it does not bear offspring if he has in himself general knowledge encompassing all mules. And if he knows the determinate, that it is a mule, and does not know the absolute, he only needs the general knowledge, which is that the mule does not bear offspring. And consider this in all matters; you will find it to be so.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the necessity of logical syllogism (qiyas mantiqi). He uses the example of the mule to illustrate that knowledge of a particular instance and knowledge of a universal rule are independent. One can know a specific mule without knowing the universal proposition that all mules are barren, and one can know the universal without recognizing the particular. He then extends this to determinate individuals, arguing that the mind does not require a universal premise to know a particular. The practical conclusion is that syllogism is often unnecessary and can be a wasteful elongation of thought. Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that the number of premises needed varies according to the situation of the reasoner, and one may only need a single premise or multiple. The mule example serves as a concrete illustration from everyday experience to undermine the foundational claim of logicians that all knowledge of particulars must be mediated by universal premises.

العلم العام
العلم الخاص
المقدمة
البغلة

C) Knowing that this mule is barren without knowing it is a mule.

Key Takeaway
Particular and universal knowledge are independent; logical syllogism is not always necessary for obtaining knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: Variable Need for Premises
Ibn Taymiyya uses the example of a mule to critique the logicians' claim about premises and universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the number of premises in reasoning depends on the knower's state, and evaluate his critique of the logicians' fixed-premise assumption.

Core Idea
The number of premises needed depends on the knower, not the logical form.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' assumption that syllogistic reasoning requires a fixed number of premises. Using the example of a mule, he shows that the need for premises changes based on what the reasoner already knows. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for Islamic reasoning traditions like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being critiqued
The logicians hold that any valid syllogism must have a fixed number of premises (typically two or more). Ibn Taymiyya rejects this, arguing the number is not fixed.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counter-claim
He states that the need for premises is 'according to the situation of the one seeking proof' (بحسب حال المستدل). This implies variability.
3
Analyze the mule example
If someone knows a specific animal is a mule (particular) and also knows that all mules do not carry (universal), then one premise (the universal) suffices. But if they lack the universal knowledge, they need that premise. The number depends on prior knowledge.
The example: علم أن المستدل هذه بغلة علم أنها لا تحمل إذا كان قد حصل في نفسه علم عام يتناول جميع البغلات.
4
Generalize the principle
Ibn Taymiyya invites the reader to test this on all matters ('اعتبر هذا بسائر الأمور تجده كذلك'), showing the principle is universal: premises are needed only to bridge gaps in knowledge.
5
Evaluate the critique
Assess whether Ibn Taymiyya's critique undermines the logicians' project. His point is that overemphasizing formal structure ignores the epistemic context of the reasoner.
This connects to how Islamic jurists use premises flexibly in legal analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: مثال البغلة ينطبق على سائر النظريات بحسب حال استعماله تطويلاً وتكثيراً للفكر والنظر والكلام بلا فائدة وأن الحاجة إلى المقدمات الحاجة إلى بحسب حال المستدلّ . فقد يحتاج تارةً إلى مقدمة وتارةً إلى ثنتين، وتارة إلى ثلاث، وتارةً إلى أكثر من ذلك . وأنه تارةً يجهل كون المعين بغلة، وإذا كان كذلك فمتى علم أن المستدل هذه بغلة علم أنها لا تحمل إذا كان قد حصل في نفسه علم عام يتناول جميع البغلات . وإذا علم المعين، وهو أنها بغلة ولم يعلم المطلق، لم يحتج إلا إلى علم العام، وهو أن البغلة لا تحمل . واعتبر هذا بسائر الأمور تجده كذلك
The second aspect: The example of the mule applies to all theories in accordance with its application, prolonging and multiplying thought, reflection, and speech without benefit, and that the need for premises is according to the need of the one seeking proof. For sometimes he needs one premise, sometimes two, sometimes three, sometimes more than that. And that sometimes he is ignorant of the specific thing being a mule, and if it is so, then when he knows that this seeker of proof is a mule, he knows that it does not carry, if there has occurred in his mind a universal knowledge that encompasses all mules. And if he knows the specific thing, that it is a mule, but does not know the absolute, he only needs the universal knowledge, which is that the mule does not carry. And consider this regarding all matters, and you will find it so.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' view on the use of premises in syllogistic reasoning. He begins by stating that the example of the mule applies to all theories, and that the overuse of premises only prolongs thought without benefit. He emphasizes that the need for premises depends on the situation of the one seeking proof. Sometimes one premise suffices, sometimes more. He illustrates with the example of a mule: if one knows that a specific animal is a mule (particular knowledge) and also has the universal knowledge that all mules do not carry (a universal premise), then one can deduce that this mule does not carry. But if one only knows the particular (that it is a mule) without being aware of the universal premise, then one only needs the universal knowledge to apply it. This shows that the application of universals to particulars is contingent on prior knowledge of the universal. Ibn Taymiyya uses this to argue that the logicians' method of multiplying premises is often unnecessary and that the real need for premises depends on the individual's state of knowledge. He concludes by inviting the reader to test this on all matters.

العلم العام

Key Takeaway
The number of premises in reasoning is not fixed but depends on the epistemic context of the reasoner, as shown by the mule example.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Inference Classification
Critique of logicians' inference classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' threefold classification of inference and apply his criterion of necessary concomitance to evaluate a proof.

Core Idea
Necessary concomitance, not generality, determines valid inference.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the standard division of inference into qiyas (general to specific), istiqra' (specific to general), and tamthil (one specific to another). He argues that this classification is not exhaustive and introduces the crucial concept of malzum (that which necessarily implies) and lazim (the necessary consequent) as the true basis for valid inference. Let's examine his critique and alternative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' classification
The logicians divide inference into three types: from general to specific (qiyas), from specific to general (istiqra'), and from one specific to another (tamthil). Locate this in the Arabic: 'تارة يكون بالعام على الخاص وهو القياس، وتارةً بالخاص على العام وهو الاستقراء، وتارةً بأحد الخاصين على الآخر وهو التمثيل'.
2
Notice Ibn Taymiyya's claim of corruption
He states that this enumeration and division are corrupt ('فساد هذا الحصر والتقسيم'). What grounds does he give? He points out that there are other types of inference, such as from a specific to a specific, and from equal to equal, which are not captured.
3
Extract his alternative condition
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the proof (dalil) must be a malzum (necessary concomitant) of the judgment (hukm). The relation is not about generality but about necessary implication: the proof must be such that if it holds, the judgment necessarily follows.
He says: 'لا بد في الدليل من أن يكون الدليل يكون ملزوماً للحكم'.
4
Apply to the nabidh example
The example: 'the intoxicating nabidh in dispute is forbidden'. The proof could be 'every intoxicant is forbidden'. Here, the proof (every intoxicant) is more general than the subject (nabidh), but it is a malzum for the judgment. The judgment (forbidden) is the lazim. This shows that the proof can be more general than the subject, but not more general than the judgment itself.
The text breaks off, but the principle is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود هنا الكلام على جنس «القياس» و «الدليل» مطلقاً. وقد بسطنا الكلام على هذا في غير هذا الموضع لَمَّا تكلمنا على ما ذكروه من أن الاستدلال تارة يكون بالعام على الخاص وهو «القياس»، وتارةً بالخاص على العام وهو «الاستقراء»، وتارةً بأحد الخاصين على الآخر وهو «التمثيل»، وبينا فساد هذا الحصر والتقسيم، وفساد ما ذكر في حكم الأقسام فإن من أنواع الاستدلال ما يُستدل فيه بمعين على معين، وبالمساوي على المساوي، سواء كان معيناً أو كلياً. فليس من ضرورة الدليل أن يكون أعم أو أخص بل لا بد في الدليل من أن يكون الدليل يكون ملزوماً للحكم، والملزوم قد يكون أخص من اللازم، وقد يكون متساوياً له، ولا يجوز أن يكون أعم منه . لكن قد يكون أعم من المحكوم عليه الموصوف الذي هو موضوع النتيجة المخبر عنه . فإن المطلوب الذي هو النتيجة إذا كانت هو أن النبيذ المسكر المتنازع فيه حرام
And the intended here is the discussion of the genus of 'qiyas' and 'proof' in absolute terms. We have elaborated on this elsewhere when we discussed what they mentioned: that inference is sometimes from the general to the specific, which is qiyas; sometimes from the specific to the general, which is istiqra'; and sometimes from one specific to another, which is tamthil. And we showed the corruption of this enumeration and division, and the corruption of what was mentioned concerning the rulings of the divisions. For among the types of inference is that in which one infers from a specific to a specific, and from the equal to the equal, whether it is specific or universal. So it is not necessary for the proof to be more general or more specific. Rather, it is necessary in the proof that the proof be a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the judgment; and the necessary concomitant (malzum) may be more specific than the necessary (lazim), and may be equal to it, and it is not permissible for it to be more general than it. But it may be more general than the subject described, which is the subject of the conclusion that is reported. For the intended which is the conclusion, if it is that the intoxicating nabidh in dispute is forbidden
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' classification of inference. He begins by stating his focus: the genus of qiyas and proof in general. He refers to his earlier works where he discussed their claim that inference is divided into three types: qiyas (from general to specific), istiqra' (from specific to general), and tamthil (from one specific to another). He declares this enumeration and division to be corrupt, as well as the rulings they assign to each. He points out that there are other types: inference from a specific to a specific, and from equal to equal, whether the terms are particular or universal. Therefore, it is not necessary for a proof to be more general or more specific than the conclusion. The essential condition for a valid proof is that the proof (dalil) be a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the judgment (hukm). The concomitant (malzum) may be more specific than the necessary (lazim) or equal to it, but it cannot be more general. However, it may be more general than the subject of the conclusion. He gives an incomplete example: the conclusion 'the intoxicating nabidh in dispute is forbidden'—the proof would be something like 'every intoxicant is forbidden,' which is more general than the subject 'nabidh' but more specific than the property 'forbidden'? The example is cut off, but the point is that validity depends on the relation of necessary concomitance, not on generality or specificity.

Key Terms
قياس
استقراء
تمثيل
ملزوم
لازم

D) The proof must be derived from the senses.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an inference depends on the relation of necessary concomitance between proof and judgment, not on the generality or specificity of terms.

Generality Conditions of a Valid Proof
Author establishes the logical conditions for a valid proof regarding generality and specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a proposed proof in a categorical syllogism satisfies the conditions of being more specific than the judgment and more general than the subject.

Core Idea
A valid proof must be more specific than the judgment and more general than the subject.

Ibn Taymiyya critiques the standard classification of reasoning and then presents the precise conditions a proof must meet to be a necessary condition (mulzim) for a judgment. These conditions are essential for constructing sound arguments in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Determine the proposition the argument aims to prove. For example, 'Nabidh is forbidden'. The subject is 'nabidh' and the predicate is 'forbidden'.
2
Distinguish subject and judgment
The subject (mahkum 'alayh) is what the judgment is about. The judgment (hukm) is the predicate applied to the subject. In 'Nabidh is forbidden', nabidh is the subject, forbidden is the judgment.
3
Identify the proof (middle term)
Find the term that links the subject to the judgment. In the example, the proof is 'khamr' because the hadith states 'Every intoxicant is khamr', and nabidh is intoxicating.
4
Check generality relative to subject
The proof must be more general than the subject or equal to it. Compare scopes: 'khamr' covers all intoxicants, while 'nabidh' is a subset of intoxicants, so khamr is more general.
If the proof were more specific than the subject, it would not prove the judgment for all cases of the subject.
5
Check specialty relative to judgment
The proof must be more specific than the judgment or equal to it. Compare: 'khamr' is a type of prohibited thing, but not all prohibited things are khamr, so khamr is more specific than 'forbidden'.
If the proof were more general than the judgment, the judgment could fail even when the proof exists, so entailment is lost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على جنس القياس» و «الدليل» مطلقاً
والمقصود هنا الكلام على جنس «القياس» و «الدليل» مطلقاً. وقد بسطنا الكلام على هذا في غير هذا الموضع لَمَّا تكلمنا على ما ذكروه من أن الاستدلال تارة يكون بالعام على الخاص وهو «القياس»، وتارةً بالخاص على العام وهو «الاستقراء»، وتارةً بأحد الخاصين على الآخر وهو «التمثيل»، وبينا فساد هذا الحصر والتقسيم، وفساد ما ذكر في حكم الأقسام فإن من أنواع الاستدلال ما يُستدل فيه بمعين على معين، وبالمساوي على المساوي، سواء كان معيناً أو كلياً. فليس من ضرورة الدليل أن يكون أعم أو أخص بل لا بد في الدليل من أن يكون الدليل يكون ملزوماً للحكم، والملزوم قد يكون أخص من اللازم، وقد يكون متساوياً له، ولا يجوز أن يكون أعم منه . لكن قد يكون أعم من المحكوم عليه الموصوف الذي هو موضوع النتيجة المخبر عنه .
فإن المطلوب الذي هو النتيجة إذا كانت هو أن النبيذ المسكر المتنازع فيه حرام فاستدل على ذلك بأن النبيذ المسكر خمر بالنص، وهو قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : كل مسكر خمر - رواه مسلم وغيره . فـ الخمر أعم من «النبيذ» المتنازع فيه، وأخص من «الحرام» و «الحرام» هو الحكم، وهو الخبر، وهو الصفة، وهو المطلوب بالدليل، وهو الذي يسمونه الحد الأكبر، ويسمونه «محمول النتيجة» و «النبيذ» هو المحكوم عليه وهو المبتدأ المخبر عنه وهو الموصوف، وهو محل الحكم، وهو الذي يسمونه «الحد الأصغر»، و «موضوع النتيجة». و «الخمر» هو الدليل، وهو «الحد الأوسط» . والمطلوب بالدليل معرفة الحكم، لا معرفة عينه فهذا الدليل يجب أن لا يكون أخص من محل الحكم، بل يكون إما مساوياً له، وإما أعم منه، لأنه لا بد أن يشمل جميع محل الحكم . فإذا كان أخص لم يشمله .
ويجب أن لا يكون أعم من الحكم، بل يكون إما مساوياً له، وإما أخص منه . لأن الحكم لازم له فإذا كان أعم منه أمكن وجوده بدون وجود الحكم، فلا يصلح أن يكون دليلاً مستلزماً له فلا بد في الدليل أن يكون مساوياً للحكم، أو أخص منه، ليكون مستلزماً له ولا بد أن يكون أعم من المحكوم عليه، أو مساوياً له، ليتناول جميع صُوَر المحكوم عليه، وإلا لم يكن دليلاً على حكمه، بل على حكم بعضه .
والناس هنا قد يضطرب أذهانهم في «الدليل»، هل يجب أن لا يكون أعم من الحكم المدلول عليه، أو لا يكون أخص؟ وسبب ذلك أن المدلول عليه قد يُعنى به المحكوم عليه «الحكم» نفسه، وقد يعني به «المحكوم عليه . فإذا أقمنا الدليل على «أن النبيذ حرام» فقد يقال : المدلول عليه هو «النبيذ»، وهذا يجب أن لا يكون أعم من الدليل، بل إما مساوياً، وإما أخص. وقد يقال : المدلول عليه هو الحكم، وهو «حرمة النبيذ». وهذا يجب أن لا يكون أخص من الدليل، بل يكون إما مساوياً له، وإما أعم منه . لأن الحكم لازم للدليل والدليل لازم للمحكوم عليه فلا بد أن يكون المحكوم عليه
Discussion of the genus of syllogism and proof in general. What is intended here is discussion of the genus of 'syllogism' and 'proof' in general. We have elaborated on this elsewhere when we discussed what they mentioned: that inference is sometimes from the general to the specific, which is 'syllogism'; sometimes from the specific to the general, which is 'induction'; and sometimes from one specific to another, which is 'analogy'. And we showed the corruption of this enumeration and division, and the corruption of what was mentioned regarding the rule of the divisions, for among the types of inference are those in which one infers from a specific to a specific, and from the equivalent to the equivalent, whether specific or universal. So it is not necessary for the proof to be more general or more specific; rather it is necessary for the proof to be a necessary condition (mulzim) for the judgment, and the necessary condition may be more specific than the necessary consequence, or equal to it, and it is not permissible for it to be more general than it. But it may be more general than the subject described which is the subject of the conclusion that is reported. For the desired conclusion, if it is that the intoxicating nabidh in dispute is forbidden, then one infers that by the fact that intoxicating nabidh is khamr by text, and it is the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Every intoxicant is khamr' – narrated by Muslim and others. So 'khamr' is more general than 'nabidh' in dispute, and more specific than 'forbidden'. And 'forbidden' is the judgment, and it is the predicate, and it is the attribute, and it is what is sought by the proof, and it is what they call the major term, and they call it the 'predicate of the conclusion'. And 'nabidh' is the subject of the judgment, and it is the subject of the predicate, and it is the described, and it is the locus of the judgment, and it is what they call the 'minor term', and the 'subject of the conclusion'. And 'khamr' is the proof, and it is the 'middle term'. And what is sought by the proof is knowledge of the judgment, not knowledge of its essence. So this proof must not be more specific than the locus of the judgment, but rather either equal to it or more general than it, because it must encompass all of the locus of the judgment. If it were more specific, it would not encompass it. And it must not be more general than the judgment, but rather either equal to it or more specific than it, because the judgment is a necessary consequence of it; if it were more general than it, its existence would be possible without the existence of the judgment, so it would not be suitable as a proof that necessitates it. So it is necessary for the proof to be equal to the judgment or more specific than it, so that it necessitates it; and it must be more general than the subject or equal to it, to cover all cases of the subject; otherwise it would not be a proof for its judgment, but rather for the judgment of some of it. And people's minds may be confused here regarding the 'proof': is it required that it not be more general than the judgment indicated, or that it not be more specific? The reason for that is that 'the indicated' may mean the subject itself, or it may mean the judgment. So if we establish the proof that 'nabidh is forbidden', it may be said: the indicated is 'nabidh', and this must not be more general than the proof, but rather equal or more specific. And it may be said: the indicated is the judgment, namely 'the prohibition of nabidh'. And this must not be more specific than the proof, but rather equal to it or more general than it. Because the judgment is a necessary consequence of the proof, and the proof is a necessary condition for the subject; so the subject must be
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the nature of proof (dalil) within the context of qiyas (syllogism). Ibn Taymiyya begins by critiquing the standard classification of reasoning into syllogism (general to specific), induction (specific to general), and analogy (one specific to another). He argues that this division is flawed because there are inferences from specific to specific and from equivalent to equivalent. The key condition for a valid proof is that it must be a necessary condition (mulzim) for the judgment; it can be more specific or equal to the judgment, but never more general. It can be more general or equal to the subject, but never more specific. He illustrates this with the example of intoxicating nabidh (a fermented drink) being forbidden because it is khamr (wine) based on the hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr.' He then maps the terms: 'khamr' is the middle term (had al-awsat), 'nabidh' is the minor term (subject of conclusion), and 'forbidden' (haram) is the major term (predicate of conclusion). He explains that the proof (khamr) is more general than the subject (nabidh) and more specific than the judgment (forbidden). He then refines the condition: if the proof were more specific than the subject, it would not cover all cases; if it were more general than the judgment, the judgment could be absent even when the proof exists, so it would not entail the judgment. Finally, he addresses confusion about whether the proof must not be more general than the 'indicated' (madlul 'alayh). This confusion arises because 'indicated' can refer either to the subject (object of the judgment) or to the judgment itself. If it refers to the subject, the proof must not be more general than it; if it refers to the judgment, the proof must not be more specific. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: the relationship between proof, subject, and judgment is one of necessary entailment, with constraints on generality and specificity.

محكوم عليه
حكم
حد أوسط
ملزوم

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical proof (middle term) must be more general than the subject and more specific than the judgment to ensure necessity and coverage.

The Syllogism

Ibn Taymiyya's Classification of Qiyas: Qiyas al-Tadakhul
Ibn Taymiyya classifies qiyas and illustrates qiyas al-tadakhul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of qiyas according to Ibn Taymiyya and the structure of qiyas al-tadakhul.

Core Idea
In qiyas al-tadakhul, the minor term is included in the middle, and the middle in the major.

Ibn Taymiyya presents an alternative classification of syllogisms based on the relationship between terms. This classification is grounded in the types of propositions: categorical, conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive. He then elaborates on the first type, qiyas al-tadakhul, using a concrete example from Islamic law to show how inclusion works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of qiyas
Ibn Taymiyya lists qiyas al-tadakhul (inclusion), qiyas al-talazum (concomitance), and qiyas al-ta‘anud (opposition). Note that this classification differs from the standard figures.
The types correspond to categorical, conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive propositions respectively.
2
Understand the basis of classification
The classification is based on the type of proposition: categorical for inclusion, conditional conjunctive for concomitance, conditional disjunctive for opposition.
Mixed syllogisms arise from combinations of these.
3
Focus on qiyas al-tadakhul: identify the three terms
In the example 'Every intoxicant is wine, and every wine is forbidden', identify the minor (intoxicant), middle (wine), and major (forbidden).
The minor must be included in the middle, either as more specific or equal.
4
Verify the inclusion relationship
Check that 'intoxicant' is included under 'wine' (since wine is a type of intoxicant) and 'wine' under 'forbidden' (since wine is forbidden). This forms a chain.
Inclusion can be like 'man in animal' (more general) or like 'man, rational, risible' (mutually entailing).
5
Apply the inclusion principle to fiqh
Ibn Taymiyya notes that if someone drinks wine repeatedly, one hadd penalty suffices because the later acts are included in the earlier ruling.
This shows how logical inclusion mirrors legal inclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع أن يقال : «القياس ثلاثة أنواع : قياس التداخل، وقياس التلازم، وقياس التعاند، باعتبار القضايا الحملية والشرطية المتصلة والشرطية المنفصلة . ومن ذلك تعرف المختلطات. فنقول مثلاً في قياس التداخل : له ثلاثة حدود - الحد الأصغر، والحد الأوسط، والحد الأكبر. إذا قال : كل مسكر خمر، وكل خمر حرام» فـ «المسكر» مثلاً هو الحد الأصغر، و «الخمر» الأوسط، و «الحرام» الأكبر. والأصغر لا بد أن يكون داخلاً في الأوسط، لأنه أخص منه أو مساوياً له، والشيء يدخل في أكثر منه وفي نظيره كما يدخل الإنسان في الحيوان فإن الحيوان أعم؛ وكذلك الإنسان والناطق والضحاك متلازمة، فكل منها يتناول الآخر. وكذلك التداخل في الأحكام الشرعية، فإن من شرب الخمر، ثم شرب ثم شرب، كفاه حد واحد.
The fourth aspect: it is said: 'The syllogism is of three types: syllogism of inclusion (qiyas al-tadakhul), syllogism of concomitance (qiyas al-talazum), and syllogism of opposition (qiyas al-ta‘anud), considering categorical, conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive propositions. From that, mixed syllogisms are known. For example, in the syllogism of inclusion: it has three terms - the minor term, the middle term, and the major term. If one says: 'Every intoxicant is wine, and every wine is forbidden', then 'intoxicant' for example is the minor term, 'wine' is the middle, and 'forbidden' is the major. The minor must be included in the middle, because it is more specific or equal to it, and a thing is included in what is more general than it and in what is similar to it, just as man is included in animal, for animal is more general; likewise, man, rational, and risible are mutually entailing, so each covers the other. And similarly, the inclusion in legal rulings: for whoever drinks wine, then drinks, then drinks, one single hadd penalty suffices for him.'
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique, where he classifies qiyas (syllogism) into three types: qiyas al-tadakhul (syllogism of inclusion, where the minor is included in the middle), qiyas al-talazum (syllogism of concomitance, based on mutual entailment), and qiyas al-ta‘anud (syllogism of opposition, based on mutual exclusion). This classification is based on the types of propositions: categorical (hamliyya), conditional conjunctive (shartiyya muttasila), and conditional disjunctive (shartiyya munfasila). Mixed syllogisms arise from combinations. Ibn Taymiyya then illustrates the first type, qiyas al-tadakhul, with a concrete example: 'Every intoxicant is wine, and every wine is forbidden.' Here, the minor term 'intoxicant' (muskir) is included in the middle 'wine' (khamr) because it is more specific; the middle is included in the major 'forbidden' (haram). He notes that the minor must be included in the middle, being either more specific or equal to it. This is like the inclusion of 'man' in 'animal' because animal is more general. He then gives an example from fiqh: if someone drinks wine repeatedly, one hadd penalty suffices, illustrating the concept of inclusion (tadakhul) in legal rulings. The point is that once the middle term is fully conceived, the syllogism becomes unnecessary; the classification helps understand different inferential relationships.

الحد الأوسط

The Syllogism

Three Types of Syllogism and Qiyas al-Tadakhul
Author outlines three types of qiyas and explains tadakhul with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of qiyas (tadakhul, talazum, ta'anud) and analyze a categorical syllogism for term inclusion in qiyas al-tadakhul.

Core Idea
The three-fold classification of qiyas is based on proposition type, and tadakhul requires the minor to be included in the middle.

Ibn Taymiyya presents a classification of syllogisms into three types, each tied to a specific propositional form: categorical (hamliyya), conditional conjunctive (shartiyya muttasila), and conditional disjunctive (shartiyya munfasila). He then unpacks qiyas al-tadakhul using a classic fiqh example. Understanding this classification helps you see how syllogistic reasoning is applied across different logical forms. In this lesson, we will break down the three types and practice analyzing a categorical syllogism for proper inclusion of terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of qiyas
Locate the three names (tadakhul, talazum, ta'anud) in the passage and note their associated proposition types: categorical, conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive.
These correspond to the three main branches of the syllogism in Aristotelian logic.
2
Focus on qiyas al-tadakhul
Note that tadakhul uses categorical (hamliyya) propositions only. The passage gives the example: 'Every intoxicant is wine, every wine is forbidden'.
3
Extract the three terms from the example
Identify the minor term (al-had al-asghar) = 'intoxicant', the middle term (al-had al-awsat) = 'wine', and the major term (al-had al-akbar) = 'forbidden'.
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion; the major is the predicate.
4
Verify the inclusion condition
Check that the minor term is included in the middle term, i.e., it is either more specific (akhas) or equal (musawi). Here, 'intoxicant' is more specific than 'wine' – all intoxicants are wine in the context of the example.
Ibn Taymiyya uses the analogy of human being included in animal.
5
Derive the conclusion
Because the minor is included in the middle, the conclusion 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' follows validly. This is a first-figure categorical syllogism (Barbara).
The conclusion is explicit: 'The required conclusion is: Every intoxicant is forbidden.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْوَجْهُ الرَّابِعُ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «الْقِيَاسُ ثَلَاثَةُ أَنْوَاعٍ: قِيَاسُ التَّدَاخُلِ، وَقِيَاسُ التَّلَازُمِ، وَقِيَاسُ التَّعَانُدِ، بِاعْتِبَارِ الْقَضَايَا الْحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ الْمُتَّصِلَةِ وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ. وَمِنْ ذَلِكَ تُعْرَفُ الْمُخْتَلِطَاتُ. فَنَقُولُ مَثَلًا فِي قِيَاسِ التَّدَاخُلِ: لَهُ ثَلَاثَةُ حُدُودٍ - الْحَدُّ الْأَصْغَرُ، وَالْحَدُّ الْأَوْسَطُ، وَالْحَدُّ الْأَكْبَرُ. إِذَا قَالَ: كُلُّ مُسْكِرٍ خَمْرٌ، وَكُلُّ خَمْرٍ حَرَامٌ. فَـ «الْمُسْكِرُ» مَثَلًا هُوَ الْحَدُّ الْأَصْغَرُ، وَ «الْخَمْرُ» الْأَوْسَطُ، وَ «الْحَرَامُ» الْأَكْبَرُ. وَالْأَصْغَرُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ دَاخِلًا فِي الْأَوْسَطِ، لِأَنَّهُ أَخَصُّ مِنْهُ أَوْ مُسَاوٍ لَهُ، وَالشَّيْءُ يَدْخُلُ فِي أَكْثَرَ مِنْهُ وَفِي نَظِيرِهِ كَمَا يَدْخُلُ الْإِنْسَانُ فِي الْحَيَوَانِ فَإِنَّ الْحَيَوَانَ أَعَمُّ؛ وَكَذَلِكَ الْإِنْسَانُ وَالنَّاطِقُ وَالضَّحَّاكُ مُتَلَازِمَةٌ، فَكُلٌّ مِنْهَا يَتَنَاوَلُ الْآخَرَ. وَكَذَلِكَ التَّدَاخُلُ فِي الْأَحْكَامِ الشَّرْعِيَّةِ، فَإِنَّ مَنْ شَرِبَ الْخَمْرَ، ثُمَّ شَرِبَ ثُمَّ شَرِبَ، كَفَاهُ حَدٌّ وَاحِدٌ. وَالنَّتِيجَةُ الْمَطْلُوبَةُ هِيَ «كُلُّ مُسْكِرٍ حَرَامٌ».
The fourth aspect is to say: 'The syllogism (qiyas) is of three types: syllogism of overlapping (tadakhul), syllogism of concomitance (talazum), and syllogism of opposition (ta'anud), based on categorical propositions, conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive. And from that, the mixed [syllogisms] are known. For example, in syllogism of overlapping: it has three terms - the minor term, the middle term, and the major term. When he says: "Every intoxicant is wine, and every wine is forbidden," then 'intoxicant' for example is the minor term, 'wine' is the middle, and 'forbidden' is the major. The minor must be included in the middle, because it is more specific than it or equal to it. A thing is included in what is more general than it and in its equivalent, just as 'human' is included in 'animal' because animal is more general. Similarly, 'human', 'rational', and 'laughing' are mutually concomitant, so each of them covers the other. Likewise, overlapping in legal rulings: for one who drinks wine, then drinks again, then drinks again, one single prescribed punishment suffices for him. And the required conclusion is: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.'
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is an exposition by Ibn Taymiyya of the classification of qiyas (syllogism) as held by logicians. He states that qiyas is divided into three types based on the type of propositions used: qiyas al-tadakhul (overlapping), which uses categorical (hamliyya) propositions; qiyas al-talazum (concomitance), using conditional conjunctive (shartiyya muttasila); and qiyas al-ta'anud (opposition), using conditional disjunctive (shartiyya munfasila). Mixed syllogisms (mukhtalatat) are derived from these. He then elaborates on qiyas al-tadakhul with the classic example: 'Every intoxicant is wine, every wine is forbidden, therefore every intoxicant is forbidden.' He identifies the terms: minor = intoxicant, middle = wine, major = forbidden. The condition for a valid tadakhul syllogism is that the minor term must be included in the middle term, meaning it is either more specific or equal to it. Ibn Taymiyya illustrates this by noting that 'human' is included in 'animal' (the species-genus relation), and also by citing terms that are co-extensive like 'human', 'rational', and 'laughing' (mutalazima). He then applies the principle to Islamic law: repeated drinking of wine incurs only a single hadd punishment because the acts overlap in effect. The passage thus provides a clear definition and example of the first type of syllogism, grounding logical concepts in familiar Islamic legal examples.

Checkpoint
In qiyas al-tadakhul, what condition must hold between the minor and middle terms?
A) They must be identical.
B) The minor must be more general than the middle.
C) The minor must be included in the middle (more specific or equal).
D) The middle must be included in the minor.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a tadakhul syllogism depends on the minor term's inclusion in the middle term, and this classification enables precise reasoning in both logic and fiqh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a qiyas al-tadakhul using a fiqh ruling about prayer: 'Every prayer is an act of worship, and every act of worship requires intention; therefore every prayer requires intention.' Identify the minor, middle, and major terms, and verify the inclusion condition.
?
Given the premises 'Every transaction involving riba is prohibited' and 'Every sale of gold for gold with delay is riba', determine whether the conclusion 'Every sale of gold for gold with delay is prohibited' follows as a valid tadakhul. Explain why or why not.
