Three Types of Qiyas: Inclusive, Analogical, and Causal
Ibn Taymiyya classifies qiyas into three types: shumul, tamthil, and ta'lil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qiyas al-shumul, qiyas al-tamthil, and qiyas al-ta'lil and identify which type of reasoning is used in a given Islamic legal argument.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya shows three ways to know a ruling: by explicit inclusion, by analogy to a precedent, or by direct causal reasoning.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya classifies the types of qiyas (reasoning) relevant to juristic deduction. He distinguishes three methods: the inclusive syllogism (shumul), analogy (tamthil), and causal reasoning (ta'lil). Understanding these distinctions helps us analyze how scholars derive rulings from sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling being established
Note the example: prohibition of intoxicants. The ruling is 'this is forbidden'.
2
Recognize qiyas al-shumul
This uses a middle term explicitly, e.g., 'this is wine' + 'wine is forbidden' → 'this is forbidden'. The conclusion follows from inclusion.
It is a categorical syllogism.
3
Recognize qiyas al-tamthil
One sees that an intoxicant shares the same basis (manat) of prohibition as wine, so they are equated. No explicit middle term is needed.
This is the typical juristic analogy.
4
Recognize qiyas al-ta'lil
One perceives the harm (mafsada) directly in the new case and rules prohibition to ward it off, without requiring a specific original case.
It resembles causal reasoning in usul.
5
Compare the three
Shumul is direct inclusion; tamthil requires an original (asl); ta'lil may dispense with a specific asl and rely on the cause directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : من المعلوم أن معرفة القلب بثبوت المحمول للموضوع، وهو ثبوت الصفة للموصوف، وهو ثبوت الحكم المسئول عنه مثل حرمة المسكر : قد تحصل بواسطة هذا الحد، وهو أن يعلم أن هذا خمر مع أن الخمر حرام، وهذا قياس الشمول. وقد تحصل بغير هذا مثل أن يرى أن المسكر مساو لخمر العنب في مناط التحريم، فيسوى بينهما في التحريم، وهذا قياس التمثيل. وقد تحصل بأن يرى فيه المفسدة التي في الخمر، فيحكم بالتحريم لدرء تلك المفسدة، وهذا قياس التعليل. ويسمى قياس التمثيل وقياس التعليل يشملهما جنس القياس، لكن القياس قد يحتاج في إثبات الحكم في الفرع إلى أصل معين، فيلحق الفرع به، إما لإبداء الجامع، وإما لإلغاء الفارق. فإن إبداء الجامع، وهو علة الحكم في الأصل، يسمى العلة. وأما ما يدل على العلة، وهو قياس الدلالة، فهذا صار قياس تمثيل وتعليل معاً. وإن قاس بإلغاء الفارق، وهو أن يبين له أنه ليس بينهما فرق مؤثر وإن لم يعلم عين العلة، فهذا قياس تمثيل لا تعليل. وقد يقوم دليل على أن الوصف الفلاني مستلزم للحكم وإن لم يعرف له أصل معين، وهذا قياس تعليل، وهو يشبه قياس الشمول. وقياس الشمول وقياس التمثيل متلازمان فكل ما ذكر بهذا القياس يمكن ذكره بهذا القياس. فإن قياس الشمول لا بد فيه من حد أوسط مكرر، وذاك هو مناط الحكم في قياس التمثيل. وهو القدر المشترك، وهو الجامع بين الأصل والفرع. مثال ذلك إذا قيل : النبيذ حرام قياساً على الخمر، لأن فيه الشدة المطربة، وهذه هي العلة في الخمر.
So we say: It is known that the heart's knowledge of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject, which is the affirmation of the attribute to the described, which is the affirmation of the ruling asked about, such as the prohibition of intoxicants: it may be obtained by means of this middle term, such that he knows that this is wine and that wine is forbidden, and this is qiyas al-shumul (inclusive syllogism). And it may be obtained otherwise, such that he sees that the intoxicant is equal to grape wine in the basis (manat) of prohibition, so he makes them equal in prohibition, and this is qiyas al-tamthil (analogical syllogism). And it may be obtained such that he sees in it the same harm (mafsada) that is in wine, so he rules prohibition to ward off that harm, and this is qiyas al-ta'lil (causal syllogism). And qiyas al-tamthil and qiyas al-ta'lil are both encompassed by the genus of qiyas, but qiyas may need in establishing the ruling in the branch a specific original case (asl), and then the branch is annexed to it, either by displaying the commonality (jami') or by eliminating the difference (ilgha' al-fariq). For displaying the commonality, which is the cause (illa) of the ruling in the original, is called 'illa. And as for what indicates the cause, which is qiyas al-dalala (indicative syllogism), this becomes qiyas tamthil and ta'lil together. And if one reasons by eliminating the difference, which is to show that there is no effective difference between them even if one does not know the exact cause, this is qiyas tamthil without ta'lil. And a proof may be established that a certain description necessarily entails the ruling even if no specific original is known, and this is qiyas ta'lil, and it resembles qiyas al-shumul. And qiyas al-shumul and qiyas al-tamthil are interdependent: everything mentioned by one qiyas can be mentioned by the other. For qiyas al-shumul must have a middle term that is repeated, and that is the basis (manat) of the ruling in qiyas al-tamthil. It is the common aspect (al-qadr al-mushtarak), which is the commonality (jami') between the original and the branch. An example is when one says: Nabidh is forbidden by analogy to wine, because it contains intoxicating strength, and that is the cause in wine.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a classification by Ibn Taymiyya of the types of qiyas (reasoning) relevant to juristic deduction. He begins by reaffirming that the heart's knowledge of a ruling (e.g., prohibition of intoxicants) can be obtained in three ways. First, qiyas al-shumul (syllogism of inclusion), also known as categorical syllogism, where one knows explicitly that 'this is wine' and 'wine is forbidden', thus concluding 'this is forbidden'. This is the standard Aristotelian syllogism. Second, qiyas al-tamthil (analogical syllogism), where one sees that an intoxicant shares the same basis of prohibition (manat al-tahrim) as grape wine, so they are equated. Third, qiyas al-ta'lil (causal syllogism), where one perceives the same harm (mafsada) that calls for prohibition, not needing a specific precedent. He then explains that the latter two are species of the genus 'qiyas'. Analogy (tamthil) requires a specific original (asl) to which the branch (far') is annexed either by identifying the common cause (jami'/illa) or by eliminating differences (ilgha' al-fariq). When one explicitly identifies the cause, it becomes qiyas tamthil and ta'lil simultaneously (since stating the cause is also causal). If one merely eliminates differences without knowing the exact cause, it is pure tamthil. However, if a proof shows that a description entails a ruling without reference to a specific original, that is qiyas ta'lil and it resembles shumul because it classifies the ruling. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya asserts that qiyas al-shumul and qiyas al-tamthil are interdependent: every shumul syllogism has a middle term (hadd awsat) that corresponds to the shared 'illa in tamthil. For instance, in the prohibition of nabidh (date wine) by analogy to khamr (grape wine), the middle term in a categorical form would be 'every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant' – the middle term 'intoxicant' is the same as the manat (basis) in the analogy. Thus, the two methods converge.

Key Terms
قياس الشمول
قياس التمثيل
قياس التعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Qiyas al-tamthil
C) Qiyas al-ta'lil
D) Qiyas al-dalala

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya identifies three distinct ways to affirm a ruling: direct inclusion (shumul), analogy (tamthil), and causal reasoning (ta'lil).

The Syllogism

8 min
Interdependence of Inclusive Syllogism and Analogy
Ibn Taymiyya explains the interdependence of qiyas al-shumul and qiyas al-tamthil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to demonstrate how the middle term in an inclusive syllogism corresponds to the basis (manat) in an analogy.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that every inclusive syllogism can be recast as an analogy, and vice versa.

After classifying the three types, Ibn Taymiyya explains the fundamental unity between inclusive syllogism (shumul) and analogy (tamthil). He shows that the middle term in shumul is exactly the shared basis (manat) in tamthil. This insight bridges formal logic and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term in a shumul argument
Take 'All intoxicants are forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden'. The middle term is 'intoxicant'.
2
Identify the manat in a tamthil argument
In 'Nabidh is forbidden by analogy to wine because it contains intoxicating strength', the manat (basis) is 'intoxicating strength' (shidda mutriba).
3
Observe they are the same
Ibn Taymiyya says the middle term in shumul is the manat in tamthil. 'Intoxicant' and 'intoxicating strength' refer to the same property causing prohibition.
The middle term is the common aspect (qadr mushtarak) between original and branch.
4
Check the example: nabidh
The passage explicitly gives the example: 'Nabidh is forbidden by analogy to wine because it contains intoxicating strength, and that is the cause in wine.' This shows the equivalence.
5
Generalize
Any valid analogy can be turned into a categorical syllogism with the manat as the middle term. Conversely, any categorical syllogism with a middle term that is a common property can be expressed as an analogy.
Interdependence (talazum) means each can be transformed into the other.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Three Types of Qiyas in Ibn Taymiyya's Epistemology
Ibn Taymiyya establishes the mutual dependence of categorical and analogical reasoning and refutes logicians' exclusivity claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish qiyas al-shamul, qiyas al-tamthil, and qiyas al-ta'lil and identify their components in an Islamic legal argument.

Core Idea
The genus 'qiyas' comprises three species: categorical, analogical, and causal.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya identifies three distinct ways by which the heart comes to know a judgment such as the prohibition of an intoxicant. Each corresponds to a type of qiyas: shumul (inclusion), tamthil (representation), and ta'lil (causation). Understanding these types is essential for grasping his broader argument about their equivalence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the judgment and subject
In the example, the judgment is prohibition (hurmah) and the subject is nabidh (date wine). Notice how the passage begins with the heart's knowledge of affirming the predicate to the subject.
2
Recognize the three methods of knowing
The passage lists three ways: via a middle term (shumul), via equality/similarity (tamthil), and via perceiving the corruptive attribute (ta'lil). Locate each in the Arabic.
The third method is qiyas al-ta'lil, though the passage also mentions subtypes.
3
Trace each method to the corresponding qiyas type
Match each method to the named qiyas: (1) knowing by the middle term = qiyas al-shamul; (2) knowing by equality = qiyas al-tamthil; (3) knowing by the corruptive attribute = qiyas al-ta'lil.
Ibn Taymiyya also includes qiyas al-dalala which combines tamthil and ta'lil.
4
Distinguish the subtypes within tamthil
Note the difference between 'showing the common factor' (ibda' al-jami') which is ta'lil, and 'eliminating the difference' (ilgha' al-fariq) which is tamthil but not ta'lil. Find where the passage discusses this.
5
Apply the typology to the nabidh example
The passage gives formulations: 'Nabidh is forbidden by analogy to khamr because it contains intoxicating potency' (tamthil/ta'lil) and 'Nabidh is intoxicating, every intoxicant is forbidden' (shumul). Identify which type each represents.

قياس الشمول». وقد يحصل بغير هذا مثل أن يرى أن المسكر مساو لخمر العنب في مناط التحريم، فيسوَّى بينهما في التحريم، وهذا قياس التمثيل» [٣٣٩] وقد يحصل بأن يرى فيه المفسدة التي في الخمر، فيحكم بالتحريم لدرء تلك المفسدة، وهذا «قياس التعليل» (1) «قیاس» و «قياس التمثيل و قياس التعليل» يشملهما جنسُ القياس، لكن القياس قد يحتاج في إثبات الحكم في الفرع إلى أصل معين، فيُلحق الفرع به، إما لإبداء الجامع، وإما متلازمان فكل ما ذكر بهذا القياس يمكن ذكره بهذا القياس فإن قياس الشمول لا بد فيه من حد أوسط مكرر، وذاك هو مناط الحكم في قياس التمثيل. وهو القدر المشترك، وهو الجامع بين الأصل والفرع. مثال ذلك إذا قيل : النبيذ حرام قياساً على الخمر، لأن فيه الشدَّة المُطْرِبة ، / وهذه هي العلة في التحريم، أو لأنه مسكر والمسكر هو علة التحريم وبيَّن ذلك بدليله كان قياساً صحيحاً. وإذا قيل : النبيذ مسكر ، وكل مسكر حرام»، أو «النبيذ فيه الشدة المطربة، وما فيه الشدة المطربة فهو حرام ، فهذا أيضاً صحيح وكل ما أمكن أن [٣٤٠] يستدل به على صحة المقدمة الكبرى أمكن أن يستدل به على كون الوصف المشترك علة للحكم في الأصل . وكل ما أمكن أن يستدل به على الصغرى فإنه يستدل به على ثبوت الوصف في الفرع . ثم إن كان ذاك الدليل قطعياً فهو «قطعي» في القياسين، وإن كان ظنياً فهو «ظني» في القياسين. وأما دعوى من يدعي من المنطقيين وأتباعهم أن اليقين إنما يحصل بـ قياس الشمول دون قياس التمثيل فهو قول في غاية الفساد. وهو قول من لم يتصور حقيقة القياسين كما قد بسط في موضعه . وقد يعلم الحكم المطلوب بنص على أن كل مسكر حرام، كما قد ثبت هذا قد يعلم الحكم بنص الشارع الحديث في الصحاح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. وإذا كان كذلك لم يتعين قياس الشمول لإفادة الحكم، بل ولا قياس من الأقيسة، فإنه قد يُعلم بلا قياس . وإذا علم بقياس الشمول فكل ما يعلم بقياس الشمول فإنه يعلم بقياس التمثيل أيضاً، كما تقدم. ويجعل الحد الأوسط هو الجامع بين الأصل والفرع. والدليل الذي يقيمه صاحب قياس الشمول على صحة المقدمة الكبرى الكلية يقيمه صاحب قياس التمثيل على علية الوصف. وإن الجامع، وهو الوصف المشترك الذي الحد الأوسط في قياس الشمول»، هو مستلزم للحكم، وهو علية في الأصل، كما يقيمه في ذاك على أن الحد الأكبر لازم للحد الأوسط. فالحد الأكبر في قياس الشمول هو الحكم في قياس التمثيل، والحد الأوسط هو الجامع المشترك، ويسمى المناط»، والحد الأصغر (۱) هو الفرع. ويمتاز قياس التمثيل بأن فيه ذكر أصل يكون نظيراً للفرع الذي [٣٤١] هو الحد الأصغر، وقياس الشمول ليس فيه هذا فصار في قياس التمثيل ما في قياس الشمول
We say: It is known that the heart's knowledge of the affirmation of the predicate for the subject—which is the affirmation of the attribute for the described, which is the affirmation of the requested judgment, such as the prohibition of intoxicants—may be obtained by means of this middle term, which is to know that this is khamr while khamr is forbidden, and this is the 'syllogism of inclusion'. And it may be obtained by other than this, such as seeing that the intoxicant is equal to grape wine in the locus of prohibition, so they are equated in prohibition, and this is the 'syllogism of representation'. And it may be obtained by seeing in it the corruption present in khamr, so he rules by prohibition to repel that corruption, and this is the 'syllogism of causation'. The genus 'syllogism' encompasses 'syllogism of inclusion', 'syllogism of representation', and 'syllogism of causation', but the syllogism may need, in proving the judgment in the branch, a specific root, so he attaches the branch to it, either by showing the common factor or by eliminating the difference. For showing the common factor, which is the cause of the judgment in the root, is called 'cause'. As for what indicates the cause, which is the 'syllogism of indication', this becomes both representation and causation together. And if he measures by eliminating the difference—that is, by showing that there is no effective difference between them even if the specific cause is unknown—this is a syllogism of representation, not causation. And sometimes evidence establishes that a certain attribute entails a judgment even if no specific root is known, and this is a syllogism of causation, and it resembles the 'syllogism of inclusion'. The syllogism of inclusion and the syllogism of representation are mutually concomitant: everything mentioned by this syllogism can be mentioned by that syllogism. For the syllogism of inclusion necessarily has a repeated middle term, and that is the locus of judgment in the syllogism of representation. It is the common factor, which is the link between the root and the branch. For example, if it is said: nabidh is forbidden by analogy to khamr, because it contains the intoxicating potency, and this is the cause of prohibition, or because it is intoxicating and intoxicants are the cause of prohibition, and he explains that with its evidence, it is a valid syllogism. And if it is said: 'Nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden', or 'Nabidh contains intoxicating potency, and whatever contains intoxicating potency is forbidden', this is also valid. And everything by which one can infer the correctness of the major premise can be used to infer that the common attribute is the cause of the judgment in the root. And everything by which one can infer the minor premise can be used to infer the existence of the attribute in the branch. Then, if that evidence is certain, it is 'certain' in both syllogisms; if it is probable, it is 'probable' in both. As for the claim of some logicians and their followers that certainty is only obtained through the syllogism of inclusion, not through the syllogism of representation—this is an extremely corrupt statement. It is the statement of one who has not grasped the reality of the two syllogisms, as has been elaborated elsewhere. And the requested judgment may be known by a text that 'every intoxicant is forbidden', as has been established by the hadith in the Sahih collections from the Prophet (peace be upon him). If that is the case, then the syllogism of inclusion is not necessary for conveying the judgment, nor indeed any syllogism, for it may be known without a syllogism. And if it is known by the syllogism of inclusion, then everything known by the syllogism of inclusion is also known by the syllogism of representation, as previously mentioned. The middle term is made the common factor between the root and the branch. The evidence that the proponent of the syllogism of inclusion adduces for the correctness of the universal major premise is adduced by the proponent of the syllogism of representation for the causality of the attribute. And that the common factor—the shared attribute that is the middle term in the syllogism of inclusion—entails the judgment, and is the cause in the root, just as he adduces in that that the major term is necessary for the middle term. Thus the major term in the syllogism of inclusion is the judgment in the syllogism of representation; the middle term is the shared common factor, and it is called the 'locus'; and the minor term is the branch. The syllogism of representation is distinguished by containing a mention of a root that is analogous to the branch which is the minor term, whereas the syllogism of inclusion does not contain this. Thus in the syllogism of representation there is what is in the syllogism of inclusion.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin is a critical exposition of the relationship between two types of qiyas: qiyas al-shamul (syllogism of inclusion, i.e., categorical syllogism) and qiyas al-tamthil (syllogism of representation, i.e., analogical reasoning). The author refutes the claim of the logicians that only the categorical syllogism yields certainty, while analogy yields only probability. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that the heart's knowledge of a judgment (e.g., the prohibition of an intoxicant) can be obtained in three ways: via a middle term indicating the inclusion of the subject under a universal rule (this is qiyas al-shamul), via seeing that the intoxicant shares with wine the locus of prohibition (this is qiyas al-tamthil), or via seeing a specific corruptive attribute that is the cause of prohibition (this is qiyas al-ta'lil, which he also discusses). He notes that all these are species of the genus 'qiyas'. He explains the subtypes: showing the common factor (the cause) is ta'lil; indicating the cause is qiyas al-dalala, which combines tamthil and ta'lil; and eliminating the difference (ilgha' al-fariq) is tamthil but not ta'lil. He then asserts the mutual concomitance (talazum) between qiyas al-shamul and qiyas al-tamthil: every argument that can be presented in one form can be presented in the other. The middle term of the categorical syllogism is precisely the common factor (jami') between the root (asl) and branch (far') in analogy. He illustrates with the example of nabidh being forbidden by analogy to khamr. The same evidence that proves the major premise in the categorical syllogism also proves the causality of the attribute in the root. Thus certainty or probability belongs to both equally, depending on the evidence. Ibn Taymiyya condemns the logicians' claim that certainty is exclusive to categorical syllogism as extremely corrupt. He adds that the judgment may also be known by direct textual evidence (nass) without any syllogism. Furthermore, if it is known via categorical syllogism, it can also be known via analogy by setting the middle term as the common factor. In the analogy, the root is mentioned explicitly, while in the categorical syllogism it is not, but otherwise the logical structure is identical. This passage is a sophisticated argument demonstrating that analogical reasoning, when properly grounded, is epistemologically equivalent to categorical syllogism, and that the logicians' privileging of the latter is unfounded.

Key Terms
قياس الشمول
قياس التمثيل
قياس التعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Qiyas al-shamul
B) Qiyas al-tamthil (ilgha' al-fariq)
C) Qiyas al-ta'lil
D) Qiyas al-dalala

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya identifies three species of qiyas—shumul, tamthil, and ta'lil—which are distinguished by how the judgment is known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. In a fiqh argument, identify which type of qiyas is being used: shumul, tamthil, or ta'lil. Use the nabidh example as a model.
?

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
The Mutual Concomitance and Epistemological Parity of Categorical and Analogical Reasoning
Ibn Taymiyya establishes the mutual dependence of categorical and analogical reasoning and refutes logicians' exclusivity claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that qiyas al-shamul and qiyas al-tamthil are mutually concomitant and epistemologically equivalent, and critically assess the logicians' claim of exclusivity for categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Categorical and analogical reasoning are two sides of the same coin; certainty depends on evidence, not form.

Ibn Taymiyya asserts that every argument expressible as a categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shamul) can also be expressed as an analogical argument (qiyas al-tamthil), and vice versa. This mutual concomitance undermines the logicians' claim that only categorical reasoning yields certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of qiyas al-shamul
Take the example: 'Nabidh is intoxicating; every intoxicant is forbidden; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' This is a categorical syllogism with major premise, minor premise, and conclusion.
The middle term is 'intoxicant'.
2
Identify the structure of qiyas al-tamthil
The same argument can be phrased: 'Khamr is forbidden because it is intoxicating; nabidh is also intoxicating; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Here the root (asl) is khamr, the branch (far') is nabidh, and the common attribute is intoxicating.
The judgment is transferred from root to branch via the common attribute.
3
Map the terms between the two syllogisms
Ibn Taymiyya maps them explicitly: the major term in shumul (forbidden) is the judgment in tamthil; the middle term (intoxicating) is the common factor (jami') and locus (manat); the minor term (nabidh) is the branch.
Thus, the same logical content appears in both forms.
4
Trace the evidence for premises
The evidence proving the major premise in shumul (e.g., a hadith that every intoxicant is forbidden) also proves that the common attribute is the cause of the judgment in the root. Similarly, evidence for the minor premise (nabidh is intoxicating) proves the attribute exists in the branch.
Therefore, certainty or probability of the evidence applies equally to both syllogisms.
5
Assess the logicians' claim
Ibn Taymiyya states that the claim that certainty is exclusive to shumul is 'extremely corrupt' because the two are mutually concomitant. If the evidence is certain, both are certain; if probable, both are probable. Moreover, the judgment can be known by direct textual evidence (nass) without any syllogism.
This refutes the necessity and exclusivity of categorical syllogism for certainty.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : من المعلوم أن معرفة القلب بثبوت المحمول للموضوع، وهو ثبوت الصفة للموصوف، وهو ثبوت الحكم المسئول عنه مثل حرمة المسكر : قد تحصل بواسطة هذا الحد، وهو أن يعلم أن هذا خمر مع أن الخمر حرام، وهذا قياس الشمول». وقد يحصل بغير هذا مثل أن يرى أن المسكر مساو لخمر العنب في مناط التحريم، فيسوَّى بينهما في التحريم، وهذا قياس التمثيل» [٣٣٩] وقد يحصل بأن يرى فيه المفسدة التي في الخمر، فيحكم بالتحريم Commentary
This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin is a critical exposition of the relationship between two types of qiyas: qiyas al-shamul (syllogism of inclusion, i.e., categorical syllogism) and qiyas al-tamthil (syllogism of representation, i.e., analogical reasoning). The author refutes the claim of the logicians that only the categorical syllogism yields certainty, while analogy yields only probability. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that the heart's knowledge of a judgment (e.g., the prohibition of an intoxicant) can be obtained in three ways: via a middle term indicating the inclusion of the subject under a universal rule (this is qiyas al-shamul), via seeing that the intoxicant shares with wine the locus of prohibition (this is qiyas al-tamthil), or via seeing a specific corruptive attribute that is the cause of prohibition (this is qiyas al-ta'lil, which he also discusses). He notes that all these are species of the genus 'qiyas'. He explains the subtypes: showing the common factor (the cause) is ta'lil; indicating the cause is qiyas al-dalala, which combines tamthil and ta'lil; and eliminating the difference (ilgha' al-fariq) is tamthil but not ta'lil. He then asserts the mutual concomitance (talazum) between qiyas al-shamul and qiyas al-tamthil: every argument that can be presented in one form can be presented in the other. The middle term of the categorical syllogism is precisely the common factor (jami') between the root (asl) and branch (far') in analogy. He illustrates with the example of nabidh being forbidden by analogy to khamr. The same evidence that proves the major premise in the categorical syllogism also proves the causality of the attribute in the root. Thus certainty or probability belongs to both equally, depending on the evidence. Ibn Taymiyya condemns the logicians' claim that certainty is exclusive to categorical syllogism as extremely corrupt. He adds that the judgment may also be known by direct textual evidence (nass) without any syllogism. Furthermore, if it is known via categorical syllogism, it can also be known via analogy by setting the middle term as the common factor. In the analogy, the root is mentioned explicitly, while in the categorical syllogism it is not, but otherwise the logical structure is identical. This passage is a sophisticated argument demonstrating that analogical reasoning, when properly grounded, is epistemologically equivalent to categorical syllogism, and that the logicians' privileging of the latter is unfounded.

According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, what is the relationship between qiyas al-shamul and qiyas al-tamthil?

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Equivalence of Categorical and Analogical Syllogisms
Ibn Taymiyya argues for equivalence of categorical and analogical syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that categorical and analogical syllogisms are equivalent in evidential structure and refute the logicians' claim of categorical superiority.

Core Idea
Categorical and analogical syllogisms are identical in logical structure; only the certainty of the premises matters.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the standard Aristotelian hierarchy that elevates categorical syllogism above analogical reasoning. In this passage, he demonstrates that both forms rely on the same logical elements: a middle term (shared attribute) connecting the subject to the predicate. By analyzing the internal structure, he shows that any evidence used in one can be mapped onto the other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two syllogism types
Locate qiyas al-shumul (categorical syllogism) and qiyas al-tamthil (analogical syllogism) in the passage. Note that Ibn Taymiyya uses these terms to contrast the two forms.
In the passage, qiyas al-shumul is the categorical syllogism of the logicians, while qiyas al-tamthil is the analogical reasoning used in fiqh.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's examples
Write down the categorical form: 'Nabidh is intoxicating; every intoxicating is prohibited; therefore nabidh is prohibited.' Then write the analogical form: 'Nabidh has exhilarating intensity (the shared attribute); the root case (e.g., wine) is prohibited because of that attribute; therefore nabidh is prohibited.'
Both examples concern the prohibition of nabidh (date wine) based on intoxication.
3
Map the components
In the categorical syllogism, the middle term is 'intoxicating'. In the analogical syllogism, the shared attribute (wasf mushtarak) is 'exhilarating intensity'. Notice that the major premise in categorical (every intoxicating is prohibited) corresponds to establishing that the shared attribute is the cause (illa) of the ruling in the original case. The minor premise (nabidh is intoxicating) corresponds to establishing that the attribute exists in the branch.
Thus, the logical structure is isomorphic: the middle term in categorical is the shared attribute in analogical.
4
Analyze the link between certainty and form
Ibn Taymiyya argues that if the evidence for the premises is certain, the conclusion is certain in both syllogisms; if probable, it is probable in both. Therefore, the form does not determine certainty—only the premises do.
This directly refutes the logicians' claim that only categorical syllogisms yield certainty.
5
Critically evaluate the logicians' claim
Consider the basis of the logicians' claim: they argue that categorical syllogisms have a universal major premise, while analogical reasoning is particular. Why does Ibn Taymiyya call this claim 'extremely corrupt'? Because the universality of the major premise is itself established by evidence, which can be equally certain or probable as the evidence for the 'illa.
This step involves assessing whether the logicians' distinction is valid given the isomorphism Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه الرابع : تلازم قياس الشمول وقياس التمثيل. التحريم، أو لأنه مسكر والمسكر هو علة التحريم وبيَّن ذلك بدليله كان قياساً صحيحاً. وإذا قيل : النبيذ مسكر ، وكل مسكر حرام»، أو «النبيذ فيه الشدة المطربة، وما فيه الشدة المطربة فهو حرام ، فهذا أيضاً صحيح وكل ما أمكن أن يستدل به على صحة المقدمة الكبرى أمكن أن يستدل به على كون الوصف المشترك علة للحكم في الأصل . وكل ما أمكن أن يستدل به على الصغرى فإنه يستدل به على ثبوت الوصف في الفرع . ثم إن كان ذاك الدليل قطعياً فهو «قطعي» في القياسين، وإن كان ظنياً فهو «ظني» في القياسين. وأما دعوى من يدعي من المنطقيين وأتباعهم أن اليقين إنما يحصل بـ قياس الشمول دون قياس التمثيل فهو قول في غاية الفساد. وهو قول من لم يتصور حقيقة القياسين كما قد بسط في موضعه .
The fourth station - the fourth aspect: The mutual entailment of categorical syllogism and analogical syllogism. The prohibition, or because it is intoxicating and the intoxicating is the cause of prohibition, and he clarified that with its evidence, then it is a valid syllogism. And if it is said: 'Nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicating is prohibited', or 'Nabidh contains an exhilarating intensity, and whatever contains an exhilarating intensity is prohibited', then this is also valid. And everything that can be used as evidence for the correctness of the major premise can be used as evidence that the shared attribute is the cause of the ruling in the original case. And everything that can be used as evidence for the minor premise can be used as evidence for the existence of the attribute in the branch. Then if that evidence is certain, it is 'certain' in both syllogisms, and if it is probable, it is 'probable' in both syllogisms. As for the claim of those among the logicians and their followers that certainty is only obtained through categorical syllogism and not through analogical syllogism, it is an extremely corrupt statement. It is the statement of one who has not conceived the reality of the two syllogisms, as has been elaborated in its place.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a critical comparison between categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul) and analogical syllogism (qiyas al-tamthil). Ibn Taymiyya argues that they are equivalent in their evidential structure. He begins by giving examples: the statement 'Nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicating is prohibited' is a categorical syllogism, while 'Nabidh has an exhilarating intensity, and whatever has that is prohibited' is an analogical one. Both are valid if the premises are sound. He then establishes a parallelism: any evidence that proves the major premise in categorical (e.g., 'every intoxicating is prohibited') can prove that the shared attribute (intoxicating) is the cause ('illa) of the ruling in the original case. Similarly, evidence for the minor premise (e.g., 'nabidh is intoxicating') establishes that the attribute exists in the branch. Thus, the certainty or probability of the premises determines the certainty or probability of the conclusion in both types equally. Ibn Taymiyya then directly attacks the logicians' doctrine that certainty is exclusive to categorical syllogism, calling it extremely corrupt and accusing them of misunderstanding the true nature of both syllogisms.

Key Terms
قياس الشمول
قياس التمثيل
الحد الأوسط
الوصف المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the relationship between the certainty of a syllogism and its form (categorical vs analogical)?
A) Categorical syllogisms are always certain; analogical are always probable.
B) The certainty depends on the certainty of the premises, not the form.
C) Analogical syllogisms can never yield certainty.
D) Categorical syllogisms are more certain because they use a universal major premise.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demolishes the claim that categorical syllogism is epistemically superior to analogical reasoning; both stand or fall on the strength of their premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Compare the following two arguments: (a) 'Every intoxicant is prohibited. Beer is an intoxicant. Therefore beer is prohibited.' (b) 'Wine is prohibited because it is intoxicating. Beer is intoxicating. Therefore beer is prohibited.' Identify which is categorical and which is analogical, and show the structural equivalence by mapping the middle term/shared attribute.
?
Assess whether the certainty of the conclusion in (a) and (b) depends on the form or the premises. If the major premise 'every intoxicant is prohibited' is based on a revealed text (like Qur'an 5:90 which prohibits khamr, and the Prophet said 'every intoxicant is khamr'), then how do both syllogisms have the same epistemic status?

Knowing Rulings from Revelation Without Syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya shows rulings known from nass without syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a ruling can be known directly from a prophetic text (nass) without needing any syllogism, and identify when a single universal premise suffices.

Core Idea
A clear prophetic text renders the logicians' two-premise syllogism pointless.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya attacks the core claim of the logicians: that all certain knowledge requires a categorical syllogism with two premises. He uses a simple example from Islamic law: the prohibition of every intoxicant. He shows that this ruling comes directly from the Prophet ﷺ, making any syllogism superfluous. Moreover, even when applying the ruling to a specific intoxicant, a single premise (e.g., 'every intoxicant is wine') suffices, especially when based on the actual usage of the Companions. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being refuted
Ibn Taymiyya opens by stating that the required ruling can be known by a nass (text). This directly challenges the claim that all assent knowledge (tasdiq) requires logical syllogism.
The claim is that two premises are necessary; Ibn Taymiyya denies even the need for one.
2
Examine the example: intoxicants are prohibited
The hadith 'every intoxicant is prohibited' is a universal proposition from revelation. Ibn Taymiyya argues that if you have this from the Prophet, you do not need to construct a syllogism.
3
See how a single premise suffices
He considers two scenarios: (a) you know that wine is intoxicant and that intoxicant is prohibited, then you know each individual intoxicant is prohibited without syllogism; (b) you learn from Companions' usage that 'every intoxicant is wine' (a single premise), then you apply the ruling. No two-premise syllogism is needed.
4
Generalise the principle
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the logicians' requirement of a categorical syllogism with two premises is not correct, invalidating their broader claim. Revelation provides universal propositions directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يعلم الحكم المطلوب بنص على أن كل مسكر حرام، كما قد ثبت هذا الحديث في الصحاح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. وإذا كان كذلك لم يتعين قياس الشمول لإفادة الحكم، بل ولا قياس من الأقيسة، فإنه قد يُعلم بلا قياس . وإذا علم بقياس الشمول فكل ما يعلم بقياس الشمول فإنه يعلم بقياس التمثيل أيضاً، كما تقدم. ويجعل الحد الأوسط هو الجامع بين الأصل والفرع. والدليل الذي يقيمه صاحب قياس الشمول على صحة المقدمة الكبرى الكلية يقيمه صاحب قياس التمثيل على علية الوصف. وإن الجامع، وهو الوصف المشترك الذي الحد الأوسط في قياس الشمول»، هو مستلزم للحكم، وهو علية في الأصل، كما يقيمه في ذاك على أن الحد الأكبر لازم للحد الأوسط. فالحد الأكبر في قياس الشمول هو الحكم في قياس التمثيل، والحد الأوسط هو الجامع المشترك، ويسمى المناط»، والحد الأصغر (۱) هو الفرع. ويمتاز قياس التمثيل بأن فيه ذكر أصل يكون نظيراً للفرع الذي هو الحد الأصغر، وقياس الشمول ليس فيه هذا فصار في قياس التمثيل ما في قياس الشمول /٣٥٥ وزيادة . وقد بسط هذا في موضع آخر (۱) . وقد يكفيه وقد لا يحتاج إلى دليل آخر ذي مقدمتين ولا قياس ولا غيره، بل يكفيه مقدمة واحدة. وقد يستغني أيضاً عن تلك المقدمة بتصوّره التام ابتداء، مثل أن يكون نفس علمه بأن الخمر حرام قد تصور معه مسمى الخمر أنها المسكر، فصار علمه بجميع مفردات الخمر ،سواء، فيعلم أن هذا المسكر خمر حرام، وهذا المسكر خمر حرام، وأمثال ذلك . أو يعلم أن الخمر حرام ولا يعلم أن كل مسكر يسمى خمراً، بل يظن ذلك الاسم مختصاً ببعض المسكرات . فإذا علم بنص الشارع، أو باستعمال الصحابة الذين نزل فيهم القرآن، أو بأنها لما حُرّمت لم يكن عندهم من عصير الأعناب شيء وإنما كان الذي يسمونه خمراً هو المسكر من نبيذ التمر، أو بغير ذلك من الأدلة، إذا عَلم هذه المقدمة الواحدة، وهو كل مسكر خمر علم الحكم. فتبين أن قولهم : إن المطلوب لا بد فيه من القياس، وذلك القياس يجب أن يكون القياس المنطقي الشمولي، ولا بد فيه من مقدمتين ليس بصواب. وهذا يبطل قولهم: «لا علم تصديقي إلا بالقياس المنطقي ، كما تقدم (٢) . وأما هنا فالمقصود بيان قلة منفعته أو عدمها . وذلك أن هذا المطلوب إن كان معه قضية عُلمت من جهة الرسول تفيده العموم، وهو أن كل مسكر حرام حصل مدعاه . فالقضايا الكلية المتلقاة عن الرسل تفيد العلم في المطالب الإلهية . وأما ما يستفاد من علومهم فالقضايا الكلية فيه إما منتقضة، وإما أنها بمنزلة قياس التمثيل، وإما أنها لا تفيد العلم بالموجودات المعينة بل بالمقدرات الذهنية، كالحساب والهندسة فإنه وإن كان ذلك يتناول ما وجد على ذلك المقدار فدخول المعين فيه لا يعلم بالقياس، بل بالحس، فلم يكن القياس محصلاً للمقصود. أو تكون مما لا اختصاص لهم بها، بل يشترك فيها سائر الأمم بدون خُطور منطقهم بالبال، مع استواء قياس التمثيل وقياس الشمول .
And the required ruling may be known by a text stating that every intoxicating is prohibited, as this hadith is established in the sound collections from the Prophet (peace be upon him). If that is the case, categorical syllogism is not necessary for conveying the ruling, nor indeed any syllogism, for it may be known without a syllogism. And if it is known through categorical syllogism, then everything known through categorical syllogism is also known through analogical syllogism, as previously mentioned. And the middle term is made the common attribute between the root and the branch. And the evidence that the proponent of categorical syllogism establishes for the correctness of the universal major premise, the proponent of analogical syllogism establishes for the causality of the attribute. And the common attribute, which is the shared description that is the middle term in categorical syllogism, is entailing the ruling and is causal in the root, just as he establishes in that [categorical] that the major term is necessary for the middle term. So the major term in categorical syllogism is the ruling in analogical syllogism, and the middle term is the shared common attribute, and it is called the 'manat', and the minor term is the branch. And analogical syllogism is distinguished by mentioning a root that is similar to the branch, which is the minor term, and categorical syllogism does not have this. So in analogical syllogism there is what is in categorical syllogism plus an addition. And this has been elaborated elsewhere. And it may suffice him, and he may not need another proof with two premises nor a syllogism nor anything else, but a single premise suffices him. And he may also dispense with that premise by his complete conception initially, such as his very knowledge that wine is prohibited having conceived with it the meaning of wine that it is the intoxicant. So his knowledge becomes equal regarding all individual wines, so he knows that this intoxicant is prohibited wine, and that intoxicant is prohibited wine, and so on. Or he knows that wine is prohibited but does not know that every intoxicant is called wine, rather he thinks that name is specific to some intoxicants. Then if he knows by the text of the Lawgiver, or by the usage of the Companions among whom the Quran was revealed, or by the fact that when it was prohibited they had no grape juice but what they called wine was the intoxicant from date nabidh, or by other evidence, if he knows this single premise, that every intoxicant is wine, he knows the ruling. Thus it is shown that their statement that the required [knowledge] necessarily requires the syllogism, and that syllogism must be the logical categorical syllogism, and must have two premises, is not correct. And this invalidates their statement: 'There is no assent knowledge except through logical syllogism', as previously mentioned. And here the purpose is to show its little benefit or lack thereof. That is because this required [ruling] if there is with it a proposition received from the Messenger that conveys generality, namely that every intoxicant is prohibited, then his claim is achieved. For universal propositions received from the messengers convey knowledge in divine matters. As for what is derived from their sciences, the universal propositions therein are either contradictory, or they are like analogical syllogism, or they do not convey knowledge of specific existents but rather of mental estimations, like arithmetic and geometry, for even if that concerns what exists in that measure, the inclusion of the specific is not known by syllogism but by sense perception, so the syllogism did not achieve the purpose. Or they are matters in which they have no speciality, but other nations share without their logic coming to mind, with the equality of analogical and categorical syllogisms.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage continues Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim that all certain knowledge requires a categorical syllogism. He begins by showing that a ruling like 'every intoxicant is prohibited' can be known directly from prophetic text (hadith), without any syllogism. Even if one uses a syllogism, the analogical form is equivalent to the categorical, with the added benefit of explicitly mentioning a root case. He then illustrates how a single premise, such as knowing that 'every intoxicant is wine' (based on Companions' usage), suffices to apply the ruling to all intoxicants, without needing two premises. This demonstrates the logicians' requirement of two premises and a categorical syllogism is false. He then distinguishes between universal propositions from prophets, which yield certain knowledge in divine matters, and those from human sciences, which are either contradictory, merely analogical, or concern mental abstractions (like geometry) that do not give knowledge of concrete particulars. He concludes that the logicians' universal premises are either flawed or unnecessary, and that their logic does not provide unique benefit.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Categorical vs Analogical Syllogism: Ibn Taymiyya's Equivalence Thesis
Ibn Taymiyya compares categorical and analogical syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare categorical and analogical syllogism as presented by Ibn Taymiyya, and explain why he holds that analogical is superior.

Core Idea
Analogical syllogism includes everything categorical has, plus a concrete root case.

Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' categorical syllogism is not only unnecessary, but also inferior to the analogical syllogism used in fiqh. He shows that the two are structurally equivalent, with the middle term corresponding to the 'illa (effective cause) and the minor term to the branch. However, analogical syllogism explicitly mentions a root (asl) that serves as a concrete example, making it more informative. This lesson explores that comparison and the broader implication for universal premises from human sciences.

Guided Path
1
Map the terms between the two syllogism types
Identify the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) in categorical and see how it corresponds to the common attribute (al-jami') in analogical. Similarly, major term = ruling, minor term = branch. Write down the mapping.
2
Note the addition in analogical syllogism
Analogical explicitly mentions a root (asl) that is similar to the branch (far'). Categorical does not. This addition makes analogical more informative because you can see the reason for the ruling.
3
Examine the epistemic status of universal premises
Ibn Taymiyya says that universal propositions from the sciences (like geometry) are either contradictory or mental abstractions that don't give knowledge of concrete existents. Contrast this with universal propositions from revelation, which are certain.
4
Evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's conclusion
He concludes that the logicians' categorical syllogism is not beneficial because it can be replaced by analogical or by direct revelation. The logicians' universal premises are problematic.

قياس . وإذا علم بقياس الشمول فكل ما يعلم بقياس الشمول فإنه يعلم بقياس التمثيل أيضاً، كما تقدم. ويجعل الحد الأوسط هو الجامع بين الأصل والفرع. والدليل الذي يقيمه صاحب قياس الشمول على صحة المقدمة الكبرى إن المطلوب لا بد فيه من القياس، وذلك القياس يجب أن يكون القياس المنطقي الشمولي، ولا بد فيه من مقدمتين ليس بصواب. وهذا يبطل قولهم: «لا علم تصديقي إلا بالقياس المنطقي ، كما تقدم (٢) . وأما هنا فالمقصود الشمول .
And the required ruling may be known by a text stating that every intoxicating is prohibited, as this hadith is established in the sound collections from the Prophet (peace be upon him). If that is the case, categorical syllogism is not necessary for conveying the ruling, nor indeed any syllogism, for it may be known without a syllogism. And if it is known through categorical syllogism, then everything known through categorical syllogism is also known through analogical syllogism, as previously mentioned. And the middle term is made the common attribute between the root and the branch. And the evidence that the proponent of categorical syllogism establishes for the correctness of the universal major premise, the proponent of analogical syllogism establishes for the causality of the attribute. And the common attribute, which is the shared description that is the middle term in categorical syllogism, is entailing the ruling and is causal in the root, just as he establishes in that [categorical] that the major term is necessary for the middle term. So the major term in categorical syllogism is the ruling in analogical syllogism, and the middle term is the shared common attribute, and it is called the 'manat', and the minor term is the branch. And analogical syllogism is distinguished by mentioning a root that is similar to the branch, which is the minor term, and categorical syllogism does not have this. So in analogical syllogism there is what is in categorical syllogism plus an addition. And this has been elaborated elsewhere. And it may suffice him, and he may not need another proof with two premises nor a syllogism nor anything else, but a single premise suffices him. And he may also dispense with that premise by his complete conception initially, such as his very knowledge that wine is prohibited having conceived with it the meaning of wine that it is the intoxicant. So his knowledge becomes equal regarding all individual wines, so he knows that this intoxicant is prohibited wine, and that intoxicant is prohibited wine, and so on. Or he knows that wine is prohibited but does not know that every intoxicant is called wine, rather he thinks that name is specific to some intoxicants. Then if he knows by the text of the Lawgiver, or by the usage of the Companions among whom the Quran was revealed, or by the fact that when it was prohibited they had no grape juice but what they called wine was the intoxicant from date nabidh, or by other evidence, if he knows this single premise, that every intoxicant is wine, he knows the ruling. Thus it is shown that their statement that the required [knowledge] necessarily requires the syllogism, and that syllogism must be the logical categorical syllogism, and must have two premises, is not correct. And this invalidates their statement: 'There is no assent knowledge except through logical syllogism', as previously mentioned. And here the purpose is to show its little benefit or lack thereof. That is because this required [ruling] if there is with it a proposition received from the Messenger that conveys generality, namely that every intoxicant is prohibited, then his claim is achieved. For universal propositions received from the messengers convey knowledge in divine matters. As for what is derived from their sciences, the universal propositions therein are either contradictory, or they are like analogical syllogism, or they do not convey knowledge of specific existents but rather of mental estimations, like arithmetic and geometry, for even if that concerns what exists in that measure, the inclusion of the specific is not known by syllogism but by sense perception, so the syllogism did not achieve the purpose. Or they are matters in which they have no speciality, but other nations share without their logic coming to mind, with the equality of analogical and categorical syllogisms.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage continues Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim that all certain knowledge requires a categorical syllogism. He begins by showing that a ruling like 'every intoxicant is prohibited' can be known directly from prophetic text (hadith), without any syllogism. Even if one uses a syllogism, the analogical form is equivalent to the categorical, with the added benefit of explicitly mentioning a root case. He then illustrates how a single premise, such as knowing that 'every intoxicant is wine' (based on Companions' usage), suffices to apply the ruling to all intoxicants, without needing two premises. This demonstrates the logicians' requirement of two premises and a categorical syllogism is false. He then distinguishes between universal propositions from prophets, which yield certain knowledge in divine matters, and those from human sciences, which are either contradictory, merely analogical, or concern mental abstractions (like geometry) that do not give knowledge of concrete particulars. He concludes that the logicians' universal premises are either flawed or unnecessary, and that their logic does not provide unique benefit.

الحد الأصغر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what does analogical syllogism have that categorical syllogism lacks?
A) A middle term
B) A universal premise
C) An explicit root case (asl)
D) A conclusion

Key Takeaway
Analogical syllogism is not inferior to categorical; it contains everything categorical has plus a concrete root, and is the method used in Islamic fiqh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct both a categorical and an analogical syllogism for the ruling: 'The sale of gharar is prohibited.' Use the hadith of selling a bird in the sky as root. Compare the two forms.
?
Ibn Taymiyya says universal propositions from human sciences do not give knowledge of specific existents. Explain why a geometric theorem like 'all triangles have angles summing to 180 degrees' does not, by itself, tell you that a particular shape drawn on paper is a triangle. How does this support his critique?
